Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Abstract
This Master’s Thesis investigates Human-Robot Interaction (HRI), between humans and mobile
hospital robots, at the Danish public hospitals through User-Centered Design. A multi-layered
value perspective shows how the value created through the improvement of HRI propagates up-
wards to also create value through eﬃciency and innovation. To improve the HRI the humans,
as users, are investigated through a survey and interviews. The analyses lead to the creation
of two distinct personas representing opposing views on the HRI. This results in a selection of
user requirements and requirements for users. The user requirements serve as the foundation
of developing an improved robot that is more human-aware and social than the existing mobile
robots at the Danish hospitals. The improvement is created with the use of Artiﬁcial Intelligence
(AI). This includes the implementation of the A* algorithm using social cost maps and a deep
neural network to predict human behavior. The improvements are demonstrated through com-
puter simulations. The improvement of the robot through AI is evaluated on three benchmarks
capturing Social burden, Comfort and Eﬃciency along with subjective evaluations, all to encap-
sulate the user requirements. The social burden is improved by a 95.0% reduction in the number
of times human agents replan. The comfort is improved by an 83.1% reduction in the number of
times the robot is close to a human agent. The human agent eﬃciency is on average increased
by 15.5%, while the robot eﬃciency is decreased by 31.7%.

==PAGE:6==
Chapter 1
Introduction
The pressure on the Danish public Health Care system keeps growing. As it is often discussed
in the media, the demands are higher, and the resources are fewer. Additionally, the need for
solutions is urgent, but the requirements for the solutions are also high. One way to remedy the
pressure can be to exploit the fast technological development, where many diﬀerent technolog-
ical solutions are continuously presented. In this regard, it is natural to begin by looking into
the areas where human resources can be released by automated solutions. Speciﬁcally, one way
to do this is to implement physical robots, which are currently popular in the industry. They
can fulﬁll repeating tasks so that human resources can be used for other purposes. The physi-
cal robot investigated in this project are the Autonomous Mobile Robots (AMRs). In the Danish
Health Care system, the idea of using AMRs for repeated transportation tasks at the hospitals
arose in the 2010s. The idea was to remedy the personnel by letting autonomous mobile robots
do the transportation of for instance linens, blood samples and food.
However, when implementing robots in a hectic and demanding environment with many dif-
ferent kinds of humans, like the Danish hospitals, new challenges will naturally arise. These
challenges will often concern the humans because the environment is centered around humans.
Thus, the environment requires that the robots are human-aware and to some extent social. Some
of these challenges were experienced by the authors of this project in an empirical study in the
spring of 2020 (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020). Through this study, it became clear that many of
the challenges appear when humans are directly involved with the robots. One way to solve
the challenges lies in the intersection between studying the human and studying the robot. The
solution is to understand how the robots should be improved to ﬁt the exact environment of the
Danish hospitals.
The idea of solving the challenging interaction between human and robot is an area of interest
in the scientiﬁc literature. The ﬁeld of Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) is very broad and investi-
gates how the interaction can be solved from both a human perspective and a robot perspective.
Thus, the challenges seen at the Danish hospitals are well-known in other domains. This project
dives into the HRI at the Danish hospitals, where the AMRs autonomously drive at the hospital
hallways, resulting in some interesting interactions with humans to solve. The aim is, that the
challenges experienced today can be overcome by improving the mobile hospital robots based on
a deep knowledge of the hospital and, in particular, the humans within the hospital environment.
1
==PAGE:7==
Chapter 2
Scoping
This chapter outlines the scope of the project by ﬁrst narrowing the focus and afterward arguing
for an interdisciplinary approach to the project. This leads to the formulation of a research
question with several sub-questions presented at the end of the chapter to be answered through
this study.
2.1
Narrowing the focus of the project
The mobile hospital robots are social robots according to the deﬁnition by Duﬀy (2003), where a
social robot is simply a physical robot placed in a human environment, that aims at solving both
its own and others’ goals. The environment is in this project the Danish public hospitals. The
scope of the project is therefore the Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) at the Danish hospitals be-
tween humans and mobile robots. In order for the robot to be successfully implemented, multiple
surrounding aspects are naturally of importance, too. This includes buildings, IT services, WiFi,
the shape of cabinets mounted at the robot, explicit interactions and organizational structures
among many other things. Most of the surrounding aspects are neglected in this project, where
the actual HRI is in focus. This means that the focus is on the situation when human and robot
meet each other in a hallway and the interaction arises. Thus, the interactions of investigation
are implicit, meaning that the interaction occurs when the robot meets a human, but an interac-
tion is not the purpose of the robot (Kruse et al. 2013). The purpose is transportation of goods.
The environment of Danish public hospitals is interesting, seen from a human perspective due
to the uncertainty and unforeseen days and work tasks, and the few resources. Furthermore, the
mobile robots are a relatively new phenomenon at the Danish hospitals which creates room for
improvement. The suggestion for an improved robot in this project is based on Artiﬁcial Intel-
ligence (AI) and is made in a computer-simulated environment. This environment is built upon
the knowledge of the Danish hospitals. The aim is therefore to test the robot in this simulated
environment before implementing it in a physical robot in a real-life environment.
2.2
Arguments for an interdisciplinary project
In HRI, one can imagine that it is crucial to combine the perspectives of both the humans and the
robots in order to see the full picture and create a valuable interaction. This becomes even more
important in a busy environment like the Danish public hospitals where people of all kinds have
their every day among robots.
2
==PAGE:8==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
To elaborate, the interdisciplinary approach is inspired by the concept of Value Proposition (Os-
terwalder et al. 2014). Value Proposition is a part of the Business Model Canvas and the idea
behind the concept is to identify what pains and gains the customer experiences and how the
customer uses the product. These are then met through the design of the product. The value is
created if the ﬁt between customer and product is strong, meaning that all pains and gains are
met and the product makes the jobs easier for the customer. In this way, value for the customers
ends up creating value for the organization, as well.
In the context of HRI at the Danish hospitals, the Value Proposition concept can be used to un-
derstand how the improvement of the interaction can create value. If the robot is designed to
meet the needs of the humans and the human learns how to interact with the robot, the good
HRI will result in creation of value, as shown Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Value Proposition as framework for understanding HRI in the project.
This use of the Value Proposition allows an overall coherent understanding of how both per-
spectives are important for the value creation based on HRI throughout this project. This project
is therefore an interdisciplinary project that aims to cover both the human perspective and the
robot perspective.
The knowledge production in this project is based on the education and knowledge of the authors
of the project being engineers and coming from two diﬀerent engineering areas; the area of Busi-
ness Analytics focusing on management and the area of Artiﬁcial Intelligence. The knowledge
production is extended by including relevant theories and methods from other disciplines, as
well. Thus, a range of diﬀerent areas is used to understand the problem adequately. It is actively
chosen that the project includes methods and analyses from both management, social sciences
and user experience as well as the technical world of both symbolic and sub-symbolic Artiﬁcial
Intelligence. The project embraces the interdisciplinary way of achieving a goal that both the
human perspective and the robot perspective seek to achieve.
3
==PAGE:9==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
2.3
Research question
Based on the above scoping, the research question is determined to be as follows:
How can the challenges, that arise today in the interaction between humans and mobile robots at
Danish hospitals, be interpreted and improved from both a human perspective and by using Artiﬁ-
cial Intelligence to create value?
To answer the research question, the six sub-questions below will be explored:
• Which role do the mobile robots play at the Danish public hospitals as an organization?
• How do the existing mobile robots work and how can they be modeled and used as a
baseline in a simulation environment?
• Which user requirements arise in the interaction between humans and mobile robots at
Danish public hospitals and why?
• How can an in-depth understanding and analysis of the human in the HRI also improve
the interaction?
• How can the user requirements be used to develop an AI that elevates the robot’s under-
standing of and consideration for humans?
• How can the improvements be demonstrated through simulations?
4
==PAGE:10==
Chapter 3
Background
Before diving into how the research question from the previous chapter are answered, this chap-
ter seeks to establish the background knowledge of the project. This includes a presentation of
the preliminary work carried out by the authors prior to this project. Furthermore, it includes an
examination of the literature regarding Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) with focus on diﬀerent
perspectives. This chapter also presents the existing mobile robots and the future innovations
that are in pipeline for one of the robot developing companies.
3.1
Preliminary work
Prior to this project, a small preliminary project was completed in the spring of 2020 with the
purpose of investigating the sociotechnical challenges of mobile hospital robots and establishing
contacts at the Danish public hospitals. The project was carried out by Charlotte Friis Theisen
and Laura Marott, the authors of this project. The title of the project was "Clariﬁcation of so-
ciotechnical challenges concerning hospital robots". The results in the project were built upon
emails sent to every public hospital in Denmark in order to identify which hospitals are currently
using mobile robots, which are planning to do so, and which have decided not to. Furthermore,
four in-depth interviews were conducted with Sygehus Sønderjylland, Odense Universitetshos-
pital, Sjællands Universitetshospital and Bispebjerg Hospital, respectively. These hospitals were
selected since they were either currently using mobile robots or planned to do so in the future.
All interviews had a duration of approximately one hour and covered the purpose of robots, the
timeline of the robot project, pitfalls and beneﬁts of the robots, the reception of the robots among
the employees, and speciﬁc situations of problems experienced. This led to an analysis based on
statements from the four interviews regarding the purpose and vision, organizational challenges,
technical challenges, and the interaction with humans. The outcome of the analysis was a set of
sociotechnical challenges experienced at the visited hospitals. The results from the preliminary
work have led to several cases of challenging interactions being formulated at the beginning of
this project and presented in Chapter 5. Some of the ﬁndings from the preliminary work will
be used to support this thesis. The report is unpublished, but referred to by the citation: Friis
Theisen & Marott (2020).
3.2
Literature review
A literature review is carried out to investigate other researchers’ work in the ﬁeld of HRI. This
serves as both knowledge gaining and inspiration for this project.
In order to ﬁnd relevant scientiﬁc literature, databases such as Google Scholar, DTU FindIt and
5
==PAGE:11==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Scopus are used. First, searches are made based on keywords, and afterward, a snowball method
is used where terms and references from the chosen papers are followed up on. The key words
include:
• Human-Robot Interaction
• Social robots
• Social norms
• Attitude
• Agency
• Artiﬁcial Intelligence
• Planning
• Goal Recognition
• Theory of Mind
• Health care
• Mobile robots
The papers included in this review are chosen based on a combination of how relevant the title
and abstract seem and how many times the given paper is cited by others. This results in a total of
28 papers covering relevant work on HRI viewed from diﬀerent perspectives and disciplines. The
papers are not covering HRI exhaustively, but it is the aim to exploratively investigate the litera-
ture on HRI from as many diﬀerent disciplines and angles as possible. The papers are presented
below in a thematic literature review.
3.2.1
Human-Robot Interaction
HRI is still a relatively young area of research that by Dautenhahn (2007a) is said to have been
of general interest since the 1990s. It is interdisciplinary and concerns both the human element
and the robot element in the interaction. Since it is still a young area, no clear methodology for
investigating the interaction exists yet (Dautenhahn 2007b, Kruse et al. 2013). Today, this is still
evident in the high variation in the suggested solutions, as it will be presented in the following.
Dautenhahn (2007a) also argues that it would be damaging to the ﬁeld of HRI to have a dogmatic
attitude and deﬁne the correct way of study once and for all since the ﬁeld is complex and varies
from case to case.
Dautenhahn (2007b) emphasizes that it is crucial to not only consider the robots from the robot-
centered perspective but also include the human-centered perspective in order to understand
the user of the robots, being the humans in the interaction. In relation to this, Kruse et al. (2013)
state that it is important for future research to consider the human perspective and the robot
perspective at the same time when designing the algorithms for the robots.
Kruse et al. (2013) distinguish between explicit and implicit interaction. Explicit interaction is
if the purpose is to get a reaction from another agent in the interaction. Implicit interaction is
when the action is modiﬁed due to the presence of another agent, but no reaction is intended.
This is an important aspect to include when designing the robots. In terms of mobile hospital
robots, the interaction investigated is implicit, since the interaction is not intended but happens
if human and robot incidentally meet each other in the hallway. Explicit interactions occur at the
6
==PAGE:12==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
beginning and end of the robot’s route when humans need to decide the next job of the robot.
These explicit interactions are not further investigated in the project.
Even though HRI is said to be interdisciplinary and to include both human and robot perspec-
tives, it is found that the scientiﬁc literature tends to focus on either methods regarding the
human element or methods regarding the robot element with only a little focus on the other
element. It is rare that methods from both elements are combined in the studies. The following
sections present each perspective on HRI separately.
3.2.2
Human perspective on HRI
This section presents the diﬀerent aspects of the HRI that concern the interaction seen from a
human perspective. This includes what social robots are and how they are deﬁned as successful
in the literature. Furthermore, it is explored which approaches the human-centered literature
suggests to make the HRI successful.
Social robots
Before discussing the success of a social robot, it is relevant to understand what a social robot
is. Dautenhahn (2007b) distinguishes between ﬁve diﬀerent types of social robots, ranging from
socially evocative, as the most simple type, to socially interactive, as the most advanced type.
Duﬀy (2003), however, uses a simpler way of deﬁning a social robot. According to him, a social
robot is deﬁned as a physical entity placed in a social environment with the aim of fulﬁlling its
own goals and the common goals of the environment.
In this regard, a discussion arises in the literature about whether the robot is human-like and
social in terms of ontology or psychology. Kahn et al. (2007) discuss if the robot is actually
human or only perceived as human. The same is discussed by Duﬀy (2003), that distinguish
between AI recreating the human brain and thereby the social abilities, or AI simulating human
behavior and social abilities. Kahn et al. (2004) also emphasizes that the robot is not social but
acts social, meaning that it is not social ontologically speaking, but makes humans believe it is
social.
The successful social robot
Another discussion throughout the literature about human-centered HRI concerns how to ap-
praise the success of the social robots. Duﬀy (2003) asks if they should be perceived and judged
like humans or as their own race? Dautenhahn (2007a) states that it is important to not see
robots as humans and thereby not use human-human interaction as a benchmark for human-
robot interaction. Though, Duﬀy (2003) argues that anthropomorphic qualities might facilitate
the interaction since humans are more likely to interact with the robot if they recognize some
human traits. However, too much anthropomorphism will lead to humans not trusting the robot,
which is referred to as the phenomenon of "The Uncanny Valley" (Duﬀy 2003, Kruse et al. 2013).
Therefore, according to Duﬀy (2003), it is important to balance between peoples’ expectations of
the robot and the actual capability of the robot, especially when people expect the robots to be
similar to humans.
7
==PAGE:13==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Malle et al. (2015), Li et al. (2019), and Kahn et al. (2004) investigate the diﬀerence in how hu-
mans attribute morality to humans and robots. They have the same predeﬁned hypothesis; that
perceived morality is crucial for the success of the robot. Malle et al. (2015) state that society is
nothing without morality and social robots cannot avoid moral dilemmas when placed in society.
All three papers are built upon empirical studies that investigate the phenomenon of morality in
HRI. Malle et al. (2015) and Li et al. (2019) have sent out surveys with moral dilemmas to measure
peoples’ opinion on norms and morality when it comes to robots. The norms seem to have great
importance to how people perceive the behavior of the robot (Li et al. 2019). This can also be
referred to as the robot’s sociability (Kruse et al. 2013). Kahn et al. (2004) investigate peoples’
moral relationship to the robotic dog AIBO. They conclude that in many aspects humans per-
ceive a robot similar to a pet.
Hancock et al. (2011) focus on the partnership between human and robot and state that trust
is essential for such teamwork to succeed. These authors have made a literature review of 21 pa-
pers and found that the most inﬂuencing factor on the trust is the performance of the robot. This
correlates with the statement made by Duﬀy (2003) about balancing expectation and capability
to create success. If the humans expect something from the robot that it cannot do, the perfor-
mance will be perceived as poor and the trust is broken (Hancock et al. 2011). Trust is related
to what Kruse et al. (2013) call naturalness, which includes terms like predictable, understand-
able and readable. It is about the humans knowing what to expect from the performance of the
robot. A topic, that is not far from the trust, is comfort. Comfort is about how people perceive
the robot’s actions. It is, according to Kruse et al. (2013) about annoyance, stress, safety, and if
the robot is perceived as safe. In psychology, the term "Sense of agency" is about the human’s
feeling of being in control in the situation (Moore 2016). This is at the same time connected to
knowing what one’s actions will result in and how they will aﬀect others, which reduces stress
and strengthens the feeling of safety. Sense of agency can be related to both trust, naturalness
and comfort.
Kahn et al. (2007) state that not just one but several aspects should be considered when reviewing
the success of a social robot. These aspects are by Kahn et al. (2007) converted into psycholog-
ical benchmarks that can be used to directly measure the success of the robots. The idea arises
from computer science, where benchmarks are often used to measure the success of new work.
Kahn et al. (2007) wonder if this can be used when trying to build human-like robots. The idea of
benchmarking is also shown by Kruse et al. (2013) who, through their literature review, ﬁnd the
three aforementioned categories of sociability, naturalness and comfort that altogether deﬁne a
successful robot.
Approaches to making the interaction successful
Several approaches to ensure a successful interaction are suggested in the literature with the
common opinion that it is important to take the humans into consideration. Adams (2002) sug-
gests methods like User-Centered Design, where the product is built based on the user, or Human
Decision-Making, where the reasoning of humans should be investigated and incorporated in the
robot. She states that the purpose is to understand the humans as users and the tasks and goals
that must be achieved by the robot. The user is in focus and not the robot. Adams (2002) empha-
8
==PAGE:14==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
sizes the relevance of the designer having a good understanding of the user when developing a
robot that is a part of HRI, which includes understanding the needs and requirements of the user.
The relevance of user involvement is supported by Dautenhahn (2007a). According to her, it is
crucial for the development that the public is empowered by robots and actively participate in the
design phase instead of being surprised by what is coming out of the lab. The user involvement
is practiced by both Malle et al. (2015) and Li et al. (2019) through surveys and Kahn et al. (2004)
through chat room posts.
Dautenhahn (2007b) states that some domains require more social skills than others, for instance
in therapy, nursing homes or private homes. The degree of social skills can be found by analyz-
ing the domain and the robot’s contact with humans. The same is found by Li et al. (2019), who
also state that the norms are often unique across cultures because of history and sociopolitical
reasons. Thus, to design the robot to interact with humans, it is crucial to know the norms and
rules in the given culture and domain.
The solution, according to Dautenhahn (2007b), is that social intelligence should be the founda-
tion of the robot, where the social intelligent part is investigated and modeled before designing
the robot. In this way, the process is turned up-side-down compared with today, where the social
rules are often put on top of the designed robot (Dautenhahn 2007b).
Another way to create a successful interaction is through collaboration or teamwork between the
agents, being human or robot (Hancock et al. 2011, Seeber et al. 2019, 2020). Seeber et al. (2019)
deﬁnes collaboration as team members working together to reach a shared goal. The goal can be
anything, it could, for instance, be to ensure a good interaction. Seeber et al. (2019) argue that it is
necessary to switch from seeing the technology and Artiﬁcial Intelligence (AI) as a tool to seeing
it as a team member that helps to reach the goals. This is related to the idea of trust being crucial
for the collaboration, as Hancock et al. (2011) state. They, as mentioned, state that trust is heavily
dependent on the performance of the technology. If people tend to see the AI more as an agent
in the team, rather than a tool, it might be easier to understand that it has its own limitations.
People might then be more indulgent and helpful since all agents in the team work together to
achieve the shared goal, as Seeber et al. (2019) state. Seeber et al. (2020) have made a survey with
65 researchers in order to investigate important research questions within the domain of having
an AI team member. Based on their survey, Seeber et al. (2020) provide a research agenda built
on three design areas being machine artifacts, collaboration and institution. These design areas
deal with various design choices and challenges of AI in team collaboration. This has the aim
to help to gain an understanding of what needs to be addressed when collaborating with AI. At
the same time Seeber et al. (2020) work with dualities such as more anthropomorphism might
lead to more manipulation, or more responsibility to the robot might lead to loss of control for
the human. Such dualities are important to address if the AI should be seen as a team member
according to Seeber et al. (2020).
Both Compagna et al. (2016) and Shaw-Garlock (2011) suggest that robots can be seen as so-
cial actors. Shaw-Garlock (2011) argues that because of peoples’ tendency to anthropomorphize
robots and the intelligence of robots, the robots are more than just a thing. She claims that HRI
can be assumed to be equivalent with human-human interaction, which is in contrast with other
9
==PAGE:15==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
authors, for instance Dautenhahn (2007b) and Duﬀy (2003). This discussion is related to agency.
Burkitt (2016) uses Oxford English Dictionary (second edition) to deﬁne agency in its simple ev-
eryday form as an "action or intervention producing a particular eﬀect" and an agent as "a person
or thing that takes an active role or produces a speciﬁed eﬀect". Burkitt (2016) argues that this
deﬁnition is commonly used in social sciences, but more advanced deﬁnitions might be found
for social scientists focusing on the agency as its own topic. On a more complex level, Burkitt
(2016) himself deﬁnes relational agency, which means that the agents in a network are producing
an eﬀect on each other through their actions. With the support from the focus on a distributed
agency by Van Oost & Reed (2011), the main idea of the agency is then connected to which agent
acts in the interaction and who is the one to ﬁx it if it goes wrong no matter if the agent is human
or non-human.
3.2.3
Robot perspective on HRI
This section presents the speciﬁc design solutions seen from the robot-centered perspective on
HRI. This includes a presentation of diﬀerent concepts and algorithms that can be used to tech-
nically develop a social robot.
Handling humans
Many diﬀerent approaches exist to how social robots should handle humans. One prominent
feature for most papers is that the robot should be able to avoid collision with humans and other
obstacles, but the way of doing this diﬀers in literature (Kruse et al. 2013). Another goal is to
not only avoid humans but also understand and collaborate with humans (Rabinowitz et al. 2018,
Wang et al. 2020). Thus, it is ﬁrst interesting to look into how the literature handles the humans.
Sgorbissa & Zaccaria (2015) divide the possible obstacles in the environment into three cate-
gories: static, moving, and removable, where humans fall into the category of moving obstacles.
The robot can either stop and wait or drive around the humans when meeting them. Sgorbissa &
Zaccaria (2015) discuss the concept of humans being collaborative and ﬁnding interest in avoid-
ing the robot too. Stern et al. (2019) state that diﬀerent agents can have diﬀerent abilities. This is
called colored agents. Alternatively, anonymous agents are used, where they all have the same
abilities. Frank & Riek (2020) see humans as social obstacles and notes that it is important to
include an understanding of the social context. These authors have also earlier found that it is
feasible to track people and equipment with wearable sensors.
Dittadi & Bolander (2018) suggest goal recognition as a way of handling humans by observ-
ing what they do and calculate the probabilities of their goals based on prior probabilities and
past actions. In relation to this, Albrecht & Stone (2017) present a literature review about agents
modeling other agents. Here they ﬁnd many diﬀerent methods of modeling agents. They also
state that it is often assumed that the agent, the robot interacts with, is rational, meaning that
the agent executes optimal plans with respect to a known cost deﬁnition.
Other authors models other agents through Theory of Mind when robots have to interact with
humans (Rabinowitz et al. 2018, Wang et al. 2020). Rabinowitz et al. (2018) suggest an algorithm
10
==PAGE:16==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
based on a neural network called ToMnet (Theory of Mind network). The focus of their paper is
how an observing agent can learn autonomously to model other agents using limited data. The
ToMnet handles agents of diﬀerent species. This means agents that have diﬀerent movement
patterns and diﬀerent goals. Wang et al. (2020) are testing their ideas of how to model Theory
of Mind on the game Overcooked. They develop Bayesian Delegation that uses inverse planning
to infer the intentions of other agents. This allows for a better coordination and collaboration
between the agents.
Overall algorithmic approaches and formal problem deﬁnitions
Besides the handling of humans, the overall algorithm used in the robot is essential for the success
of the robot. To investigate this, Kruse et al. (2013) provide a literature review of human-aware
navigation. They ﬁnd that to create human-aware navigation, the software is often divided into
deliberate and reactive components. Deliberate components are planning the whole motion be-
fore executing, whereas reactive components only plan a small time ahead during the execution.
Architectures that combine these two are called hybrid or layered architectures (Kruse et al.
2013). Many other papers have a similar approach.
Karpas & Magazzeni (2020) state that the classical model for how an agent interacts with its en-
vironment is Sense-Think-Act. It is, according to Karpas & Magazzeni (2020), often eﬃcient to
split the planner into two components: executive and planner. This is to make the robot work
faster. The executive component is responsible for acting with the environment. The planner
plan or re-plan how to execute.
Sato et al. (2018) present a layered approach in their algorithm based on social norms. The paper
presents a new path-ﬁnding method where the mobile robot keeps to the right when meeting a
pedestrian in a corridor. This is done by a combination of a global cost map, a local cost map
and an attractive force map. The global cost map is based on a simultaneous localization and
mapping (SLAM) map of the whole environment. The local cost map is used to avoid obstacles
near the robot. The attractive force map makes the robot keep to the right. The local planner,
suggested by Sato et al. (2018), uses the Dynamic Window Approach. Fox et al. (1997) describe
the Dynamic Window Approach as a local plan that relies on the equations from the dynamics
of the robot. This is done by reducing the search space to those velocities reachable under the
dynamic constraints. Hereafter, the velocity maximizing the objective function is chosen. Fox
et al. (1997) at the same time state that collision avoidance must be divided into two categories:
global and local. The global part includes roadmaps and assumes that a complete model of the
robot’s environment is available. The complete trajectory from the start to the goal is computed
oﬄine. However, these global plans are not appropriate for fast obstacle avoidance, according
to Fox et al. (1997). Local plans, also called reactive plans by Fox et al. (1997), are used to model
small fractions of the world in order to control the robot.
Another version of a layered structure is shown by Garrett et al. (2018). Their algorithm, FFRob,
iteratively alternates between a sampling phase and a planning phase until it is able to ﬁnd a
solution. In the sampling phase, the problem is discretized and in the planning phase, the algo-
rithm performs a discrete search to decide whether a solution exists.
Many papers suggest an A* search algorithm for planning on a global level (Garrett et al. 2018,
11
==PAGE:17==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Sato et al. 2018). Kruse et al. (2013) explain that A* is a very commonly used search algorithm
for ﬁnding the shortest or most energy-eﬃcient path. Sometimes it is seen with modiﬁcations.
According to Kruse et al. (2013), in HRI, the goal is not necessarily to ﬁnd the shortest path, but
also for the path to be for instance suﬃciently safe, comfortable and natural to humans in the
area. This can be handled by adding cost functions, where the purpose is to add costs to the
robot’s actions depending on the context, environment and state in order to control the actions.
Sato et al. (2018) also work with the idea of cost in a map structure on both a global and local
level to make their social norm-based collision avoidance. As an alternative to state-space search
it is also very common to use Markov decision processes (MDP) or partially observable Markov
decision processes (POMDP) (Kruse et al. 2013, Rabinowitz et al. 2018, Karpas & Magazzeni 2020,
Frank & Riek 2020).
When making the algorithms the world is often seen as a 2D grid world (Kruse et al. 2013, Wang
et al. 2020, Fox et al. 1997). Kruse et al. (2013) have found in their literature review that most
of the human-aware path planning methods use graph-based methods with graphs representing
states in a 2D environment. The vertices are then the locations that the robot can be in without
collision, and the edges are where the robot can travel without causing a collision. Grid worlds
naturally lead to many simpliﬁcations. Kruse et al. (2013) state that most literature simpliﬁes
the situations by either assuming that only the robot moves or making the human movement
simple by for instance not changing speed, which is an eﬀect of using a grid world. According to
Kruse et al. (2013), more recent publications tend to assume more detailed and precise informa-
tion about human positions due to modern sensors. This provides some new possibilities when
predicting the behavior, which can be done by pose vectors or occupancy grids.
In relation to the grid representation of the world both Stern et al. (2019) and Albrecht & Stone
(2017) mention the consideration about the system being open or closed meaning whether new
agents can arrive. Stern et al. (2019) look into the conﬁguration of the agents like the size and
the speed. Furthermore, they also emphasize the existence of diﬀerent objective functions. They
suggest makespan that is about the number of time steps it takes for each agent to reach the goal
or sum of cost that is about the cost for each agent to reach the goal. They also state that other
objective functions might be more appropriate for a given problem.
According to Albrecht & Stone (2017), the formulation of plans is important, and these authors
points on STanford Research Institute Problem Solver (STRIPS) as a suited planning language.
This is supported by Karpas & Magazzeni (2020) that explain STRIPS as using Boolean proposi-
tions to describe the possible states, where a state is represented by the set of all propositions that
hold in that state. STRIPS is a grounded representations, using a ﬂat or propositional represen-
tation of the world (Karpas & Magazzeni 2020). Planning Domain Deﬁnition Language (PDDL)
is used to lift the planning problems to ﬁrst-order logic, which is much richer (Karpas & Mag-
azzeni 2020). PDDL can be represented as text strings, which STRIPS cannot. Grounded PDDL
and STRIPS are practically the same since PDDL is inspired by STRIPS.
Evaluation of social robots
Even though robot-centered literature is much about developing the algorithms, it is also essen-
tial for many authors to test and evaluate their work. There are two main evaluation strategies,
12
==PAGE:18==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
according to Kruse et al. (2013): empirically placing a robot in a human environment to create sit-
uations of interaction or running automated experiments in a simulator. Both requires assumed
measurements for what is good and bad performance (Kruse et al. 2013). They have found that
sociability, naturalness and comfort are important benchmarks for humans. When evaluating
the robots, it is diﬃcult to analyze the impact in regard to these because they are subjective and
context-dependent.
Both Fox et al. (1997) and Sgorbissa & Zaccaria (2015) are doing experiments in a human environ-
ment, described as real-world settings. Fox et al. (1997) are doing experiments in real corridors
with humans.
The algorithm presented by Frank & Riek (2020) is evaluated in a more speciﬁc setting. It is
evaluated in a real-world simulation of a crowded emergency department where the robot must
deliver medicine to a patient’s room. For each trial, Frank & Riek (2020) will record the executed
plan’s path length, duration, and “social burden” as estimated by a post-trial questionnaire. This
can be correlated to the idea of sociability, naturalness and comfort presented by Kruse et al.
(2013). Frank & Riek (2020) state that they will compare their algorithm to the FFRob-algorithm
that is socially naive. The idea of comparing algorithms is seen in other papers like when Wang
et al. (2020) compare the performance of Bayesian Delegation to other agent algorithms such as
Uniform Priors, Fixed Beliefs, Divide and Conquer and a Greedy approach.
3.2.4
Application of this project
Many suggestions on how to include and measure whether robots live up to what humans expect
from them are found in the human-centered literature. However, it is also seen how complex the
interaction is to understand. Robot-centered literature at the same time has many suggestions to
how the robots can become more socially aware and how they can move in corridors and avoid
humans.
This project aims to take the learnings from both perspectives and combine them. This includes
the learning of how crucial user involvement is and how much the success of the robot is depend-
ing on the given domain. It also includes the implementation of the idea of a layered structure
and an algorithm that ﬁts the domain. Furthermore, the ideas of how to model human agents are
taken into account. The overall application of this project is to provide a thorough investigation
and implementation of a social robot that is successful in the interaction with humans at the
Danish hospitals. This includes everything from understanding the user needs to implementing
and evaluating the algorithm for the robot built on these.
3.3
Autonomous mobile robots
In the preliminary work, it was found that many of the robots that are used at the Danish hospi-
tals are AMRs from the Danish company Mobile Industrial Robots (MiR) (Friis Theisen & Marott
2020). Therefore, an interview with their product manager is conducted in the beginning of this
project to understand the existing robots and explore their take on future innovations. The inter-
view is unstructured to allow it to take any interesting direction. It is conducted on the 12th of
October 2020 and can be found transcribed in Danish in Appendix A.1. Unless otherwise stated
13
==PAGE:19==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
all the following statements are based upon the interview.
The robots come in diﬀerent versions but they are all ﬂat and rectangular, only about 30-40
centimeters in height. It is possible to mount diﬀerent top modules, turning the robot into for
example a moving cabinet (Mobile Industrial Robots 2020). The robots are used to transport dif-
ferent goods such as linens, samples and food internally at the hospitals (Friis Theisen & Marott
2020). The robot has lidar-sensors that allow it to see 360 degrees at a height of 20 centimeters
above the ground. Furthermore, it has a 3D-camera in front that allows it to create a perception
of obstacles that are above and below the 20cm lidar-sensors. It has a few other technical in-
struments, that also help it construct a perception of its surroundings. In general, it is possible
for it to self-locate and detect obstacles. However, it cannot diﬀerentiate between any types of
obstacles or detect if the obstacles are moving or not. It only has a momentary image of the
surroundings.
The robot creates a global plan based on a static map of the environment. They only deviate from
the global plan if an obstacle appears. In this case, the robot calculates a local plan to get around
the obstacle. However, the algorithms to calculate the plans are rather simple and socially naive.
Since the robots are designed primarily for use in production companies (e.g. a storage facility),
it is a priority that they are in operation 24/7 and the employees are assumed to be able to adjust
to the needs of the robot.
Currently, AI is not used in planning by the robots, but MiR has several ideas for upcoming
AI projects. They currently have pilot projects regarding a new product; the AI-camera. This
camera uses machine learning for image classiﬁcation. The cameras are stationary and meant to
be placed in high traﬃc areas to support the robots by passing on information. This is information
regarding what types of objects are currently in the area. In the future, MiR plans to implement
the AI-camera in the robots, so that the robot has a better understanding of the obstacles it meets.
The product manager from MiR also mentions that in the future they want the robots to detect
directions of obstacles and be able to guess their next moves in order to adjust the robot’s own
movement.
14
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 4
Research Design
In this chapter, it is described how the project is carried out in order to answer the research ques-
tion. One overall framework is presented that creates the structure of the report and ensures the
interdisciplinarity. The interdisciplinary approach results in inclusion of many diﬀerent methods
and data. Therefore, all methods used throughout the project are only presented in this chapter to
guide the reader through the report, but the full description of the speciﬁc methods is presented
continuously when relevant. A data overview is also presented at the end of this chapter.
4.1
Chronological elements based on User-Centered Design
This project is structured based on the User-Centered Design process as framework, which for
instance Adams (2002) suggests as one way to include the users in the design of the product.
User-Centered Design provides a chronological way of investigating the users and improving
the product accordingly with an additional iterative feedback process (Curedale 2019, Interaction
Design Foundation 2020a). User-Centered Design is very case-dependent, thus the content of the
process can vary. The framework takes several elements into consideration, always with the user
in focus. Thus, the design of a solution is ensured to be interdisciplinary. User-Centered Design
is a part of User Experience (Interaction Design Foundation 2020b) and is illustrated in Figure
4.1.
Figure 4.1: User-Centered Design as chronological framework for the project.
The dashed arrows in Figure 4.1 pointing back from the last phase to the other three phases
indicate that the iterative process can take diﬀerent forms. The design process can re-enter any
of the ﬁrst three phases depending on the outcome of the ﬁnal evaluation. It should be noted
that this project only aims at completing a single iteration of the process and, based on the ﬁnal
evaluation, discuss what the next step should be. Since the content of each of the four phases is
15
==PAGE:21==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
case-dependent, each of the phases are brieﬂy described below, but, as mentioned, the methods
are elaborated when used throughout the report.
Furthermore, it should be noted that each chapter, corresponding to one of the phases from Figure
4.1, contains a section of reﬂections that discuss the methods, data and the outcome of the given
phase. This is done in order to discuss the content of the phases continuously instead of at the
end of the report. However, a discussion regarding the overall interdisciplinary approach and
the use of User-Centered Design is included at the end of the report to assess the project from a
meta-perspective.
4.1.1
Understand Context of Use
The Understand Context of Use in Figure 4.1 concerns an investigation of how the current context
surrounding the robot is. With a knowledge of both why the robots are chosen to be implemented
at the Danish hospitals and the current use of the robot, it is possible to understand what to be
aware of when designing the improved robot. Moreover, an understanding of the context lays
the foundation for the success of the robot, since it reveals how the implementation of the robot
seeks to create value.
This Understand Context of Use, therefore, contains the use of the following theories:
• Market pull and technology push (Osterwalder et al. 2014, Dixon 2001)
• Mobile robots as organizational change at the Danish hospitals (Friis Theisen & Marott
2020)
• Value creation (Osterwalder et al. 2014)
Furthermore, the Understand Context of Use includes observations, converted into use cases, of
how the robots function today in the situations where it interacts with humans.
4.1.2
Specify User Requirements
To Specify User Requirements from Figure 4.1, an analysis of HRI seen from the human perspec-
tive is conducted. This part of the project aims to understand the users on a deeper level and not
only from a business point of view. This contributes to improving the robots by deﬁning speciﬁc
user requirements based on the ﬁndings. Besides investigating what the users require from the
robot, it is also essential to understand how the users have to adjust to the robot in order to make
the interaction as smooth as possible. Thus, requirements for the users are also suggested.
This part of the research is based on survey data and interview data. The methods and theo-
ries that are used to Specify User Requirements include:
• Personas (Curedale 2019, Nielsen 2020, Dam & Siang 2020, UXplanet 2018)
• Descriptive data analysis of survey data
• Clustering (Herlau et al. 2016, Statistics How To 2018)
• Bricolage for interview analysis (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015)
– Categorization
16
==PAGE:22==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
– Discourse analysis (Haandbæk 2020)
– Theoretical reading based on Social Construction of Technology (Bijker 2013, Klein &
Kleinman 2002) and Actor-Network Theory (Law 1992, Randall et al. 2007, Miettinen
1999)
• Scripting (Akrich 1994, Akrich & Latour 1994)
4.1.3
Design Solutions
The part Design Solutions in Figure 4.1 primarily concerns the development of a robot built with
an AI that aims to meet the user requirements from the previous phase. More speciﬁcally this is
done in a simulation environment where the AI robot represents a possible future mobile hospital
robot that ﬁts the domain of the Danish public hospitals.
The theories and methods used for the design include:
• Layered approach to robot design
• 2D-grid world
• Multi-agent path planning
• First-order-logic formal domain deﬁnition (Ghallab et al. 1998)
• A* search algorithm (Russell & Norvig 2010)
– Social cost map
– Manhattan distance heuristics (Craw 2010)
• Neural network (Bishop 2009)
4.1.4
Evaluate Against Requirements
In the end, the improved robot is compared to a baseline robot developed based on observations
of the existing mobile robots at the Danish hospitals. Normally, User-Centered Design suggests
that the users are directly involved in the evaluation by experiencing the product in a real-life
setting (Curedale 2019). However, since it is out of scope to implement the code in a physical
robot, the evaluation is done in a simulator, but with the user requirements as benchmarks to
still ensure the users being considered.
The methods used for Evaluate Against Requirements include:
• Benchmarking and measurements (Kruse et al. 2013)
• Simulations (Ross 2013)
• Summary statistics
• Subjective evaluation
The results from Evaluate Against Requirements lays the foundation for a discussion of whether
it is relevant to repeat some of the previous phases for a more comprehensive solution.
17
==PAGE:23==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
4.2
Data overview
As the methods used in each phase of User-Centered Design are explained continuously, so is
the data collection. This is chosen since several diﬀerent types and sources of data is used in the
diﬀerent phases, thus it is more natural to describe it when used. However, this section provides
an overview of all data included in the research in order to answer the research question. The
data is triangulated since the research question is investigated through several diﬀerent data
types and data sources throughout the four phases of User-Centered Design. The data include:
• Background:
– Expert interview (see Section 3.3 and Appendix A.1)

– Survey (see Appendices B.1 and B.2)
– Semi-structured elaborating interviews with users (see Appendices B.6 and B.7)

– Data for training and testing of neural network
• Evaluate Against Requirements:

D.1)

tdh2xNJn8G1byvWGM488GIwkcU7kWD?usp=sharing)
