==PAGE:24==
Chapter 5
Understand Context of Use
The ﬁrst part of User-Centered Design is the Understand Context of Use of the product, which
in this case is the mobile hospital robots. Understanding what context the robots are used in
contributes to establishing user requirements that naturally will be context-dependent. This
correlates to what Dautenhahn (2007b) states on knowing the domain in order to know the so-
cial skills that is required of the robot.
Much of the knowledge behind this chapter originates from the work done in the spring of 2020
(Friis Theisen & Marott 2020). The preliminary work served as an investigation of the use of
the robots, current challenges, and an understanding of why the robots has been chosen to be
implemented at the Danish hospitals, which is all a part of the context.
This chapter begins by establishing an understanding of where the decision of implementing
mobile robots at Danish hospitals originates from and what the purpose of the robots is. This is
followed by a presentation of how the mobile robots are aﬀecting the organization of the Danish
hospitals based on learnings from the preliminary work. It is afterward investigated how value
is created in regard to the robots. At the end, the existing mobile robots are described to identify
issues in the interaction with humans through observations.
5.1
Technology push or market pull?
When assessing where the decision of implementing mobile robots at the Danish public hospitals
originates from, it may be useful to look into the market mechanisms behind the decision. The
terminology of technology push versus market pull can be used for this purpose. According to
Osterwalder et al. (2014) technology push is when the technology is developed before an actual
need for the product is seen on the market. Market pull is when the technology is developed
based on an already known demand. It is important to note that a technology will not necessar-
ily be stable through time but instead it will develop over time based on how it is used as well
as new technological advances. For example, an earlier version of the mobile hospital robots is
the Automated Guided Vehicle (AGV). The AGVs follow a ﬁxed route. Today, the technology has
advanced and allows for the Autonomous Mobile Robots (AMRs) to freely navigate the environ-
ment (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020).
It can be argued that the idea of mobile robots is a combination of technology push and market
pull. To elaborate on this, it is relevant to understand that the upper level of the decision hierar-
chy of the Danish Healthcare System is divided into two; the Danish Government (Sundheds- og
19
==PAGE:25==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Ældreministeriet 2019), and the Regions (Danske Regioner 2020). The Danish Government makes
decisions about overall topics such as the economy of the hospitals. Beneath the Government,
the ﬁve Regions in Denmark are the highest authority regarding the day-to-day operation of
the Danish hospitals. These diﬀerent decision-makers have diﬀerent goals and intentions when
making a decision on new technology. On a low level of the decision hierarchy, the decisions are
made internally at the hospitals, for instance regarding direct patient treatment.
At a governmental level, eﬃciency is the primary focus. This is evident in the requirement of a
2% annual eﬃciency improvement at each hospital (Ejbye-Ernst 2019) and what is called “Qual-
ity Fund Constructions” (Danish: Kvalitetsfondsbyggeri) which among other things are meant to
ensure a more eﬃcient operation at the Danish hospitals in the future (Sundheds- og Ældremi-
nisteriet 2019).
At the regional level, technology and innovation are in focus. An overall national strategy across
the ﬁve Regions is currently focusing on “The good life for all citizens of Denmark” (Danske Re-
gioner 2020). The strategy is broken down into diﬀerent development strategies for each of the
Regions with a focus on how each of them can meet the overall strategy. The development
strategy for Region Midtjylland (2019) states that: “Few monetary resources are given to the
healthcare. The solution is to invent new things, not to run faster”. At the same time Region
Syddanmark (2020) states that: “New technology and digital solutions are developing fast. This
creates opportunities and challenges”. Region Hovedstaden (2020) and Region Nordjylland (2020)
have similar statements in their strategies.
Based on the upper decision-makers’ diﬀerent points of view, the implementation of the mo-
bile robots at the Danish hospitals can be seen as a combination of technology push and market
pull (Dixon 2001). Looking at the robots from the governmental point of view, the robots serve
as a reactionary solution that has occurred based on the needs at the hospitals to provide more
eﬃciency and thereby save money and meet the eﬃciency requirements. The robot solution is
in that manner based on a market pull, which means that the hospitals and the government are
demanding the solutions to solve a current problem regarding eﬃciency. However, seen from the
Regions’ point of view, the innovation strategies open up room for a robot solution to be pushed
into the organization and create new opportunities. The robot solution is in this manner based
on technology push. This raises an interesting paradox on whether the robots should improve
eﬃciency or be innovative and thereby prepare the hospitals for the future, or both.
5.2
Mobile robots as a change of the organization
Overall, the Danish hospitals have the treatment of patients as the primary goal (Danske Re-
gioner 2020). This means that everything done at the hospital aim at curing the patients, as well
as keeping the treatment process a good experience for both patients and employees (Danske Re-
gioner 2020). Although the number of hospitals in Denmark is relatively high and the hospitals
diﬀer in size, location and medical expertise, this report assumes all Danish hospitals as similar
organizations in terms of available resources, types of tasks, types of people and organizational
structure.
20
==PAGE:26==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Because of the strength of both push and pull mechanisms in the decision of mobile hospital
robots, the implementation can be seen as unavoidable, and a change of the hospitals is therefore
happening based on the implementation. The AMRs were seen at the Danish public hospitals at
the beginning of the 2010s with the initial purpose of transporting blood samples (Sygehus Søn-
derjylland 2012). Thus, looking at the maturity of the change, the mobile hospital robots are
still in the young phase of implementation. This can also be seen in the relatively low number
of Danish hospitals that have implemented the robots (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020). It is also
evident since the manufacturers of the robots are still seeking knowledge in other areas such as
self-driving cars because that area is much more developed (Godske 2020). Thus, the implemen-
tation is not yet at its end which makes it possible to still aﬀect it. In the following, knowledge
and quotes from the interviewees in the preliminary work by Friis Theisen & Marott (2020) lays
the foundation for understanding the change that happens at the Danish hospitals when mobile
robots are introduced.
When using the robots, it is ﬁrst of all possible to replace the humans in some of the repeated
tasks, speciﬁcally the transportation of diﬀerent goods (such as linens or surgery tools), blood
and food. This is one of the main purposes of implementing the robots (Friis Theisen & Marott
2020). As found in the preliminary work, the use of robots in that manner leads to opportunities
for rethinking the processes at the hospital:
“
(...) the transportation robot that has been part of forcing everyone else to think com-
pletely diﬀerent.
Sjællands Universitetshospital, Friis Theisen & Marott (2020)”
When rethinking the processes, the goal is to release resources for other things, which was also
found in the preliminary work:
“
Let us free the warm hands, but let the warm hands do what they are best at, the cold
do what they can and get the automatic hands to support where they can.
Sjællands Universitetshospital, Friis Theisen & Marott (2020)”
Furthermore, with the use of robots, some tasks can be done outside normal working hours,
which gives more ﬂexibility. A big part of the change is therefore linked to who is doing the
transportation tasks, how they are done, and when they are done.
Another change can be seen in new decision roles arising based on the robots. Some employees
have to be responsible for the robots and some employees must work side by side with them.
The mobile robots often belong to either the Logistic Department or Facility Management, thus
the tasks and responsibilities of these departments are expanded (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020).
In the preliminary work, it has been found that the organization around the robot is crucial for
the success of the implementation.
All of the above implies that mobile robots have a huge impact on the people at the hospital. First
of all, both employees, patients and relatives will experience the robots in their environment and
21
==PAGE:27==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
will be forced to interact with them. Secondly, since the robots are taking over some of the
transportation tasks, many employees will have direct interaction with the robots during the
workday. The interaction is likely to aﬀect their attitude towards the robots, either positively
or negatively. At the same time, their presumptions might also determine the success of the
relationship between them and the robots:
“
We were very aware of the possibility of this [the implementation of robots] being a red
ﬂag especially from some groups of employees that thought they might lose their job.
We have experienced people sabotaging the robots. (...) For instance by often using the
emergency stop. (...) And obstacles were placed in front of them.
Sygehus Sønderjylland, Friis Theisen & Marott (2020)”
Since people, in general, are essential for the hospitals, both in terms of the work being centered
around patients, and the employees being such a strong asset for the organization, it is crucial
for the implementation that the relationship between the robot and the people at the hospital is
handled correctly. As found in the preliminary work, if the technology does not work, people
stop relying on it:
“
It always turns out that it is all the small things that make the project stop. If you hand
something over to the operation department that breaks down all the time, then they
will lose their motivation because they can’t do the job and then you don’t have support
for the project.
Bispebjerg Hospital, Friis Theisen & Marott (2020)”
All of the above highlights some of the ﬁndings from the preliminary work regarding how the
robots are changing the organization of the Danish hospitals. As it is seen in all quotes, the
change aﬀects the people in many diﬀerent ways. It can therefore be argued that people’s expe-
riences with the robot have a signiﬁcant impact on how successful the robot is perceived. Since
the change is still young, it is not yet established what is needed of the robots in order for the
hospitals to perceive them as successful and valuable. However, it is essential to establish this
knowledge.
5.3
Value creation of mobile robots at the Danish hospitals
The above analysis of the market mechanisms and the presentation of the organizational change
imply that the value creation regarding the introduction of mobile robots in Danish hospitals has
several layers as illustrated in Figure 5.1:
22
==PAGE:28==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 5.1: The three layers of value creation regarding mobile hospital robots at Danish hospitals.
At the top layer, a high-level decision is made where the paradox of eﬃciency or innovation is
found. Eﬃciency and innovation are not necessarily opposites but can be seen as two diﬀerent
perspectives on the value creation of the mobile hospital robots. The reason behind the imple-
mentation will aﬀect the deﬁnition of value. Neither eﬃciency nor innovation can be concretized
without looking at the lower layers of value creation. Thus, the value creation on this top layer
highly depends on how well the actual implementation is succeeding at the hospitals.
At the middle layer, the decisions made internally at the individual hospitals are found. The
hospitals have more speciﬁc requirements for what the robots must do and fulﬁll in order to be
valuable and thereby maybe elevate eﬃciency or be innovative. In the preliminary work, the
following was found:
“
We agreed that to implement new technology it must be an advantage on three areas:
productivity, quality and work environment.
Sygehus Sønderjylland, Friis Theisen & Marott (2020)”
This quote highlights that the requirements internally at the hospitals are multi-dimensional.
The improvement of productivity, quality and work environment might be dependent on the
individual hospitals and some hospitals might have other requirements, too. Furthermore, this
middle layer also includes everything surrounding the interaction such as the physical and orga-
nizational settings that is relevant for the implementation to be successful. To evaluate all this,
it requires an organizational analysis of the hospitals, which has been chosen to be beyond the
scope of this project, because this project primarily concerns the HRI happening in the bottom
layer.
This leads to the ﬁnal bottom layer of the value creation. Here, the focus is on the value cre-
ated directly between the user and the product. Osterwalder et al. (2014) state that the value is
always depending on how well the user and the product match each other. Seen in the context
of mobile robots at the Danish hospitals, the value is, thus, depending on a smooth interaction
between human and robot. This dependency is supported by the ﬁndings in the preliminary
work described above. Here it is evident that the humans are heavily aﬀected by the robots and
23
==PAGE:29==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
a well-working interaction is crucial.
Osterwalder et al. (2014) state that if the ﬁt between user and product creates value, it will be
valuable for the entire organization. This corresponds to the value propagating from a successful
interaction all the way up to the top layer. Thus, it is essential to investigate and improve the
interaction in order to create value at all layers of the organization. Improving the interaction is
the exact aim of this project.
5.4
Challenging interactions
Besides understanding the organizational context and the deﬁnition of value creation, it is im-
portant to understand the challenges of the robots at the Danish hospitals today. The existing
robot is a part of the context since its deﬁciencies are the motivation behind this project; if it
worked well today in the interaction with humans, there would be no need for improvement.
The existing robot is referred to as the Baseline robot since it will lay the foundation for eval-
uating the new robot. The Baseline robot is built based on observations made primarily in the
preliminary work (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020) and some during this project. In the prelimi-
nary work, two hospitals with mobile robots in operations were visited, Sygehus Sønderjylland
and Odense Universitetshospital. At these two hospitals, the robots were observed in action.
At Sygehus Sønderjylland, the aim was to see the drop-oﬀand pick-up of a cabinet with food.
At Odense Universitetshospital the aim was to observe the robot transporting blood samples
completing one of its routes. The observations were exploratory meaning that no predeﬁned
objectives were established. Thus, these observations were not structured. In this project it was
planned to complete structured observations at Sjællands Universitetshospital to support the un-
structured observations. However, the robot was out of operation on the day of visit and due to
Covid-19 it was not possible to establish a new appointment. Even though the robot was out of
operation it was still possible to observe its route and some of the challenges that could poten-
tially occur.
Based on the observations, the following cases are established as issues of the Baseline robot.
The cases are made from a “blue eye-perspective” meaning that the speciﬁc technology behind
is not described. It should be noted that the observations are made on three hospitals where
the settings, context, departments, traﬃc and robot types are diﬀerent. However, all the cases
contribute to the understanding of the issues of today’s robots. The ﬁrst ﬁve cases focus on the
robots and cases 6 and 7 focus on the humans. The cases concerning the robot have been vali-
dated by the product manager at MiR (see Section 3.3 and Appendix A.1).
24
==PAGE:30==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Case 1: Forbidden zones on the route
Place: Odense Universitetshospital
In some areas, a margin along the wall of the hallways is deﬁned as a
forbidden zone, where the robot cannot drive. This is to make sure that
there is always room for a hospital bed to pass by, thus the robot will
never disturb any urgent situation. Sometimes an obstacle is placed
in the hallway so that it is impossible for the robot to pass it without
entering the forbidden zone. In this situation, the robot will stop and
wait until the obstacle is removed. The robot exhibits this behavior no matter the context; it will
never try to drive around the obstacle even if the hallway is completely empty because it is not
allowed to enter the forbidden zone.
Case 2: Humans on the route
Place: Odense Universitetshospital
Once the robot is assigned a job, it will calculate a route to follow all
the way to the goal. This route is recalculated as soon as an obstacle
appears on the route. In some cases, the obstacle is a human. This
means that the robot will begin to diverge from the initial route based
on the calculations made after the occurrence of the obstacle – the
human. However, the direction and intention of the human are not
taken into account. Therefore, the recalculation and divergence from
the route are often not needed, for instance, if the human has moved when the robot arrives at
the given spot.
Case 3: Empty elevator required
Place: Sygehus Sønderjylland (Sønderborg)
The robot is driving in an area with both patients and personnel and
it uses the same elevators as they do. When the robot needs to use the
elevator, it requires to be alone inside the elevator because of the lack
of space. The robot speaks this in Danish and positions itself in front of
the elevator. However, the humans do often not listen and try to join
the robot in the elevator. This leads to a standstill until the human
leaves the elevator again and the robot can use it alone. The robot will
not leave the elevator and it requires a large distance between itself
and any obstacle before it continues. This situation has eventually led to the cessation of robots
in this particular area.
25
==PAGE:31==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Case 4: Elevator control take-over
Place: Odense Universitetshospital
The robot uses the same elevator as the personnel. The robot has ﬁrst
priority when entering an elevator. A human is allowed to join the
robot and the robot will not complain. However, the robot takes full
control over the elevator, which means that the elevator will go to the
ﬂoor requested by the robot. The human cannot aﬀect this by pushing
the buttons in the elevator. Therefore, the humans knowing this issue
might prefer to take the stairs if they are not going to the same ﬂoor
or even in the same direction as the robot.
Case 5: The robot has ﬁrst priority in the hallways
Place: Sygehus Sønderjylland (Aabenraa og Sønderborg)
When a human meets a robot and there is no space for them to pass
each other due to narrow hallways or obstacles, the robot will imme-
diately stop and try to calculate a new route around the human. This
stop might cause the hallway to be blocked. If it is not possible to ﬁnd
a route around the human, the robot will just wait until the human,
which is an obstacle from the robot’s point of view, moves. The robot
will not consider driving reversely to free the narrow space so that the
human can pass by. This means that the human must go back and wait
for the robot to pass, thus the robot ends up having priority in this situation.
Case 6: Emergency stop when robot is annoying
Place: Sjællands Universitetshospital (Køge)
If the employees at the hospital ﬁnd the robot annoying, for instance
if it drives too slow, stops in the wrong places, or is driving where the
person tries to walk, the employees tend to hit the emergency stop
button. After doing this, they can remove the robot by pushing it to
the side of the hallway. It will no longer annoy the employees. When
this happens, the robot will not start again without manual involve-
ment.
Case 7: Human-human communication in areas with limited space
Place: Sjællands Universitetshospital (Køge)
Some areas at the hospital have a limited space meaning that it can
be diﬃcult for two persons to pass each other if one of them is trans-
porting something. Diﬃculties are particularly present when turning
around a corner. In such situations, it is common that the humans
meeting each other communicate in order to ﬁgure out who should
pass and who should wait. The right choice depends on the situation.
The crucial part of this case is the communication between the hu-
mans because this communication ensures that the situation proceeds optimally for both parts.
26
==PAGE:32==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
With these observations, it is clear that the existing robot is not satisfying for the users. This
makes it essential to dive into the creation of an improved robot. The above cases primarily lays
the foundation for the Baseline robot in Chapters 7 and 8 but also provides insights on the is-
sues experienced today, which helps to understand the users and their concerns. The cases are
therefore valuable to keep in mind in the next chapter, which deals with an understanding of the
users in order to specify what is required for the improved robot.
5.5
Reﬂections on Understand Context of Use
Since this chapter functions as "setting the scene" built on learnings from the preliminary work,
only a few models have been included. This is due to the idea of exploiting the gained pre-
knowledge in order to be able to put more eﬀort into the subsequent chapters.
It has also been the aim to expand the knowledge from the preliminary work with observations
from Sjællands Universitetshospital. The idea was to collect more structured observations to sup-
port the unstructured observations from the preliminary work. But since the robot was out of
operation on the day of the visit, only unstructured observations of robots from the preliminary
work are available. This might not be as comprehensive as if structured observations were done
in this project with more clearly deﬁned objectives.
The description and understanding of the Baseline robot is purely based on observations val-
idated by the product manager at MiR. However, the detailed technical concepts and algorithms
are unknown. Therefore, the building of the Baseline robot presented later in the report does not
correspond 100% to the existing robots. However, this way of describing the robots also serves
as a way to introduce the reader to the current issues, thus it has several useful functions.
27
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 6
Specify User Requirements
With the understanding from the previous chapter of why the mobile robots are implemented at
the Danish public hospitals and how the robots are supposed to create value at diﬀerent layers,
it is now possible to begin the speciﬁcation of the user requirements for an improved robot. The
previous chapter showed that robots have an impact on everyone at the hospitals and that the
value on all layers depends on the interaction between humans and robots. Therefore, this chap-
ter seeks to provide in-depth knowledge of the humans as users of the robot in order to specify
their requirements. To specify the user requirements, it is essential to begin by understanding
who the users are and how they feel and think about the HRI today. This idea is inspired by the
current literature about the human-centered perspective on HRI as presented in Section 3.2 in
the Background chapter.
This chapter ﬁrst presents a method for understanding the users by creating personas (Nielsen
2020, Curedale 2019). This method is both the foundation and process for the execution of the
research of Specify User Requirements as well as the structure of the rest of the chapter. Through
this method, user groups of the mobile hospital robots are identiﬁed and analyzed, which aims
at to deﬁne some communicative personas. These personas are then used to specify the user
requirements. Besides making requirements for the robot, the users are also required to act in a
certain way based on the ﬁnal design of the robot (Akrich & Latour 1994). This is also included
at the end of this chapter.
Relevant data, methods and theories are described when used in the diﬀerent analyses to in-
tuitively follow the research process. It should be noted that the following analyses are not
concluding anything deﬁnitively, but they explore tendencies and trends in both data set and
user groups. Notice, that both humans and robots are referred to as agents in order to align the
language throughout the chapter.
6.1
Structuring through persona method
The users of most sociotechnical products are often diverse and cannot be described as only a
single group (Osterwalder et al. 2014). This is due to the complexity of sociotechnical products
leading to many diﬀerent wishes, needs, and ways of using the product. Several methods exist
to understand the users of a product and their diﬀerences. In User-Centered Design, creating
personas is one way to do this (Dam & Siang 2020). A persona can be described as a semi-ﬁctive
representation of a segment of the users with focus on needs and expectations but also with more
demographic and personal attributes attached (Curedale 2019). They are therefore not real users,
but they are synthesized directly from data on real users. Creating personas can help to un-
28
==PAGE:34==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
derstand and communicate the users’ needs, frustrations, behaviors and goals (Curedale 2019).
Several personas can serve as tools to highlight and simplify patterns since they are distinct
representations of diﬀerent types of real users. Thus, personas are useful when investigating a
complex case like HRI and in a complex environment like hospitals. They can be used to easier
specify what requirements the users have for the robot.
Personas can be created after enough knowledge of the real users is gained. According to Nielsen
(2020), the knowledge about the users can be gained from diﬀerent perspectives, thus the content
of personas can vary depending on the case. This project is primarily inspired by the engaging
perspective (Nielsen 2020). The personas are built on a broad and deep knowledge of the users,
with the aim for the designers, being the authors of this project, to be actively involved in the
understanding of the personas. The main advantage of the engaging perspective is that the in-
sights are aimed at not being too stereotypical because the designers use their experiences to
understand and communicate why the users act in speciﬁc ways in diﬀerent scenarios. Speciﬁ-
cally in this project, the users are investigated on both quantitative and qualitative data in order
to include as much background information that the ﬁnal personas can evoke empathy and be
relatable (Nielsen 2020). The persona method is interpretative, meaning that the ﬁndings must
be justiﬁed and discussed in order for others to understand. The aim is to provide intersubjective
ﬁndings.
Personas are originally known from both Business Intelligence and marketing (Curedale 2019),
but is widely used in User Experience, Design Thinking and User-Centered Design (UXplanet
2018). Since these areas are very broad the method is ﬂexible and case-dependent. Many diﬀerent
proposals to what steps to follow are seen in the literature. However, the method can be found
as divided into four overall stages in several sources (UXplanet 2018, Dam & Siang 2020), with
the possibility of dividing into more detailed stages if needed (Nielsen 2020). The very ﬁrst step
often includes data collection. However, the data used in this case is collected in two steps;
ﬁrst a survey, then elaborating interviews. The elaborating interviews are dependent on the
outcome of the survey, thus the data is not all collected at once in the beginning of the research.
The data collections are described when used within the persona method to reﬂect the actual
research process. The four overall stages are the following, named to suit this project based on a
combination of what is found in the diﬀerent sources.
1. Initial research
2. Analysis
3. Persona creation
4. Use of the personas
The rest of the chapter is built upon these four stages. In the initial research, the survey is pre-
sented in order to brieﬂy introduce the real users as one united population. The initial research
seeks to provide arguments for dividing the users into diﬀerent user groups. Hereafter, the anal-
ysis of the users begins. In the context of this project, the analysis is divided into three phases.
• Phase 1: A presentation of how the users are divided into groups that are distinctive to
some extent and thereby internally similar enough to reveal patterns. This phase is based
on quantitative survey data.
29
==PAGE:35==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
• Phase 2: An understanding of the user groups based on quantitative survey data in order
to get the ﬁrst impression of the patterns that characterize the diﬀerent user groups.
• Phase 3: An analysis based on qualitative interview data about the users in the diﬀerent
user groups in order to get to know them in-depth in regard to HRI.
After the analysis, the personas are created and described based on the in-depth knowledge of
the real users. The aim is, as Nielsen (2007) emphasizes, that these personas are relatable and
evoke empathy to a certain extent for the real users, but still are clear and easy to use to commu-
nicate the learned patterns. At the end, the personas are used to establish the user requirements
as well as the requirements for users.
To follow the above structure, the sections in this chapter are named after the four stages of
creating personas, including the three phases of the analysis.
6.2
Initial research
The initial research is built upon survey data as presented in the following sub-section. The
quantitative survey data lays the foundation for understanding all respondents, representing
users and potential users of the mobile hospital robots, as one united group. This is presented
in the sub-section after the data collection. The aim with the initial research is to provide an
argument for dividing the users into the user groups for further analysis.
6.2.1
Data collection: Survey
As the ﬁrst step of the data collection for Specify User Requirements, a survey is developed. The
overall purpose of the survey is to get input to the analysis of the humans in the HRI and thus
input to how the future robot should act in the cases where the existing robot does not act satis-
fyingly today. The survey consists of 20 questions in total. The content of the survey is inspired
by literature, where both Malle et al. (2015) and Li et al. (2019) develop surveys with scenarios
to understand peoples’ perception of norms and morality in regard to social robots. The survey
in this project primarily focuses on how the respondents want agents of diﬀerent kinds, either
human or robot, to act when the respondents meet them in diﬀerent situations in a hospital hall-
way. The survey, as it looked when distributed, can be found in Appendix B.1.
The target group is mainly people working at the hospitals since they are the ones who interact
most with the mobile robots in their daily life. However, potential patients and relatives are of
interest, too, because they also potentially have to interact with the robot. The survey, therefore,
makes it clear that even if the respondents do not work at a hospital and do not have knowledge
about mobile hospital robots, the answers are precious anyway.
After being tested on three independent persons, the survey is sent out about one month into
the project to get the data as soon as possible. The survey is distributed as a combination of
snowball sampling, where people known as working at hospitals, being either contacts from the
preliminary work or family of the authors, have redistributed the survey, and convenience sam-
pling on Facebook and the intranet of DTU (Aarhus Universitet 2020a,b). Thus, it is expected that
30
==PAGE:36==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
the respondents are a representative collection of patients, relatives and personnel at hospitals -
both clinical and administrative or logistic. The target number of responses is 150 responses. The
survey is closed after 14 days, and the ﬁnal number of responses ends at 180. The aggregated
answers to all questions can be found in Appendix B.2. It is immediately concluded, that this
population size is large enough to reveal user patterns.
6.2.2
User patterns for survey respondents
The ﬁrst part of the initial research is to look at the basic attributes of the survey population.
This includes the ﬁrst four questions from the survey. Below the basic information of the survey
population is shown as pie charts illustrating the distributions of aﬃliation to hospitals, age,
knowledge and opinion of mobile hospital robots.
Figure 6.1: The basic information of the survey population on aﬃliation to hospitals, age, knowledge
of robots, and opinion of robots.
As seen in Figure 6.1, many of the respondents are potential patients or relatives. Furthermore,
many of the respondents are between 21 and 30 years old. These two distributions might be
skewed due to the survey being distributed on Facebook, among other places, where the audi-
ence in this case is young people not working at a hospital. However, there is an appropriate
number of respondents in each group of aﬃliation and knowledge to give a useful impression of
the users. Looking at the opinion of robots, there is a notable majority of people being positive
towards robots and only a few being negative. This might result in the general opinion being
biased.
The initial research also includes looking into the more complex questions in the survey. The rest
of the survey contains questions on how the respondents prefer other agents, human or robot,
to act when meeting them in the hallways at the hospital in diﬀerent situations. In these ques-
tions, the respondents are introduced to a set of scenarios that are based on a range of diﬀerent
situations inspired by both observations and learnings from the preliminary work (Friis Theisen
31
==PAGE:37==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
& Marott 2020). These questions are referred to as scenario questions for easier mentioning. The
scenario questions begins at question 7 and include all questions up to and including question
18. The visual answer distribution for all scenario questions can be seen in Appendix B.3. The
strongest tendencies are presented in the following.
The most noticeable similarity between all respondents is a tendency showing that it is easy
for the respondents to decide what to do when clear social norms exist, as in situations that
are directly seen in other domains, like traﬃc. However, the questions, where these norms do
not exist, show more diverse answers. This indicates that people have diﬀerent ways of solving
such situations also depending on the type of opposite agent. This can be seen in Figure 6.2 that
assembles the answer distribution to questions 13, 14, 15, 16, 17 and 18. These questions are sim-
ilarly constructed where the respondent ﬁrst meets a human agent and afterward a robot agent
in a hallway where obstacles are placed in diﬀerent formations in order to replicate situations
known from other domains, like traﬃc (see ﬁgure text or Appendix B.1 for details).
Figure 6.2: The answer distribution of the following three scenarios asked for both human and robot
(questions 13, 14, 15, 16, 17, 18): Scenario 1: "Imagine that you are walking in a hospital hallway
and another person comes towards you. There is an obstacle, for instance, a closet, in your side of
the hallway, that you wish to walk around. There is only space for either you or the other person to
pass. How should the other person proceed?"
Scenario 2: "Imagine that you are walking in a hospital hallway and another person comes towards
you. There are obstacles, for instance, a closet, on both sides of the hallway. You and the other person
are equally close to the narrowing. There is only space for either you or the other person to pass.
How should the other person proceed?"
Scenario 3: "Imagine that you are walking in a hospital hallway and another person comes towards
you. There are obstacles, for instance, a closet, on both sides of the hallway. The other person is closer
to the narrowing than you are. There is only space for either you or the other person to pass. How
should the other person proceed?"
The aforementioned pattern, where the respondents ﬁnd it easier to decide on what to do when
clear norms exist, is evident because scenario 1 and 3 in Figure 6.2 replicates situations where
clear rules are applied in traﬃc. Scenario 2 is a situation that depends more on the communica-
tion between the involved agents.
32
==PAGE:38==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
The rest of the patterns found in the answers are more diverse indicating that the users are
diverse, too. One example of diversity is found in the question on how close the robot can come
to the respondents (question 7 in Appendix B.1). A histogram of the distribution is seen in Figure
6.3.
Figure 6.3: The answer distribution as a histogram for question 7: "If a mobile hospital robot have
to drive around you at a hospital hallway, how close can it come for you to still feel safe (inform in
meters)?"
The distribution of the distance shows that a great number of people prefer the robot to keep a
minimum distance between 0.5 meters and 1 meter. This corresponds to what is called "Personal
distance" by Kruse et al. (2013) that is often reserved for friends and colleagues. This indicates
that many respondents feel comfortable around the robot. According to Kruse et al. (2013), the
distance from 1.2 meters to 3.6 meters is called "Social distance" and is for acquaintances and
strangers. As seen in Figure 6.3, some respondents prefer the robot to keep a distance of more
than 1.5 meters, indicating that they feel uncomfortable around the robot. Already here, it is
evident that the users have diﬀerent preferences.
Some of the questions show that the respondents are directly disagreeing on some topics. This
concerns for instance the question of when it is acceptable to skip the queue and take the elevator
before the robot which can be seen in Figure 6.4 (question 10 in Appendix B.1).
33
==PAGE:39==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.4: The answer distribution for question 10: "Imagine that you want to use an elevator and
see that a mobile hospital robot is waiting for the elevator. In which case is it OK for you to jump in
line and take the elevator before the robot?"
The almost uniform distribution in Figure 6.4 is interesting because it reveals that people might
value themselves and their tasks at the hospital very diﬀerently in regard to the robot. This might
depend on their aﬃliation to the hospitals. However, the high amount of respondents answering
"Don’t know" indicates that it might have been diﬃcult to understand the question, or maybe
the question is not giving enough information such as what the robot is transporting, or why
the respondent wants to use the elevator. The same pattern about the diﬀerent weighting of the
agents’ tasks is seen Figure B.3 in Appendix B.3 (question 9 in Appendix B.1).
Diversity can also be found in the question where the respondents stand still in the hallway
and the robot is approaching them as seen in Figure 6.5 (question 8).
Figure 6.5: The answer distribution for question 8: "Imagine that you are standing still on a hospital
hallway (maybe you are looking for direction, looking at your phone, talking to another person) and
you see a mobile hospital robot driving towards you. How do you prefer the situation to proceed?"
34
==PAGE:40==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.5 indicates that half of the respondents want the robot to be able to react to the situation.
The other half want the robot to exhibit a simple behavior, either stop or continue to move in
the same direction, while the respondent moves around it. This is an interesting trade-oﬀabout
who should act, and thereby who should be the agent that solves the situation. This question
might reveal patterns on the respondents having diﬀerent perceptions of what the robot is able
to do and therefore should do. The same pattern of diﬀerent opinions on who should solve the
interaction is seen Figure B.5 in Appendix B.3 (questions 11 and 12).
Thus, when looking at the answer distributions from the survey resulting in the patterns pre-
sented above, it is indicated that in questions with no clear norms the respondents have answered
diﬀerently and have diverging perceptions of the situations and the robot. This suggests that
there is no collective opinion on how either human or robot should act and it depends on the
person answering the questions. The initial research has therefore provided an argument for not
seeing the respondents as one group, but instead, it is interesting to investigate the patterns for
the respondents divided into several groups that are internally similar to each other to gain a
better understanding of the diﬀerent users and their reasonings.
6.3
Analysis phase 1: Investigation of possible user groups
The next step of the persona method, as presented at the beginning of this chapter, is the actual
analysis of the users. The analysis is divided into the analysis of the quantitative survey data
(phase 1 and 2), and the analysis of qualitative interview data (phase 3). The qualitative data is
meant to enrich the quantitative data.
Phase 1 begins by presenting how the division of the users into groups takes place, followed by
the actual identiﬁcation of the diﬀerent user groups, all based on the quantitative survey data.
Afterward, the division into user groups are understood through the users’ answers to the survey
in phase 2. Methods and theories are presented continuously when used.
6.3.1
Clustering method for division of users
Clustering is a data mining method that can be used to divide data into groups that contain data
points similar to each other based on the distance between them (Herlau et al. 2016). In this
project, a cluster analysis is done on the survey data to identify user groups based on the respon-
dents of the survey. Other methods could have been used such as manually classiﬁcation based
on a small number of the questions from the survey. However, in this investigation, it makes
sense to consider several questions at once, since the users, and hence the personas, are much
more complex than what can be found in a few questions (Nielsen 2020). It is diﬃcult to do the
complex division manually, but simple to do with clustering that can consider many dimensions
of the data at the same time.
Since the survey data is rather simple, agglomerative hierarchical clustering is used, where each
observation starts by being in its own cluster and is merged with the most similar observation
or cluster (Herlau et al. 2016). This continues until all observations are merged into one cluster.
The decision on which clusters to merge is in this project based on the ward linkage method that
35
==PAGE:41==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
uses a weighted Euclidean distance to make sure to minimize the variance within each cluster,
to make them as internally similar as possible (Statistics How To 2018). The algorithm creates a
dendrogram that makes it possible to visually decide how many clusters to make based on the
length of the vertical lines that illustrates the distance to the next cluster. The visual decision
of how many clusters to make is one of the main reasons for using hierarchical clustering in
this context. It is not known in advance how many groups the respondents should be divided
into. Other clustering methods such as K-Means require the number of clusters as input to the
algorithm (Herlau et al. 2016), which makes such methods diﬃcult to use in this context without
other comprehensive investigations.
6.3.2
Identifying user groups through clustering
In the following, the clustering of respondents into diﬀerent user groups is made based on the
survey data. Before the clustering takes place, it is explained how the scenario questions are
handled in the clustering.
Obligation-to-stop-score
As seen in the initial research, the users seem to be diverse and have diﬀerent opinions on many
of the scenario questions. This makes it interesting to include the scenario questions in the di-
vision into user groups. However, the scenario questions include several questions from the
survey (questions 7 to 18), and if including them separately in the clustering it might become
challenging and non-intuitive to interpret the clustering. Therefore, one idea is to merge the
scenario questions such that the full picture of the respondents’ opinion is included instead. All
the scenario questions to some extent regard opinions on whether the robot agent is obligated to
stop compared to a human agent. A score is calculated that indicates what type of agent (being
human or robot) each respondent overall assign the obligation to stop in the interaction. This is
called the obligation-to-stop-score.
The obligation-to-stop-score is calculated by quantifying the answers to the scenario questions
from the respondents. This means that if a respondent has answered that the human should
stop or make room for the robot in a given scenario, the respondent scores -1 or -2 depending
on the answer possibilities. If the respondent has answered that the robot should stop or make
room for the human in a given scenario, the respondent scores 1 or 2 depending on the answer
possibilities. Some scenarios are asked for both human and robot as the opposite agent to the
respondent. In these questions, the score is made by comparing the answers for the human agent
and the robot agent. If the respondent wants both agents to act equal or if the respondent does
not know what to answer, the respondent will score 0. See the exact scoring possibilities for each
question in Appendix B.4.
After the assignment of scores for each answer to the scenario questions, the scores are summed
for each respondent in order to calculate the total obligation-to-stop-score. The smaller the score
is, the more the respondent prefers the human to stop in the interactions, and the higher the score
is, the more the respondent prefers the robot to stop in the interactions. The minimum score is
-8 and the maximum score is 10 which can be seen by summing the scores in Appendix B.4. The
distribution of all respondents’ obligation-to-stop-score is seen in Figure 6.6.
36
==PAGE:42==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.6: The distribution of respondents’ obligation-to-stop-score.
Figure 6.6 resembles a normal distribution, where most of the respondents prefer the robot to
stop more often and thereby exhibit a simpler behavior than the human, which is evident in
the average score at 2.32. It is interesting to see that no respondent wants the humans to always
stop, which would correspond to -8, but there are a few respondents that want the robot to almost
always stop, being the scores of 7, 8 and 9. These respondents might never rely on the robot’s
abilities to solve the interaction, but prefer it to be a passive and simple agent. The obligation-
to-stop-scores will be used when identifying the user groups as an input variable that uniﬁes the
scenario questions.
Clustering results
The clustering is made on the survey data and takes as input the answers to the aﬃliation, age,
knowledge, opinion and the obligation-to-stop-score of the respondents. This means that almost
all questions from the survey are considered in the clustering to ensure a comprehensive division.
As mentioned, all respondents to the survey are seen as real users, and therefore all respondents
are included in the clustering and found equally important.
Aﬃliation is categorical and is, therefore, made as one-hot encoding where the respondent is
assigned 1 if the aﬃliation is true (either patient/relative, administrative/logistic personnel or
clinical personnel) and otherwise 0. Age, knowledge, opinion and obligation-to-stop-score are
all ordinal on an integer scale depending on the number of answer options. The scale of the
obligation-to-stop-score also includes negative numbers to distinguish between preferring hu-
man or robot to stop.
By doing the hierarchical clustering, the dendrogram seen in Figure 6.7 is developed. The den-
drogram allows a visual exploration of how many clusters to create based on the length of the
vertical lines. Looking at Figure 6.7 it is seen that several possibilities exist. However, the cut is
decided to be made at 20 (marked in the ﬁgure), which results in three clusters and thereby three
user groups in total. This results in the division illustrated with three diﬀerent colors in Figure
6.7. The size of the clusters are shown in Table 6.1. One of the clusters is bigger than the others,
37
==PAGE:43==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
but all clusters have an appropriate size to be representative for the data set.
Figure 6.7: Dendrogram of the clustering of the respondents.
Cluster
Size
0
77
1
53
2
50
Table 6.1: Cluster sizes.
The three clusters represent three groups of respondents being the real users, and will from now
on be mentioned as the three user groups. Each of the user groups is assigned a color for easier
mentioning based on the clusters from Figure 6.7 and Table 6.1. These colors are also used in the
ﬁgures in the analysis:
• Cluster 0 is assigned blue and is from now on referred to as user group blue. The users
belonging to this group are referred to as blue users.
• Cluster 1 is assigned red and is from now on referred to as user group red. The users
belonging to this group are referred to as red users.
• Cluster 2 is assigned yellow and is from now on referred to as user group yellow. The
users belonging to this group are referred to as yellow users.
From the dendrogram, it is not possible to see how the variables decide the division into the given
user groups. Thus, to understand the user groups, each input variable from the clustering must
be investigated, which is done in the subsequent phase 2 of the analysis.
6.4
Analysis phase 2: Quantitative understanding of user groups
The quantitative understanding of the user groups, still based on the survey data, is investigated
visually and compared across the user groups in the following.
Looking into the aﬃliation in Figure 6.8 it is seen that most of the administrative personnel is
in user group blue, but all types of aﬃliations are represented in this user group. User group
red consists of an equal amount of patients or relatives and clinical personnel. And user group
yellow is mostly patients or relatives with some clinical personnel.
38
==PAGE:44==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.8: Distribution of aﬃliation to hospitals for each user groups. 0 is patient/relative, 1 is
administrative/logistic personnel, 2 is clinical personnel.
The age distributions in Figure 6.9 show that user group blue is middle-aged and older people,
user group red is young and middle-aged people, and user group yellow is young people. Note,
that the survey population contains a substantial share of young people as seen in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.9: Distribution of age for each user group. 0 = Less than 20, 1 = 21-30, 2 = 31-40, 3 = 41-50,
4 = 51-60, 5 = Over 61.
The patterns on how much knowledge people have of the robots are more indeﬁnite, as seen in
Figure 6.10. It is, however, seen that the majority of people with much knowledge are placed
in user group blue, though this user group also contains many people with no knowledge. User
group red and user group yellow are quite similar with people having either none or some knowl-
edge of the robots. It should be kept in mind that this is what the respondents themselves think
of their own knowledge of robots. This might therefore be a reﬂection of their perception of
themselves.
Figure 6.10: Distribution of knowledge of robots for each user group. 0 is none, 1 is some, 2 is much.
When investigating the opinion of robots, it is important to keep in mind that only 3.9% of the re-
39
==PAGE:45==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
spondents are negative and 22.8% are neutral as found in the pie charts of the survey population
(Figure 6.1). The rest describe themselves as positive towards robots. Thus, it is diﬃcult to say
much about the distributions of the opinion. However, when looking at Figure 6.11 it is interest-
ing that the distribution in user group red is less skewed towards positive than the distributions
in the other user groups. Furthermore, user group yellow has no negative respondents.
Figure 6.11: Distribution of opinion of robots for each user group. 0 is positive, 1 is neutral, 2 is
negative.
In Figure 6.12, the distributions of the obligation-to-stop-score are seen for each user group. User
group blue has an average score of 1.49 meaning that the respondents prefer the robot to stop in
more situations than the human, but not all situations. User group red has an average score of
5.13 meaning that the respondents prefer the robot to stop in many situations. And user group
yellow has an average of 0.64 meaning that the respondents want the robot and the human to
stop in the same amount of situations. It is notable that none of the user groups have a negative
average score, meaning that no user group wants the human to stop in most situations. Though,
some of the blue and yellow users actually have this opinion.
Figure 6.12: Distribution of obligation-to-stop-score for each user group. Negative is much prefer-
ring the human to stop, positive is much preferring the robot to stop.
The obligation-to-stop-score might be correlated with the users’ opinion towards robots since it
is seen that user group red have the smallest amount of users with a positive opinion, but the
biggest amount of users with a neutral or negative opinion, and at the same time have a higher
obligation-to-stop-score. Additionally, user group yellow has no users with a negative opinion
and a small number of users with a neutral opinion, and a lower obligation-to-stop-score. Thus,
this implies that the higher obligation-to-stop-score the users have, the less positive are they in
their opinion towards robots, and vice versa, which intuitively makes sense.
40
==PAGE:46==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Another way to look into the diﬀerences between the user groups based on the survey data is by
looking at the comments from the respondents given at the end of the survey. These are naturally
qualitative but belong to the quantitative data collection from the survey. All comments divided
into user groups are seen in Appendix B.5. The comments show some of the same patterns as
the obligation-to-stop-score. They imply that the red users are more skeptical towards the robot
and want it to stop for the humans. The yellow users, on the other hand, tend to see the robot
and personnel as equal. It is interesting how rules of traﬃc are mentioned a lot across all user
groups. These qualitative comments give another insight into the diﬀerences between the user
groups.
From the above several diﬀerences can be seen between the user groups even though some of
the distributions overlap. This quantitative understanding of the user groups gives an immedi-
ate picture of what characterizes users in the diﬀerent groups. The knowledge gained from the
above data is brieﬂy summarized in Figure 6.13. The next step is to enrich this gained knowledge
through the qualitative data of users from each user group. This is done in the next phase of the
analysis, phase 3 with references to the persona method.
Figure 6.13: Summarizing description of user groups based on the ﬁve cluster variables.
6.5
Analysis phase 3: Qualitative analysis of user groups
This phase of the analysis seeks to establish a deeper understanding of each of the user groups.
The aim is to gain knowledge on what requirements and expectations the users have for the
41
==PAGE:47==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
robot. The following analysis is qualitative meaning that the reasoning behind the answers of
the interviewees is crucial. It is essential to understand why people think like they do instead of
what they think.
Firstly, the data collection of elaborating interviews is presented. Afterward, the structure for
the qualitative analysis is presented, which the rest of the section will follow.
6.5.1
Data collection: Elaborating interviews with users
The deeper understanding of the user groups is built upon interviews with volunteering respon-
dents from the survey. The purpose of interviewing some of the respondents is to get to know the
reasoning and thoughts behind the answers since this cannot be seen from the multiple-choice
survey. The interviewees are found from the last survey question, where the respondents could
inform their email address to participate in an elaborating interview. 35 respondents have in-
formed their email addresses.
Nine elaborating interviews are conducted with three interviewees representing each user group.
Each interview has a duration of 20 to 30 minutes. The interviews are all built on the same in-
terview guide investigating some of the scenario questions from the survey. The interviews are
semi-structured since the same questions are asked for all interviewees but the elaborating ques-
tions might diﬀer depending on the answers from the interviewees (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015).
There are no expectations for the interviewees to remember their answers to the survey. The
interview guide can be found in Danish in Appendix B.6. The interviews are conducted over a
period of two weeks from the 26th of October 2020 until the 16th of November 2020 and they
are all transcribed and can be found in Appendix B.7 in Danish. The transcriptions omit moods,
pauses and speech errors, since it is assessed that this will not provide further insights. The in-
terviewees are anonymous.
The below table gives an overview of which interviewee belongs to which user groups along with
their individual description based on the attributes from phase 2 of the analysis. The interviewees
will be referred to according to this table in the further analysis.

The interviewees volunteering for user group blue have a slightly higher obligation-to-stop-score
