of the interviewees with the learnings from phase 2, it can be concluded that the interviewees
are suﬃciently representative for their respective user groups.
6.5.2
Structure of qualitative analysis
The qualitative analysis of the interviews is a bricolage containing three sub-analyses (Kvale &
Brinkmann 2015). The aim of every sub-analysis is to contribute with new knowledge but at
the same time support the already gained knowledge of each user group. The sub-analyses are
chosen based on how appropriate they are in regard to the type and number of interviews that
are included in this project. The results from the sub-analyses are interpretative and perspective-
dependent but have the aim of being intersubjective through the support of arguments and jus-
tiﬁcations. The quotes used from the interviews to obtain this are translated to English, but with
reference to the lines in the Danish transcriptions. The diﬀerent methods used in the bricolage
are presented below and described in detail when used.
• The diﬀerences and similarities between the user groups are ﬁrst explored, based on what is
explicitly said in the interviews. This is done by thematically categorizing the user groups’
opinions on diﬀerent aspects of the HRI (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015).
• To understand the user groups’ explicit mentioning of the robot, a discourse analysis is
done on the interviews, as well, where the nodal point is robot (Haandbæk 2020, Kvale
& Brinkmann 2015). With the explicit analyses from the thematic categorization and the
discourse analysis, it is possible to establish each user groups’ view on the internal hospital
hierarchy.
• In the end, the user groups’ preferences for the relationship with robots are investigated.
This is done by reading the interviews through a theoretical lens of both Social Construc-
tion of Technology (SCOT) and Actor-Network Theory (ANT) (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015).
This is not only based on what is explicitly said but also on an understanding of the inter-
views through the use of the two theories.
The results of the bricolage are presented in the following sections along with quotes to justify
the ﬁndings. Based on these analyses, the creation of the personas can take place, which leads
to the user requirements and the requirements for the user being speciﬁed.
6.5.3
Diﬀerences and similarities
Diﬀerences and similarities between the user groups are ﬁrst relevant to explore in order to get
a deeper knowledge of what deﬁnes the diﬀerent groups. These diﬀerences and similarities are
based on perceptions of, opinions on, and experiences with the mobile hospital robots which is
the core of the interviews. To analyze the diﬀerences and similarities the most prominent theme
for each user group in the interviews is identiﬁed through coding (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015). The
theme for each user group is found by reading the three interviews and categorizing every part of
the interviews into which topics are found in each of them. Note, that the topics are not decided
before reading the interviews but occur during the reading. The topic that is found the most
recurring across the three interviews within each user group is said to be the most prominent
43
==PAGE:49==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
theme for the given user group. Besides the three detected themes, a fourth theme is introduced
inspired by the literature (Duﬀy 2003, Kahn et al. 2004, 2007). The fourth theme concerns the
user groups’ opinion on whether human and robot are alike.
With the identiﬁcation of the themes, it is possible to compare all three user groups across the
four themes. The comparison is made by counting how many times each theme occurs in each
interview, either positively or negatively, which is then aggregated on each user group. A quote
can be counted in several themes. The result of this categorization is illustrated in a two-sided
horizontal bar chart that aims to quantify the use of the themes which can help illustrate both
diﬀerences and similarities. The ﬁndings are then supported by quotes from the interviews.
It is important to note, that the counting can dependent on the interpretation by the reader of the
interviews. Thus, the interpretation of the themes is explained below. The exact counts should
therefore not be considered as an exact number but more as guiding to explore the tendencies.
The results of the thematic categorization are presented after the description of the themes.
Description of themes
The four themes that are investigated in the interviews are:
• Rules and norms
• Comfort
• Respect for the function of the robot
• Human and robot are alike
The theme of Rules and norms includes situations where the interviewees act according to a cer-
tain rule or a norm. This theme is also of great interest in the scientiﬁc literature about social
robots (Kahn et al. 2004, Kruse et al. 2013). Rules and norms can be directly seen when an intervie-
wee uses a rule or norm as argumentation for the actions. This is then counted positively. When
Rules and norms is counted negatively it means that the interviewee deviates from a clear rule
and instead argues with other arguments than rules or norms. An example of negative counting
is seen in the quote below:
“
It [the robot] should be in the background. I would stop for the human out of courtesy
and I just don’t have those feelings for the robot.
P4, Appendix B.7.4, lines 94-95”
In this speciﬁc case, both the robot and P4 have an obstacle in their lane, but the robot is closer
to the narrowing. P4 earlier in the interview argued in the same situation to stop for a human
because of rules of traﬃc (which is counted positively). However, P4 does not ﬁnd it necessary
to stop for the robot and will therefore deviate from the rules. Therefore, this quote is counted
negatively.
The theme of Comfort is a concept that is also found in literature about HRI (Kruse et al. 2013,
Duﬀy 2003). Comfort covers the absence of annoyance and stress when interacting with the
robot, which means that the robot not only has to be safe but also feel safe to the human. Com-
fort might be related to the psychological term "Sense of agency" which is about people feeling in
44
==PAGE:50==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
control in the situations which creates a feeling of safety (Moore 2016). The interviewees might
ﬁnd it comforting to have a sense of agency in the interaction. When counted positively, the
interviewees are either directly or indirectly stating that they are not afraid or not bothered by
the robot. When counting negatively, the interviewees are bothered by or afraid of the robot.
The theme of Respect for the function of the robot is about whether the interviewees are respect-
ing what the robot is doing at the hospitals. When the interviewees state that they respect the
function of the robot either directly or indirectly, it is counted positively. If the interviewees
want the robot to act diﬀerently than fulﬁlling its tasks, it can be described as not having respect
for its function. An example of negative counting can be seen in the below quote:
“
There, the human must take precedence [over the robot], because there is a bigger value.
Then the robot must wait until there is free passage.
P6, Appendix B.7.6, line 112”
In this case, the robot has to wait for the human because it does not create as much value as the
human, according to P6. This is counted negatively because it indicates that P6 is not acknowl-
edging the importance of the robot delivering the goods as much as the humans moving in the
hallways.
The theme of Human and robot are alike is seen in literature as for instance anthropomorphism
of either appearance, feelings or capabilities, (Duﬀy 2003, Kahn et al. 2004) or psychological hu-
man benchmarking (Kahn et al. 2007). It is about whether the interviewees ascribe the human
and robot the same amount of agency (Burkitt 2016), importance, qualities, and abilities. This
includes how equally they should be treated, and to what extent they must act according to the
same rules. It can for instance be seen if the interviewees are directly comparing the robot to an
employee, or state that the robot might feel something. In these cases, it is counted positively.
When counted negatively, the interviewees are for instance talking about how the abilities such
as communication deviate from humans, or the interviewees are directly stating that the robot
is a thing and not a human.
Result of categorization
The aggregated counting of the four themes results in the ﬁgure below. The bars represent the
absolute counted number for each user group on each theme.
45
==PAGE:51==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.14: The aggregated positive and negative countings for user group on the diﬀerent themes
in the interviews.
It is visually clear in Figure 6.14 that the categorization shows immediate diﬀerences between
the user groups, but some of the themes also show a similarity in the counting. However, the
reasoning for the mentioning of each theme and thereby the counting cannot be seen from Figure
6.14. Thus, each theme is investigated across each user group in the following. Note, that the
interviewees will be mentioned as users from now on.
Rules and norms
The most prominent theme found for the blue user group is that the blue users typically argue
based on Rules and norms. Many of the rules they follow are connected to the rules found in
traﬃc like keeping to the right or stopping if there is an obstacle in their lane. However, norms
that occur internally at the hospital are also important for them, like how to act when meeting
a group of clinical employees. When arguing about rules and norms, the blue users apply terms
like “It is logical” or “It would be natural” (e.g. P3, Appendix B.7.3, lines 149-150). This indicates
that rules and norms are anchored in the blue users. Sometimes, the blue users argue for acting
against the rules, for instance, if they are uncertain of how the robot will act. However, even
when acting against the rules and norms, their arguments often contain an inner discussion on
this act of deviating from the rules:
46
==PAGE:52==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
“
(...) But here, I might be in doubt because I think it would be logical if it [the robot]
stopped because it has an obstacle in front of it. But I think I would stop because I can’t
see on a robot what it thinks. I mean, if it is a human you can see if they are busy or
if they think they can just pass by you. You have some kind of idea of the person you
meet and whether they hold back when you look at them. You can’t see that on a robot.
So I would stop and let it pass.
P1, blue user, Appendix B.7.1, lines 108-113”
This quote shows how P1 thinks of a rule from traﬃc where the other agent should stop because
it has an obstacle in front of it. P1 is fully aware of the rule but deviates from it because it is
diﬃcult to decide what the robot thinks. The awareness of deviating from a rule highlights the
importance of rules and norms for the blue users.
When comparing the theme of Rules and norms with the two other user groups, it is seen in
Figure 6.14 that the blue users are mentioning rules and norms only a little more than the users
in the other user groups do. Furthermore, the other user groups seem to be more in doubt and
argue an equal amount for and against acting according to rules and norms. It is interesting,
that the red users are the ones with the highest amount of not following rules and norms. When
investigating this tendency, it is found that the red users are arguing against the rules if they
are afraid of the robot hurting them or others (e.g. P6, Appendix B.7.6, lines 107-108). When the
yellow users deviate from the rules and norms it is mostly about them deferring to the personnel
at the hospitals. However, the rules that are referred the most to by both red and yellow users are
also the rules of traﬃc (e.g. P4, Appendix B.7.4, lines 49-51, and P7, Appendix B.7.7, lines 92-93).
Overall, it is interesting that rules and norms are mentioned much both positively and negatively
by all user groups. This indicates that it can be assumed that people, in general, are reﬂecting
upon rules and norms known from other areas such as traﬃc when deciding how to act in the
hospital domain. It is notable that the theme of Rules and norms have turned out to be occurring
almost the most for all user groups, even though other themes were initially identiﬁed for the
red users and the yellow users.
Comfort
The most prominent theme identiﬁed for user group red is Comfort, and the red users are espe-
cially focusing on the lack of comfort when interacting with the robot. This is seen in the amount
of the negatively counted Comfort in Figure 6.14. They state that the robot should be adjusted to
them so there will be no doubt on who is doing what (see quote below). This might explain their
high obligation-to-stop-score as seen in Figure 6.12, where red users tend to prefer the robot to
stop much more often than the human. This might be due to the red users trusting humans more
than technology. They appear to be more in doubt when interacting with robots. This can also
be explained by them feeling a lack of sense of agency when interacting with robots because they
ﬁnd it diﬃcult to predict, what the other agent will do. The red users argue that the robot cannot
know or see what people are about to do, and the red users will therefore often feel forced to
stop because of safety regards, even though they prefer the robot to stop (e.g. P6, Appendix B.7.6,
lines 71-73). Thus, the red users are not trusting the intelligence of the robot and by demanding
47
==PAGE:53==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
the robot to stop, they feel more important than the robot:
“
It is simply to create more certainty and not being in doubt about who does what and
why. It [the robot] stops, and it must have an understanding of me being more important
than it is.
P6, red user, Appendix B.7.6, lines 15-17”
This quote shows that P6 would be most comfortable if the robot just stops. This will both create
a feeling of certainty and a feeling of P6 being more important than the robot since P6 will have
ﬁrst priority. The red users state that they are often afraid of the robot or feel that it is unpleasant
for them if the robot is too near or they have to move because of the robot:
“
Yes, that is a good question. Either that [the robot drives around me] or it [the robot]
stops. It would be unpleasant if I had to move for it (...) If I move it is an implication of
the thought that it would have hit me if I didn’t move.
P4, red user, Appendix B.7.4, lines 19-23”
Again, it becomes clear that the robot is creating uncertainty and thereby discomfort. User group
red tends to not feel comfortable in the interaction and weigh the human higher than the robot
at all times. In their ideal world, limited interaction with the robot if any at all might be optimal.
When looking at user group blue’s opinion on Comfort in Figure 6.14 it is seen that the blue
users are undecided. They are counted almost equally positive and negative. When they feel
uncomfortable it is about them being worried about being hit by the robot. These situations are
often when communication is crucial for solving the interaction:
“
Because it [the human agent] creates other possibilities - another interaction, than I
believe, I could have with a robot. So the robot, with its limited abilities, must submit
to a precaution clause.
P3, blue user, Appendix B.7.3, lines 136-138”
When the blue users feel comfortable it is on the other hand because they trust that the robot
will stop before hitting them. These situations seem to be where clear rules like in traﬃc can be
found, which corresponds to what is found about user group blue relying on rules.
User group yellow is not mentioning Comfort much as seen in Figure 6.14. The few times they
do mention it, it is mostly about them being worried that people do not understand the robot and
therefore will experience annoyance or awkwardness (e.g. P8, Appendix B.7.8, lines 99-100).
To sum up, the theme of Comfort is found to be very diﬀerent for the three user groups which
indicates that the three user groups will have diﬀerent needs in terms of the behavior of the robot
when meeting it.
48
==PAGE:54==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Respect for the function of the robot
User group yellow is immediately characterized by the theme of Respect for the function of the
robot. The yellow users are willing to interact with the robot, but they are cautious to interfere
with the robot in case they are interrupting the ﬂow at the hospital. This might originate from
them often seeing the robot as an employee (e.g. P9, Appendix B.7.9, lines 27-28), which is rare
for the other user groups, especially the red users. The yellow users see themselves as mostly
relatives and state that they are thereby not contributing to the environment at the hospital:
“
Because I think they [the employee and the robot] both have a function. The only func-
tion I have as a relative is to visit someone. I am not contributing to the environment.
Do you see what I mean?
P7, yellow user, Appendix B.7.7, lines 100-101”
Furthermore, the yellow users are respecting the robot with the argument of the robot still being
new to many people:
“
Actually, I would stop and wait for it [the robot] to pass, based on it being unusual in
some way. Therefore, it makes sense that it has a purpose that it drives for. So I don’t
mind stepping aside.
P9, yellow user, Appendix B.7.9, lines 14-16”
This quote is interesting, because the other user groups, especially user group red, argue that
because the robot is new at hospitals and people are not used to it, the robot has to defer to the
humans so no one gets confused (P5, Appendix B.7.5, lines 189-191). The yellow users are here
thinking diﬀerently since they will stop for the robot instead. The only situations where the
yellow users want the robot to be careful and deviate from its function is if the opposite agent is
weak, ill or ﬁnds it diﬃcult to understand robots, like older people or children.
Looking at Figure 6.14, it is seen that user group blue is not talking much about the function
of the robot and when they do, they tend to respect it. When the blue users are respecting the
function of the robot it is mostly about the people that are waiting for the robot and its goods at
the end of its route. The blue users argue that the robot will never arrive at its destination if it is
not respected or if it has to stop frequently (P1, Appendix B.7.1, line 100). Thus, user group blue
is respecting the function of the robot because they see this as also respecting the employees at
the hospital.
User group red is mostly not respecting the function of the robot as seen in Figure 6.14. This can
be related to the aforementioned focus on comfort. The robot creates many uncomfortable situ-
ations for the red users, which leads to disrespect. Moreover, they see the robot as a tool and a
machine that is meant to support the employees but not have the same status or rights and there-
fore should be in the background at the hospitals (P4, Appendix B.7.4, lines 62-64). Furthermore,
the red users argue that since it has no feelings because the robot is not a human, people do not
have to act in courtesy to it by for instance waiting in speciﬁc situations (e.g. P4, Appendix B.7.4,
lines 94-95). Therefore, the function of the robot is not respected by user group red in the same
49
==PAGE:55==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
way as they respect the function of the employees and the patients. This might be due to the fact
that there are many employees in this user group as found in Section 6.4. The employees might
see their own tasks as more important than the robot’s and additionally not anthropomorphize
the robot but instead see it as a tool.
To sum up, the three user groups are quite diverse in their opinion on the function of the robot
which underlines the diﬀerences between them.
Human and robot are alike
When reading all interviews, a tendency of making a trade-oﬀor an inner discussion about
whether Human and robot are alike is often found. As seen in Figure 6.14, both user group blue
and user group red primarily think that human and robot are not alike, whereas user group yel-
low ﬁnds it diﬃcult to conclude. It is also seen in Figure 6.14, that this theme is occurring a lot
for all user groups.
The blue users have two primary arguments for the robot not being like a human; the robot can
be diﬃcult to understand, and it is not possible to communicate with a robot in the same way
as with a human. The ﬁrst argument leads to safety concerns. According to the blue users, the
robot has to stop for vulnerable people at the hospital, since it can potentially be a danger to
these people that might not understand or see it because they are focusing on other things:
“
I think the robot has to adjust because there are many diﬀerent people walking in a
hospital hallway with diﬀerent handicaps and ﬁelds of view and so on. So I would like
it - for safety’s sake - to stop within a given radius.
P2, blue user, Appendix B.7.2, lines 120-122”
The second argument also relates to safety but is much more than that. According to the blue
users, many situations at a hospital are complex and can only be solved by humans communi-
cating with each other for instance by interpreting body language or making eye contact. This
is not possible with a robot:
“
The human has ﬁrst priority because I can’t communicate or reconcile with a robot.
I know that they [the robots] are not that advanced. So the human should have ﬁrst
priority.
P3, blue user, Appendix B.7.3, lines 117-118”
This quote shows that because the robot is not as advanced in communication as humans are,
it should defer to humans. This indicates that one of the biggest diﬀerences between human
and robot is found in the human ability to communicate, according to user group blue. It also
indicates that since the human is superior, the agency should be ascribed more to the human in
order for the human to be the agent that solves the situations. It is interesting that, in general,
human-like communication skills are seen as the foundation for solving the interaction. This
implies that if the robot does not live up to this expectation of an ideal interaction, the blue users
tend to see it as unsuccessful, which can be related to both Duﬀy (2003) and Hancock et al. (2011)
50
==PAGE:56==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
emphasizing the importance of a balance between expectations and capabilities in the interac-
tion.
However, as it was also found earlier, user group blue sometimes tends to see the robot as a part
of the daily work at the hospitals. Thus, there are examples of them thinking that human and
robot are alike in terms of both having equally important tasks, which corresponds to them also
preferring that the human stops in some cases, cf. their obligation-to-stop-score.
When looking at Figure 6.14 it is seen that user group red are the user group that ﬁnds hu-
man and robot being the least alike. This is partly because of the communication issues that are
also found for user group blue. According to the red users, too, it is not possible to communicate
or reason with a robot like it is with a human. This correlates with them seeing the robot as a
tool and not anthropomorphize it in terms of both feelings and abilities:
“
I think in my world it is illegal to just pass by because the other agent was there ﬁrst.
But I don’t have to think about this when it is a robot.
P5, red user, Appendix B.7.5, lines 111-112”
This quote and its context can be interpreted like the robot is just a thing without feelings and
therefore P5 does not have to treat it like a human. This is in contrast to especially user group
yellow that assigns the robot human-like qualities. The yellow users are stating that they feel
bad for the robot, when it is interrupted (e.g. P9, Appendix B.7.9, line 100), which is the direct op-
posite of what the red users feel. This way of viewing the robot by the red users is also correlated
to the fact that they see the human-human interaction as the ideal throughout the interviews,
which the robot is unable to live up to. Therefore, it is perceived as less intelligent, hence a tool.
As mentioned, safety is very important to the red users. They do not feel safe around robots, but
they do so around humans. The red users think that because they cannot communicate with a
robot like they can with humans, it poses a danger to all people at the hospitals (P6, Appendix
B.7.6, lines 88-90). This is also connected to a lack of sense of agency because the missing com-
munication skills lead to the red users not feeling that they have control over the situations with
the robots. When the red users ﬁnd human and robot being alike it is often in situations where
it makes sense that both human and robot follows the same traﬃc rules without diﬀerentiation
(e.g. P5, Appendix B.7.5, lines 157-160).
Figure 6.14 shows that user group yellow is the user group with the largest amount of posi-
tive mentioning of Human and robot are alike. They are, however, still in doubt which is seen in
the almost equal amount of them mentioning that human and robot are not alike. They argue
that since the robot has a certain function at the hospital, it is almost like an employee:
“
It depends on who it is. But I think robots are as much employees as an employee is in
some ways. So I would respect that it also has a job.
P9, yellow user, Appendix B.7.9, lines 27-29”
This correlates with them respecting the function of the robot as their theme. They argue that
because of this, human and robot should follow the same rules. This also correlates with their
51
==PAGE:57==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
obligation-to-stop-score from Figure 6.12 that shows that the yellow users often also want the
humans to stop. This might indicate that the robot is assigned agency, and the yellow users
thereby are willing to enter into interactions with it where it has an active role, too. It is in that
way also easier for them to see the robot as equal. Furthermore, as mentioned, user group yellow
tends to assign the robot feelings by saying "poor robot" or feel pity for the robot, which indicates
that they directly anthropomorphize the robot, hence see human and robot alike.
The situations where human and robot are not alike, according to the yellow users, are related
to the type of the human agent. User group yellow ﬁnds both vulnerable patients and life-saving
doctors more important than the robot and in situations where the robot meets one of these, it
should stop. But since they also think there are situations where the humans should defer to the
robot, it indicates that compromises can be found in order to produce useful results for all agents
in the interactions. Furthermore, the yellow users to some extent also agree on the diﬃculty of
communicating with a robot compared to a human.
It is overall seen, that some of the same issues are found by all user groups when comparing
human and robot, but the user groups have very diﬀerent perceptions of whether human and
robot are alike.
Summing up
To conclude, clear diﬀerences are seen between the user groups especially in the themes of Com-
fort, Respect for the function of the robot, and the arguments behind Human and robot are alike.
However, it is also clear, that all user groups are highlighting some of the same aspects of the
interaction being challenging such as the communication. Furthermore, it is interesting that all
user groups are relying much on rules especially known from traﬃc. The tendencies found in
the descriptive analysis of the survey data are conﬁrmed and the ﬁrst understanding of why is
gained. An interesting notion is that the ﬁnal categorization has ended up showing that both the
theme of Rules and norms and Human and robot are alike are mentioned more by the red users
and the yellow users than the ﬁrst discovered prominent theme for each of these groups. This
underlines the value of comparing the user groups across all the identiﬁed themes because this
illustrates other patterns than ﬁrst assumed.
6.5.4
Discourses and hierarchy
Besides the above investigation of the diﬀerences and similarities, it is interesting to make a
discourse analysis to ﬁnd out what words the users are using in regards to robots. This might help
paint a more diverse picture within each user group. Furthermore, it is interesting to investigate
how each user group deﬁnes the internal hierarchy at the hospitals because this can also reveal
what the user groups think of the robot. The hierarchy can be built on the ﬁndings from the
above thematic categorization and the discourse analysis.
Discourses
The discourse analysis is made by ﬁnding all words and phrases around the nodal point chosen
to be robot in order to understand the mentioning of robots (Haandbæk 2020, Kvale & Brinkmann
2015). It is not only the words and phrases right next to the nodal point but also in the sentences
52
==PAGE:58==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
before and after. The words and phrases are translated into English and some are reformulated
when needed such that the context is not lost. Duplicated words and phrases are removed within
each interview because it is more interesting to look at the unique list of words when ﬁnding the
common discourse for each user group. If duplicated words and phrases are included, some of
them might take too much attention to see the variations. The ﬁgures below show the ﬁndings
from each interview. By interpreting these ﬁndings, a common discourse for each user group is
investigated and compared to the ﬁndings from the themes.
In Figure 6.15 the words used about the robot by user group blue is listed per user:
Figure 6.15: The words used around the nodal point "robot" by user group blue.
When looking across the blue users, it is seen that all of them are focusing on the missing abil-
ity to communicate with the robot, as it was also found in the thematic categorization. This
is evident in phrases like "Cannot see what robot thinks", "Cannot read a robot", "Robot cannot
communicate", "No eye contact with robot", and "Cannot reconcile with robot". It is also seen that
P2 is mentioning the robot as dangerous or unpredictable, which is seen in phrases like "Afraid",
"Attentive", and "Danger". P1 and P3 are, on the other hand, not afraid of the robot and ﬁnd it to
have a function that can sometimes be compared to an employee, seen in the phrases "Compare
robot with an employee" and "Respect for the function of the robot". The most consistent dis-
course for user group blue is therefore the robot being diﬃcult to communicate with since this is
seen for all blue users. The blue users recognize that the robot has limited abilities especially in
terms of communication. It seems like all three users are focusing on the consequences of these
missing abilities but overall they still respect the robot and its function at the hospital. Again
an unbalance between the need for human-like communication skills and the capabilities of the
53
==PAGE:59==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
robot is found. This was also found in the above thematic categorization. Overall, the common
discourse and the way user group blue perceives the robot can be described as an awareness of
the diﬀerences between human and robot in terms of abilities. This leads to the need for an-
other way to solve the interaction. The solution can be found in rules and norms as found in the
thematic categorization, which might be a reason why user group blue is relying that much on
those; to make it easier for all agents in the interaction.
Figure 6.16 shows the words used about the robot as the nodal point by user group red divided
into each user:
Figure 6.16: The words used around the nodal point "robot" by user group red.
Looking at Figure 6.16 the ﬁrst that catches one’s eyes is the negatively loaded words such as "Un-
comfortable", "Troublesome", "Worsened", "Chaos", "Threatening", "Afraid", and "Scared". This
indicates that the red users have a skeptical perception of robots since they feel that robots pose
a danger. This corresponds to the focus on comfort being the most prominent theme for user
group red. The next thing that can be found in Figure 6.16 is that all users are mentioning the
robot as either a tool, a thing, a machine or "just" a robot. In combination with phrases like "I do
not have to behave for the robot", "Not ascribing status to the robot", "Do not have to take care
of the robot", and "Robot cannot replace humans" it seems like user group red is not assigning
the robot as much importance as humans. This indicates, that they often prefer the robot to stop
54
==PAGE:60==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
and to not actively participate in solving the interaction. This supports what has been found in
the thematic categorization. This opinion seems to be common across the red users even though
P5 is not as skeptical as the two others. P5 mentions for instance "Equal status" which in the
interview context means that P5 is willing to enter equal interactions in some situations, but still
emphasizes that humans have to have ﬁrst priority in many situations (P5, Appendix B.7.5, lines
143-145). The overall discourse for user group red regarding the robot can be seen as skeptical
and assigning the human much more agency than the robot in order for the human to have con-
trol of the situations.
In Figure 6.17 the phrases used by user group yellow can be seen across the users:
Figure 6.17: The words used around the nodal point "robot" by user group yellow.
User group yellow seems to have some conﬂicting discourses. On one hand, all yellow users
are talking about the robot having no feelings and cannot be hurt. They mention the missing
abilities of the robot seen in phrases like "Robot cannot register", "Diﬃcult to understand robot",
"Easier for me", "Robot has no feelings", "I cannot hurt a robot". This implies a diﬀerence between
human and robot, according to the yellow users. On the other hand, the yellow users are stating
that they have respect for the function of the robot, and the robot should be highly valued and
ranked and be as similar to a human as possible. This can be seen in phrases like "Robot should be
highly valued", "Respect", "As similar to human as possible", "Higher ranked" and "Trust". These
two discourses are conﬂicting because the ﬁrst one is to some extent negative and the second
55
==PAGE:61==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
one is positive. This correlates with the aforementioned ﬁnding that user group yellow ﬁnds it
diﬃcult to conclude whether human and robot are alike. However, both discourses contribute
to the overall discourse; user group yellow seems to be aware of the missing abilities of the
robot, but are willing to adjust to the robot to make the most out of the situation when entering
the interactions. They overall agree on the robot aiming to make things as easy for humans as
possible but also state that the humans have to make things easy for the robot.
Hierarchy
The thematic categorization and the discourses imply that each user group operates with an
internal hierarchy at the hospitals among patients, relatives, robots, and personnel. These hier-
archies for each user group, based on the learnings so far, are seen in Figure 6.18. The hierarchies
might be aﬀected by the main type of people represented in each user group, meaning that an
employee and a patient or a relative might have diﬀerent views on the roles at a hospital.
Figure 6.18: The hierarchy internal at the hospital based on the thematic categorization analysis,
the argumentation, and the discourses of the user groups.
User group blue argues that the patients are important since these are the ones that the hospital
exists for. The personnel is important too, according to the blue users, since these are the ones
running the hospitals. The blue users cannot decide which of these, if any, is the most impor-
tant. The robots are below these in the hierarchy because they can sometimes be compared to an
employee. The blue users argue, that if you are a relative, you would often be in the background
(P1, Appendix B.7.1, lines 129-130), which is the reason for the relatives to be in the bottom of
the hierarchy.
User group red places the robots in the bottom of the hierarchy because robots are annoying and
are a danger for the people, thus they should be in the background. Note that the placement of
the robot in the hierarchy might be odd for the red users because they tend to see it as a tool
and not an agent. The patients are in the top since every employee has sworn to be there for
the patients at a hospital. Personnel is, according to the red users, just below the patients in
the hierarchy. They are in particular placed high because the red users value the safety of the
personnel very highly (P6, Appendix B.7.6, lines 107-108).
User group yellow places the personnel at the top of the hierarchy, since these are the ones with
the most important and life-saving tasks, according to the yellow users. Robots and patients
are weighted almost equally. The robots are seen as logistic employees by the yellow users (P8,
Appendix B.7.8, lines 126-127), meaning employees with less life-saving tasks, but still tasks that
56
==PAGE:62==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
contribute to the hospital ﬂow. The patients are important to the yellow users since these are
often vulnerable and need extra attention. The relatives are placed in the bottom since these
do not contribute to the hospital environment, according to the yellow users. The yellow users,
however, indicate that the hierarchy is not necessarily locked and compromises can be done if it
beneﬁts all agents.
Summing up
In conclusion, the discourses support the ﬁndings from the thematic categorization. However,
the discourse analysis not only shows diﬀerences between user groups but also within the user
groups. It has become clear, that not all users in one user group are agreeing on the exact same
perspectives of the robot. This creates a diﬀerentiated and more in-depth view of each user group.
However, it can be concluded that even though conﬂicting discourses are found within the user
groups, it is possible to create one common discourse for robot for each user group. All of the
analyses of the explicit statements from the interviews so far contribute to the understanding of
each user group’s opinion on the hierarchy. Once again, the diﬀerences between the user groups
have been highlighted.
6.5.5
Relationship between human and robot
With the understanding of the diﬀerent user groups based on what is explicitly said in the in-
terviews, it is now interesting to read the empirical ﬁndings through a theoretical lens (Kvale
& Brinkmann 2015). The interviews are read with chosen theories in mind to try to understand
what each user group thinks of and prefer from the relationship with robots even though it is
not explicitly said in the interviews. The aim is that this will support the above analyses to make
the personas easier to establish. The theories used are Social Construction of Technology (Bijker
2013) and Actor-Network Theory (Law 1992). These two theories are known from social science,
and are both broad, and include many aspects around the relationship. To keep it simple, the use
of each theory is interpreted and narrowed, such that the focus will be on what is useful for this
exact analysis. The use of the theories in this analysis is highly related to how the users prefer
the relationship to be, even though the theories also regards the perception of the relationship.
This distinction is made, since what is perceived is not always equal to what is preferred. The
two theories are brieﬂy described below along with the interpretations used in the analysis.
One-way relationship using Social Construction of Technology
Social Construction of Technology (SCOT) is a sociological theory arising from the concept of
constructivism (Bijker 2013). Thus, the main idea of SCOT is that technology is socially con-
structed (Bijker 2013). This means that society decides the technological development, but the
technological development has no opposite impact on society. When a new technology is intro-
duced to society, it starts by having an undetermined future and development (Klein & Kleinman
2002). Here, SCOT works with a set of relevant social groups. These social groups interpret
the technology by their internally shared use of it. The "good solution" is therefore ambiguous
because it might not ﬁt all social groups’ interpretation of the technology. The strongest social
group ends up deﬁning the use of the technology based on their interpretation. This process is
called the stabilization of the technology (Klein & Kleinman 2002). When a stabilization of the
57
==PAGE:63==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
technology is ﬁnalized, the outcome is called the closure of the technology. However, if another
social group becomes the strongest later on, a new stabilization can take place to implement this
group’s interpretation as a closure. Technology is therefore not stable over time but depends on
how the users use it. The strength of SCOT is found in the focus on humans and their role in
technological development which can help understand why the given closure occurs. However,
the biggest pitfall is that SCOT does not consider if the technology reversely aﬀects society.
The use of the ideas from SCOT is in this analysis primarily to search for evidence on whether
the user groups prefer the relationship with the mobile hospital robots to be a one-way rela-
tionship. This includes if they like the robots to be subject to humans and their interpretations,
and the robots have to have a minimal or no reverse inﬂuence on the humans. Looking at the
case of mobile hospital robots at the Danish hospitals, the idea of diﬀerent social groups can be
translated into the user groups. In this way, SCOT can also be used to understand how the user
groups think they will impact the robots in the future at the Danish hospitals.
Two-way relationship with Actor-Network Theory
Where SCOT focuses on the technology being deﬁned by society, Actor-Network Theory (ANT)
is a sociological theory focusing on society impacting technology and technology impacting so-
ciety (Randall et al. 2007). This means that the relationship between these two is reciprocal. ANT
states that both social and technological development can be understood by looking at the in-
teraction between human agents and non-human agents in a network (Randall et al. 2007). An
interaction can be seen as an isolated event between the agents, but additionally, the interaction
has an impact on a bigger network. In the case of this project, the interaction between human
and robot has an impact on the hospital network. This means that the outcome of the interaction
helps to maintain the network, wherein similar interactions take place regularly. All agents, both
human and non-human, are equal at the beginning of the interaction - called generalized sym-
metry. They are deﬁned by what they do in the speciﬁc context, not by what they are because
they are nothing outside the interaction in the network. In ANT, the internal power relationship
is therefore also determined through the interaction between the agents (Miettinen 1999). ANT
operates with the aim of gaining a common understanding between the agents that the interac-
tion and thereby the network are worth maintaining.
A network is not only deﬁned by humans but also an endless number of non-human agents
since everything can be included in the given network (Law 1992). However, when everything is
seen as an agent, the networks can be of endless sizes. ANT operates with a distinction between
intermediaries and mediators (Randall et al. 2007). Intermediaries are agents that make no dif-
ference in the interaction and mediators are agent that make a diﬀerence in the interaction. In
this project the agents are limited to the mediators. This means every agent that can potentially
be assigned agency and thereby have an active role in the interaction (Burkitt 2016, Van Oost &
Reed 2011). Basically, in this project, these agents are the humans and the robots.
ANT has been criticized for being too theoretical compared to the real world because the theory
states that everything is occurring only through the interaction in the networks, and everything
around the networks only exists because the interaction allows it to exist. However, the idea of
equal agents are interesting compared to theories like SCOT.
58
==PAGE:64==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
The ideas from ANT is in this analysis used to investigate whether the user groups prefer the
relationship with the mobile hospital robots to be a two-way relationship. Here they are willing
and want to see human and robot as equal agents to a certain extent when entering the inter-
action. This is possible to investigate through the user groups’ opinion on whether the network
and thereby the interaction is worth maintaining and, thus, perceived as valuable. And if it is,
ANT can help investigate how the users and the robots are aﬀecting each other.
The user groups’ relationships with the robot
Each interview is in the following read through the theoretical lens of both SCOT and ANT
in order to get the full picture. This means that diﬀerent reasonings are tried understood with
each of the theories to explain if the user groups prefer the relationship with robots to be a one-
way or a two-way relationship. It is searched for if the users are most likely to relate to ideas
from SCOT’s or ANT’s to explain their preferred relationship. All mediators in the interaction
are referred to as agents in the following sections to keep the language of SCOT and ANT similar.
User group blue
When looking at the blue users’ relationship with the robots it is quickly discovered that it is
ambiguous. Pieces of evidence on the blue users preferring both a one-way relationship and a
two-way relationship are found. This implies that it is diﬃcult to decide how the optimal rela-
tionship with the robot should be for this user group. This could explain why they are relying on
rules and norms as found in the thematic categorization in Section 6.5.3, because the rules and
norms might be guidelines when they are in doubt.
One of the main reasons for the blue users to sometimes prefer the relationship with robots
to be a one-way relationship is based on the limited abilities of the robot. This is one of the
ﬁndings from the previous analyses, for example, the earlier quote from P3 about the robot sub-
mitting to a precaution clause because of its limited abilities (P3, Appendix B.7.3, lines 136-138).
As mentioned earlier, it is interesting that the human abilities are seen as the ideal in the interac-
tion, because the robot is then often compared to a human on its abilities. This indicates that the
blue users ﬁnd it unlikely to be aﬀected by the robot because the robot is seen as less intelligent
than humans. This is related to SCOT’s perception of humans aﬀecting the technology, but the
technology does not aﬀect humans.
However, the blue users instigate that the robot is not yet conﬁgured to defer to humans to the
degree they wish:
“
I would act that way [step aside] because I would not know if it [the robot] would hit
me or what it would do. Because it is something unusual.
P1, blue user, Appendix B.7.1, lines 22-23”
This quote indicates that the blue users do not trust the judgment of the robot, and therefore feel
a need to step aside. However, it can be interpreted from the context of the quote in the interview
that this is not favorable for P1, but P1 feels forced to act this way because of the limited abilities
of the robot. This would not be an optimal closure for the blue users. The quote also shows
59
==PAGE:65==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
that the robot is still unusual at the hospitals, which in terms of SCOT indicates that there might
not be a closure yet, meaning that no social group has had the time to stabilize the technology.
Therefore, possibilities of inﬂuencing the conﬁguration of the robot still exist. This could for
instance be that the humans do not have to move in situations like the one in the above quote,
as P1 implies to be the best solution.
A closure that might ﬁt the blue users is related to the internal hospital hierarchy, where the
robot defers to humans because there are many diﬀerent kinds of humans at the hospital with
many diﬀerent needs (e.g. P1, Appendix B.7.1, lines 139-143). User group blue places the robot
in the middle of the hierarchy in order to adjust to patients and personnel (see Figure 6.18). This,
again, indicates that the robot is preferred to have limited or no eﬀect on humans but humans
aﬀect the robot and its way of moving in the hospital hallways. However, since the relatives
are below the robot in the hierarchy, the blue users seem to want the robot to act diﬀerently
depending on what kind of human it meets, which again creates expectations for its intelligence
level.
Overall, the blue users’ preferences for a one-way relationship is connected to the ﬁndings that
user group blue ﬁnds humans more intelligent than the robot, for instance in communication
skills (e.g. P2, Appendix B.7.2, lines 108-109), which leads to situations where the robot must de-
fer to humans. And because of the diﬀerences in intelligence level, the blue users ﬁnd it unlikely
to be aﬀected by the robot in many situations.
On the other hand, the blue users also prefer the relationship to be a two-way relationship in
some cases. Even though the communication skills and the intelligence level of the robot tend
to disappoint the blue users, the blue users are found to respect the function of the robot to a
certain extent (see Section 6.5.3). This can be seen in the following quote:
“
(...) And I think for me – if I should take it a bit further – it has something to do with
respect for the function that the robot has. I mean the robot is not just doing the function
for itself.
P3, blue user, Appendix B.7.3, lines 98-100”
This quote tells two things: Firstly, it can be interpreted as if the robot is sometimes seen as
employed to fulﬁll its function. The robot is hereby respected like an employee. Thinking of
employment of the robot indicates that there might be a mutual adjustment between human and
robot like there is between human-human colleagues. Both human and robot are in this way
assigned agency in order to enter the interaction with equal active roles to solve it. The mutual
agency is one of the main points of ANT, which describes a two-way relationship based on the
agents actively solving the interaction together. Secondly, the above quote indicates that the
robot is a part of a bigger network where it aﬀects other agents in the network. This is also a
sign of both human and robot being assigned agency since all agents adjust to and aﬀect each
other in the big network.
In a two-way relationship, all agents must adjust to each other sometimes. This is acknowledged
in the following quote from user group blue:
60
==PAGE:66==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
“
If you can’t pass and there is no space for you, the robot will not move. (...) I think I
would let it through so I could afterward pass. And then I hope it knows where to go.
P2, blue user, Appendix B.7.2, lines 90-93”
The adjustment is in this quote based on the robot having limited abilities. The situation, P2 is
mentioning, is about a narrowing in the hallway leading to only one of the agents being able to
pass at the same time. One solution could be to demand the robot to stop and in that way make
it defer to humans. However, P2 chooses to let the robot through to solve the situation with a
positive result for both agents. Relating this to intelligence and abilities, it is interesting that the
blue users are open for humans, as the most intelligent agents, to ﬁx the interaction voluntarily
when the robot disappoints to be as intelligent as a human. This indicates that the two-way
relationship can occur when intelligent agents around a less intelligent agent acknowledge the
importance of maintaining the network in order for all to receive a positive result. This main-
tenance happens when the intelligent agent adjusts to the less intelligent agent. This has some
similarities to ANT, where the willingness to maintain the network is evidence of symmetry be-
tween the agents within the network.
Overall, user group blue shows a willingness to enter the interaction with robots. Even if the
human level of intelligence is not fulﬁlled by the robot, the blue users tend to adjust the network
around the robot to handle this because they respect the purpose of the robot and want to main-
tain the network. The fact that user group blue acknowledges that a two-way relationship can
exist between the agents indicates, that they might be open for, and sometimes prefer, interac-
tions with robots as equal agents.
To sum up, pieces of evidence of the blue users preferring both a one-way relationship and a
two-way relationship with the robot are found. This indicates either a doubt in user group blue’s
perception of robots, or that the relationship is so complex that it cannot be seen as only a one-
way or a two-way relationship.
User group red
The red users’ preferences for the relationship with robots has a majority of evidences pointing at
a one-way relationship. The red users have a basic opinion on them determining what the robot
must do, but they are not accepting that the robot can form their behavior, which is similar to
the thoughts from SCOT. The red users are unlikely to ascribe the robot agency and thus prefer
it to stop for humans, which corresponds to their high obligation-to-stop-score. Therefore, their
view on the relationship can primarily be described as a one-way relationship. This can be seen
in the following quote:
“
(...) It [the robot] doesn’t [own its lane], because it is supporting the logistics and it
should be as much in the background as even possible. In the ideal world, the goods
were where they are supposed to when they are supposed to. And then you never saw
those robots.
P4, red user, Appendix B.7.4, lines 62-64”
61
==PAGE:67==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
This is supported by P6:
“
I think it is because we don’t acknowledge or think that the robot should have the right
to be prioritized above me as a person.
P6, Appendix B.7.6, lines 84-85”
Like user group blue, the red users are stating that the robot is still new and many people are
not used to it yet (e.g. P5, Appendix B.7.5, lines 190-191). This indicates, that there is still no
closure, meaning that no social group is yet dominating and deciding the conﬁguration and use
of the robot. However, the red users want the robot to defer to all humans no matter what type
the humans are, which points in a direction of a closure, where the robot always defers to the
human by having no agency and active role in the interaction. The red users seem to have the
strongest opinion on the future closure of all user groups. This is also evident in the red users
having several wishes for what the robot should be able to do and thereby how it should be
adjusted to the user. Combined with the discourse of the robot being a thing and not an active
agent in the interaction, it might be interpreted as the red users sometimes prefer that the robots
were not in the same environment as humans as the above quote from P4 indicates.
However, a modest evidence on the red users imagining a two-way relationship is found. The
red users might be willing to see the robot as more equal if it has more human intelligence. As
mentioned, the human is the ideal for the red users, meaning that if the robot does not act like or
have the abilities of a human, the red users will not assign it agency as they would with a human
agent:
“
Well, that is because we [humans] can communicate with each other and we are free
to decide and think. We can then just move faster if necessary. It [the robot] must not
prevent that the personnel has to pass fast. In that manner, it is only able to do what it
is programmed to do.
P5, red user, Appendix B.7.5, lines 151-153”
This quote states that the robot is forced to adjust because of its limited abilities. The quote also
gives the impression of the red users might be willing to enter interactions with the robot as
an equal agent if it was more intelligently programmed. However, the red users throughout the
interviews seem to be in doubt if it is a possibility that the robot can be more intelligent, which
again is an argument for them preferring the one-way relationship.
To summarize, user group red shows strong opinions on the relationship with robots being a
one-way relationship. This is mainly because of the safety of humans. This corresponds to their
skeptical views found in the other analyses. The optimal relationship for them is the robot de-
ferring to the humans and the robot should not be interrupting or aﬀecting the everyday of the
humans.
User group yellow
The yellow users are mostly preferring the relationship to be a two-way relationship. As found in
62
==PAGE:68==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
the thematic categorization in Section 6.5.3, the yellow users ﬁnd human and robot alike in many
situations. They are overall respecting the robot’s function as an employee at the hospital and
assigning the robot almost as much agency as humans in order for them to solve the interaction
together. It is hereby also acknowledged that an interaction can take place, where the yellow
users sometimes adjust to the robot:
“
I think I would still be careful with such a robot. Not because I am afraid of the robot,
but because I think it has a special function. Like a doctor has a special function. I
shouldn’t be the one to disturb.
P7, yellow user, Appendix B.7.7, lines 59-61”
Here, it is evident that P7 adjusts the behavior to ﬁt the robot because of the importance of the
robot’s tasks.
It is acknowledged by the yellow users that the interactions between human and robot are worth
maintaining, meaning that it is contributing to the overall hospital network, which can be related
to ANT. This is a sign of the interaction being important. This is seen in the following quote:
“
(...) Not because I think robots and humans are equal in that manner, but I can see that
there is a reason for it [the robot] to be there. And I know how urgent hospitals are in
general so I think that the resources will be best spent if it doesn’t stop all the time.
P7, yellow user, Appendix B.7.7, lines 102-105”
However, the yellow users seem to be aware of possible problems with the robot, and they tend
to assign it less power in big and stressing networks (P9, Appendix B.7.9, lines 18-19), which
corresponds to the discourse from Section 6.5.4, where the yellow users showed attention to the
missing abilities of the robot.
Another evidence of a two-way relationship is seen when the yellow users tend to assign the
robot more human-like attributes than the other user groups. This could be for instance “poor
robot” as mentioned earlier. They anthropomorphize the robot even though they are aware of
the diﬀerences between human and robot.
“
It would be nice if the robot is as similar to a human as possible. And I think it is possible
to train it to know what people want.
P8, yellow user, Appendix B.7.8, lines 135-136”
This quote shows that P8 is optimistic about a future, where human and robot interact with each
other based on an almost equal intelligence. It is interesting that the ideal again seems to be a
robot as similar to a human as possible instead of them accepting that a robot is a thing with
its own limitations and possibilities. In combination with the yellow users comparing the robot
to an employee, this indicates, like with the other user groups, that the ideal interaction is the
human-human interaction and that all other interactions should imitate this. This is in contra-
diction to ANT where no benchmark for how the agents should act exists beforehand since this
63
==PAGE:69==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
arises inside the interaction.
The yellow users are aware of the technology still being new, and a closure is not found yet,
like it has been found for the other user groups, as well:
“
I think in the ideal world it would be the robot that should drive. But I just don’t think
that people here, in the beginning, understand the relation with robots.
P8, yellow user, Appendix B.7.8, lines 86-87”
This indicates that no social group has stabilized the way the technology should be yet, result-
ing in people not knowing how to act around the robots and thereby no closure. However, in
contradiction to user group red, the yellow users do not seem to have an answer on who should
determine the closure, since they themselves are willing to adjust in the hospital environment
because they are relatives, as found earlier (see Section 6.5.4). The yellow users value the weak
persons at the hospital highly but also focus on the employees. The yellow users seem to have the
opinion that the robot should act based on what type of user it meets, being patient or employee
(e.g. P7, Appendix B.7.7, lines 130-132). The robot’s diﬀerent reactions might then be based on
people’s aﬃliation to the hospital.
User group yellow can in conclusion be said to have a clear idea of a two-way relationship, where
they are willing to adjust to the robot, too. The yellow users seem to diﬀerentiate between types
of humans having diﬀerent goals based on the aﬃliation to the hospital. With this in mind, they
are overall interested in solving the interaction with results that are favorable for all types of
agents both humans and robot, which requires an acceptance of a two-way relationship with
robots.
Summing up
To conclude, the blue users have an almost equal preferences for both a one-way relationship
and a two-way relationship with the robot, indicating either doubt or complexity. The red users
prefer a one-way relationship where the robot defers to the human with the primary argument
of limited abilities leading to safety issues. The yellow users prefer a two-way relationship with
the robot, where every agent is assigned agency to actively contribute to producing favorable
interactions. This is illustrated in Figure 6.19.
Figure 6.19: The preferable relationships with the robot for the three user groups. Blue: Ambiguous
both one-way and two-way relationship. Red: One-way relationship. Yellow: Two-way relationship.
64
==PAGE:70==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
The analysis can be correlated to the ﬁndings of the obligation-to-stop-score where a score close
to 0 is a sign of preferring an interaction with both human and robot as active agents. A high
positive score indicates the preferences for the robot being passive. Everything in-between indi-
cates ambiguity. Thus, the diﬀerences between the user groups are further highlighted. However,
as in the other analyses, some similarities between the user groups are found for instance the
awareness of no closure yet and the ideal interaction being similar to human-human interac-
tion. The deeper understanding of the relationship between human and robot have created more
knowledge about how the robot is seen - either as a technology that should adjust completely to
humans or as an equal agent in the interaction. These insights will help to create the personas.
6.5.6
Summarizing ﬁndings from the qualitative analysis
To sum up on phase 3 of the analysis, the full description of the ﬁndings for each user group is
built on top of Figure 6.13 that showed the description of the user groups based on the analysis
in phase 2. In Figures 6.20, 6.21, and 6.22 the insights found in the analyses of the interviews are
added for the full overview of each user group:
