6.6
Persona creation
The next step is the persona creation, cf. the persona method described in the beginning of this
chapter. This section has the goal of creating the personas based on what is now known about
the real users from the analysis of data. One main argument to create personas is to make it
easier to understand and communicate the users, and thereby specify the requirements. The
section is structured by ﬁrst assessing and discussing what is learned about the patterns of the
66
==PAGE:72==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
user groups and how this can be used to create a number of personas. Afterward, the personas
are created and described in order to highlight the found patterns. With the ﬁnal personas, the
user requirements can be speciﬁed.
6.6.1
From user patterns to personas
Throughout all the analyses, the patterns have indicated some opposite users on several aspects
and a group of users in-between these. These indications are explained below before discussing
and deciding upon the number of personas to create.
When looking at the obligation-to-stop-score in Section 6.4, it is seen that the blue users with
their average score of 1.49 are in-between the yellow users with an average score of 0.64 and the
red users with an average score of 5.13.
In the qualitative analysis, it is remarkable that the theme found to characterize the blue users
in Section 6.5.3, the rules and norms, has turned out not to be unique for this type of users. All
three user groups are arguing a great amount about rules and norms, especially in relation to
traﬃc. However, the two other user groups show other unique characteristic themes with op-
posing arguments. The red users emphasize comfort for humans in the interaction with robots
with a focus on safety, which is found as a reason for them to prefer the robot to stop and not
interfere with humans. However, the yellow users respect the function of the robot and are will-
ing to adjust themselves to robots in order to keep the hospital ﬂow running.
These two patterns for the red users and the yellow users are conﬁrmed in the discourse analysis
and the hierarchy in Section 6.5.4 even though internal conﬂicting discourses are evident, too. In
the discourse analysis, it is also conﬁrmed that the blue users rely on rules and norms because the
diﬀerences between humans and robots cause them to be in doubt, and a clear guideline to solve
the interactions is then required. The fact that they are in doubt again places them in-between
the other two user groups.
The blue users being in-between the other user groups is clearly highlighted in the theoretical
reading in Section 6.5.5 where the relationship with the robot is investigated. The red users pre-
fer a one-way relationship with the robot deferring to the humans. The opposite of this can be
described as a two-way relationship, where the agents are perceived as equal to a certain extent,
as preferred by the yellow users. The blue users are ambiguous and do not know what they pre-
fer because of either doubt or complexity.
Thus, the ﬁndings from all analyses instigate the possibility of perceiving the users in relation to
each other on several dimensions. This relation places the blue users in-between the red and the
yellow users, which leads to a discussion of building either one, two or three personas inspired
by the user groups.
If only one persona is created, it could be a strong version of the blue users, representing the av-
erage user. An argument for building this can be found in the cluster sizes in Section 6.3, where
user group blue represents the biggest share of users. However, by building only this single per-
sona, the opinions of the more extreme users are removed.
If building two personas, these opinions would instead be highlighted, since the two personas
can be created to represent the opposing patterns of the red users and the yellow users found in
the analyses. These two personas would then capture all users in-between by representing the
67
==PAGE:73==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
most extreme opinions towards robots at each end of the spectrum.
Three personas would be a replication of the user groups but with the internal diversity re-
moved, such that they would appear stronger. However, since the blue users have been found
to be in-between the two other users, it might cause too many redundant requirements between
the personas.
The strongest argument in the above is for building two personas based on the relation between
the user groups, to capture as much diversity between the personas, resulting in all users’ opin-
ions will be represented. Note that the personas are greatly inspired by the interview analyses
since these provide the deepest insights. The internal diversity found in the analyses is removed
to strengthen the patterns. Thus, the two personas are inspired by the red users and the yellow
users but are even more clearly deﬁned to represent the most extreme needs and requirements
for the users for easier communication of the personas. In the following, the personas will be
colored in stronger red and yellow colors to indicate this inspiration. The idea of the two per-
sonas as the endpoints of a scale that encapsulate all the users is visually explained in Figure 6.23
(names and pictograms are explained in the next section):
Figure 6.23: The scale illustrating the two personas as end points surrounding all the real users
based on the analyses.
The two personas are described in the subsequent section, and they will represent opposite user
requirements regarding the improved robot and thereby document the total spectrum of real
users. However, the personas are also expected to have some common user requirements that
will be necessary to take into consideration in the improved robot.
6.6.2
Description of personas
According to Nielsen (2007), it is important that the real users can identify with and relate to the
personas, or at least parts of the personas, which makes it essential to give them positive names
and descriptions to make them attractive. The two chosen names are therefore not opposites, but
instead, they try to emphasize the main ideas of the given persona in a positive tone. Normally,
the names will be proper names, but since gender is not considered in any of the analysis, nouns
are used instead.
The red persona is chosen to be named Warm-heart. This name emphasizes that this persona is
very concerned about the humans, both patients and employees, and do not want to risk their
safety by implementing new technology that might pose a danger. This is embraced in the red
pictogram of a hand holding a heart, illustrating that the well-being of the humans is the most
68
==PAGE:74==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
important to this persona.
Warm-heart is of the opinion that development in terms of both eﬃciency and innovation (cf.
the top layer value from Understand Context of Use) is derived from the well-being of the humans,
but must not be prioritized above humans. Furthermore, Warm-heart has very strong opinions
on the internal hierarchy at the hospital being maintained in order for all to fulﬁll their tasks
and respect the ones higher in the hierarchy, being for instance patients or doctors. Warm-heart
thinks that this is a natural way to eﬃciency. The technology, which is seen as just a tool, is far
below humans in order to support but not interfere with humans. Warm-heart is classiﬁed as
an insider, meaning a person working at the hospital. This can be both as clinical or administra-
tive/logistic personnel, but always with the focus on the humans. Furthermore, Warm-heart is
not young, but more likely middle-aged.
The yellow persona is called Pioneer in order to emphasize that this persona values develop-
ment and innovation highly. For this persona, innovation and eﬃciency are two sides of the
same coin. And without innovative solutions and focus on eﬃciency, the patients cannot be
treated properly, according to Pioneer. This is being illustrated with a pictogram of a person
talking about a light bulb representing new ideas and innovation.
For Pioneer, technology can remedy the personnel at the hospitals, and the personnel must also
adjust and understand the endless possibilities of technology. Technology is seen as an active
agent at the hospitals, and the Pioneer believes that it can be intelligent enough to imitate human-
human interactions. The internal hierarchy at the hospital is less important and possible to com-
promise with. Pioneer emphasizes that all types of agents adjust to each other and help each
other to reach all goals. This means that one’s own needs have to be put on standby if it is not
contributing to the total eﬃciency. Pioneer is prepared to take risks in order to maintain devel-
opment at the hospitals. Pioneer is classiﬁed as an outsider at the hospital, meaning that Pioneer
does not work at the hospital. Furthermore, Pioneer is young of age.
The description of the personas can visually be presented using a persona template (Curedale
2019, Nielsen 2020), that functions as a manageable way of comparing the two personas. Tem-
plates can be seen as visual curriculum vitae for the personas. The templates can be designed
in many diﬀerent ways, depending on what is found appropriate for the given case. However, it
is often useful to at least capture the goals and frustrations of the personas as well as a simple
description of who they are (Curedale 2019, Osterwalder et al. 2014). The following knowledge
is included in the persona template in this project inspired by looking at several suggestions to
templates (JustInMind 2020):
• Name: The persona is given a name to make it immediately clear, who the persona is.
• Biography: A short description presents the most basic of the persona in order to get to
know the persona.
• Weightings: The persona’s opinion is evaluated on a 1-5 scale on a number of attributes
in order to be able to compared it with the other persona.
• Goals: The main goals of the persona being and moving at the hospital are presented to
understand where the robot can contribute.
69
==PAGE:75==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
• Frustrations: The main frustrations that will aﬀect the persona at the hospital are pre-
sented in order to understand what the robot should avoid.
• Quote: A typical quote for the persona aims to provide a diﬀerent angle in who the persona
is. The quote is ﬁctional, with the aim of framing the persona.
The personas’ visual curriculum vitae using the same template are seen in Figure 6.24 and 6.25
for the two personas. Based on these as a communication tool of the clear user patterns, the
requirements can be speciﬁed in the next step of the persona method; Use of the personas.
Figure 6.24: The visual curriculum vitae of Warm-heart using a persona template.
70
==PAGE:76==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.25: The visual curriculum vitae of Pioneer using a persona template.
6.7
Use of the personas: Requirements
With the establishment of the personas, the last phase of the persona method is now entered;
Use of the personas. The personas are used to easier specify the user requirements because of
the strong communicative patterns. Below, the additional need of requirements for users are
discussed. Afterward, the subsequent sections present and discuss user requirements followed
by a suggestion to requirements for users.
Requirements are found in many diﬀerent forms in the scientiﬁc literature (Adams 2002). In
User-Centered Design, it is all about what the users want from the product (Interaction De-
sign Foundation 2020b, Curedale 2019). Another view on the concept of requirements is found
in terms of scripting including both in-scription and de-scription (Akrich & Latour 1994). In-
scription is what the engineer or designer designs the product to do often based on a projected
user, and at the same time what is expected from the user based on this design (Akrich 1994). The
in-scription includes instructions for how the users are expected to use the product. In-scription
can therefore be seen as the design of both technology and user. However, the projected user
often corresponds to a ﬁctional user that the engineer or designer has in mind, often without
necessarily knowing the real users, which might lead to wrong in-scriptions.
De-scription is how the real user chooses to use the product (Akrich 1994). The product is by
Akrich (1994) mentioned as a technical object, being a non-human agent in the interaction. Ac-
cording to this mentioning, the analyses in this project might reveal that the existing robot has
71
==PAGE:77==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
been in-scripted in a way that has not matched the way the humans de-script the robot, since
there are many things that the users are attentive to or not satisﬁed with in the interaction today.
This might have to do with the robots originally being designed for use in production companies
(see Section 3.3). The projected user has been too far from the real users at the hospitals, mean-
ing that the original design of the robot does not correspond to what requirements the real users
have in the hospital domain. This underlines the importance of the in-depth knowledge of the
real users like the bricolage in this chapter has provided.
Thus, it is relevant to re-inscript the robot based on the two personas. The following sections
should therefore not just choose how to design the product to match the users by only consider-
ing what the users require from the robot, as User-Centered Design suggests, but also consider
what is expected from the users in the interaction. The in-scription becomes even more relevant
since it is diﬃcult to implement all the opposing requirements from both personas, thus some
users will have to adjust. Together the following sections can be seen as the full scripting of the
robot and users for an improved interaction (Akrich & Latour 1994).
6.7.1
User requirements
The following presents the total set of user requirements based on the two personas followed by
a discussion of the choices of user requirements to be implemented in the improved robot. All
user requirements are based on what has been learned about the users throughout the analyses
encapsulated in the two personas. It should be noted that at this point in the analysis, there are
no clear answers to what user requirements to include, thus the decisions and arguments are
based on the authors of the project. However, the aim is to still provide intersubjectivity, where
the reader easily follows and understands the arguments.
Presentation of user requirements
With the understanding of how distinctive and opposite the personas are, it has become clear
that it will be diﬃcult to fulﬁll all the user requirements because some requirements might be
contradictory. Moreover, an interesting notion is that most user requirements are abstract to a
certain extent. As mentioned, the user requirements are based on the personas, but still with the
data about the real users as the foundation. It has been diﬃcult for the users in the interviews to
be speciﬁc about the exact requirements for the robot. This might have to do with the complexity
of HRI and the fact that the robots are still new and the users ﬁnd it diﬃcult to imagine yet. How-
ever, the purpose of the personas is not to establish requirements speciﬁc and detailed enough
for the technical implementation. It is the purpose to establish the requirements reﬂecting the
clear opposite patterns found between some of the users. The user requirements will therefore
naturally need interpretation when designing the robot in order to make it speciﬁc enough.
The user requirements are listed below in Figure 6.26 along with the persona that requires the
given requirement. It should be noted, that Warm-heart might prefer the robot to not be in the
same environment as the humans because of safety issues, but this solution is excluded in this
project. All requirements concern what is needed for the robot to be in the same environment
as the humans:
72
==PAGE:78==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 6.26: The ﬁnal user requirements arising from the personas and the knowledge of the users.
Some of the requirements are diﬃcult to implement in the robot. The requirement about Intel-
ligent adaption, where the robot acts diﬀerently depending on the type of human, requires that
the robot’s sensing abilities can handle this. Furthermore, the requirement that the robot Must
not replace humans is diﬃcult to implement in the robot, because this is more an organizational
decision. Regarding the requirement of Communication, it is found in the interviews, that most
users ﬁnd communication with human agents crucial when the human-human interaction be-
comes non-intuitive. Since communication is not available on the same level with the robot,
other ways of solving the diﬃcult interactions with the robot must be found to make these more
intuitive. The requirement of Communication is therefore complex and not possible to directly
implement with the technical focus that this project has. However, by training people in how to
act around and interact with the robot, they can get used to it and in that way, the interaction
will seem intuitive over time without communication. This is also an argument for establishing
the requirements for users.
Choice of user requirements
As implied in the theoretical reading in Section 6.5.5 there is still no closure of the mobile hospital
robots. Therefore, there is no natural indication of which persona should be considered the most
when designing the future robot. Thus, it is now also the designers of the improved robot, being
73
==PAGE:79==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
the authors of this project, that decide what persona to consider the most. This is an interesting
paradox; the closure is now aﬀected by the designers and not only the internal strength between
the personas.
The requirements that are chosen to be implemented are primarily based on the wishes from
Warm-heart. First of all, Warm-heart works at the hospital, which Pioneer does not, thus Warm-
heart is more in contact with the robots. Furthermore, Warm-heart represents the most robot-
skeptical users. It is assumed that if these users are satisﬁed with the improved robot, all users
will be satisﬁed. Even though the users represented by Pioneer have to compromise on the in-
telligence level and amount of robots, they will still be satisﬁed with robots being implemented
at the hospitals with the aim of further improvement in the future. Furthermore, if users repre-
sented by Warm-heart are satisﬁed, it is possible that they will be more willing to accept other
technologies in the future. And if a general willingness is found, it is easier to fulﬁll all layers
of value creation, cf. what is found in Understand Context of Use. Moreover, it has been found
important for all user groups throughout the analyses that vulnerable patients are considered in
the right way since they might not ﬁnd the energy to adjust to the robot. This will be included
in the improved robot since Warm-heart prefers the robot to defer to all types of humans. The
robot is therefore chosen to be very considering with the aim of not disturbing or interrupting
the humans. In combination with the knowledge of value creation from Understand Context of
Use, it has therefore become clear, that the robot must be designed to take this persona into ac-
count to create as much value as possible.
However, the users represented the most by Warm-heart have to compromise on one of the
requirements. When technically implementing that the robot should be Adjusted to humans it is
strongly connected to the robot being able to read the intention of the humans (the requirement
of Ability of reading humans). Warm-heart is against the robot being able to read humans based
on a concern of losing the sense of agency and control. Warm-heart has to compromise on this
concern if the robot should be able to defer to humans. However, Warm-heart will probably ben-
eﬁt from a more intelligent and predictable robot, which is able to ﬁnd the best solution for the
human. This might even result in humans experiencing more sense of agency when interacting
with it because it takes their intentions into consideration.
Based on the above and the requirements of Warm-heart, the user requirements implemented
in the improved robot are the following:
• Predictability
• Sociability
• Safety
• Comfort
• Not disturbing
• Ability of reading humans
• Adjusted to humans
• Reliability
The interpretation of each user requirement is described in the implementation in the next chap-
ter; Design Solutions.
74
==PAGE:80==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
6.7.2
Requirements for users
The speciﬁc requirements for users are presented in this section based on the decision on which
requirements to include in the improved robot. The detailed suggestions to how the organization
of the Danish public hospitals can achieve these requirements for the users are left out of this
project since the focus is on the behavior of the users. However, some ideas as to how the change
can be implemented, are included in the requirements below.
The interviews, especially with the red users, have revealed that the personnel tends to ﬁnd the
robots annoying if they are in the way or acting in another way than expected. As found through
observations at Sjællands Universitetshospital, some employees might hit the emergency button
and remove the robot to the side of the hallway if it is annoying (see Case 6 in Section 5.4). This
treatment of the existing robot highlights the importance to encourage people to be willing to
accept the change. The new robot will be improved and most of the old problems will disappear,
but the old habits of the people can ruin the implementation.
As stated, the improved robot is chosen to be aware of and considerate towards the humans,
meaning that it will to a great extent not depend on humans doing other things than moving in
the hallway. In this regard, the robot does not have to distinguish between what type of human it
meets, because it aims at being very cautious in all situations. However, the humans must still be
aware of how they move and appear since they are in the same environment as the robot. Three
overall messages can be sent to both employees, patients, and relatives about being purposeful,
keeping to the right, and being a team with the robot. These constitute the requirements for the
users. Employees can be trained and educated in how to act. This is not always possible with pa-
tients and relatives. The hospitals might therefore inform these through posters and signs at the
hospital. Figure 6.27 is a suggestion to what could be said on such a poster. The three messages
are elaborated below according to the enumeration in the ﬁgure.
Figure 6.27: Suggestion to how hospitals can learn patients and relatives about what is required
from them when meeting a robot in the hallway. Picture: Dansk Industri (from MiR)
75
==PAGE:81==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
1. Be purposeful
The robot is built for people having a purpose and acting in regard to this purpose. Thus, it has
to be able to predict the movement of the humans at the hospitals. This means that if a person is
just strolling around the hallways without knowing where to go, the robot might ﬁnd it diﬃcult
to predict where the person attempts to go. The robot has been chosen to act this way because
it is assumed that people being at a hospital often have a known goal. All employees have a
purpose, no matter if they are working with patients or going to the break room. Most patients
out of their bed will have a purpose, even if this purpose is walking up and down the hallway to
get some exercise. Here it will still be obvious in which direction the patient is going. Thus, this
requirement for the users does not take much eﬀort to fulﬁll, since most people at hospitals are
already purposeful. However, it might be useful to inform people that if they are in doubt, they
should just stop, like they would do when meeting employees. This can be done by signs when
entering the hospital, that tells people how to act like presented in Figure 6.27.
2. Keep to the right
As it has been seen throughout the analyses of the interviews, all users rely on rules and norms
to some extent. Most of the users are referring to the rules of traﬃc. This can be a way of solving
the interaction in the hallways when it is diﬃcult to communicate with the robot. It is therefore
assumed that all users are willing to act according to the rules of traﬃc, in particular, keeping
to the right. This leads to the robot following the same rule of keeping to the right. One way
to make sure this is done could be by putting arrows or other signs on the ﬂoor to make it clear
to keep to the right. For most people, this will be natural, like found in the interviews, but they
might be confused when it is not possible to get eye contact with the robot. They should know
that the rule still apply in these situations.
3. We are in this together
In this requirement for the users, the use of we refers to both human and robot. This means that
people have to understand that the robot is an agent that will help solve the interaction. A new
perception of the robot that the organization of the Danish hospitals could encourage people to
obtain is to see the robot as a team member. This perception is inspired by literature (Seeber et al.
2019, 2020, Hancock et al. 2011). Every team member contributes to reaching the shared goal of
solving the interaction in the hallways. Due to the skepticism of Warm-heart, this perception
will not be easy to establish, but it has two huge advantages.
Firstly, it allows the humans to still distinguish between human agents and non-human agents, as
Warm-heart prefers, because the agents can have diﬀerent roles and diﬀerent capabilities within
the team. As found in the interviews, people tend to ﬁnd the interactions diﬃcult with the robot,
because they see human-like communication skills as optimal. However, if people are taught to
see the robot based on what it is actually capable of, as Duﬀy (2003) calls the robots’ own race,
and a non-human team member, the interaction might be more smooth. People’s expectations
for the robot will then correspond to its capabilities, resulting in a balance leading to success
(Duﬀy 2003, Hancock et al. 2011). Patients and relatives can also be a part of the team. This way
of perceiving the robot aims at producing a common eﬀort where distributed agency results in
human and robot solving the interaction together.
Secondly, the interpretation that the robot is a team member opens for future innovative tech-
76
==PAGE:82==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
nologies to be easier implemented, because the technology is now an agent that tries to help
humans, not a tool or even an enemy. To obtain this, the humans have to ascribe the robot
agency and let it help solve the interaction instead of it just stopping as Warm-heart prefers.
Since Warm-heart has to compromise on this, it is important that they do not lose the sense of
agency. This can for instance be obtained by making the robot more predictable, as Warm-heart
also wishes.
The above requirements for the users fulﬁll what is needed for the interaction to be improved
in combination with the user requirements. With the knowledge of the users and their require-
ments for the robot, and with the users changing their behavior where needed, the full picture of
creating a better interaction for the humans has now been provided. The next step is therefore
to actually implement this knowledge in an improved robot. This is the content of the next step
in User-Centered Design, corresponding to the next chapter; Design Solutions.
6.8
Reﬂections on Specify User Requirements
Some of the reﬂections made throughout the process of analyzing and understanding the users
are discussed in this section. This includes reﬂections on the chosen persona, the methods and
the data for the analysis.
Firstly, it is very interesting that the persona that has been taken into consideration in the design
of the robot is Warm-heart because this persona is the one that the authors of the project can
relate the least to of the two personas. Before the investigation of personas, it was assumed that
people were either for robots or irrationally against robots. However, with the gained knowledge
of Warm-heart in combination with the knowledge of value creation, it has become clear, that
the robot must be designed to take this persona into account to create as much value as possible
because if the most skeptical users are satisﬁed, all the other users, in this case, are satisﬁed.
One could imagine that a consideration of Pioneer, who would not prefer the robot to defer to all
humans, could have created some unsatisﬁed users. All this shows how important user knowl-
edge is and it is a perfect example of actually gaining useful insights that have changed the ﬁrst
hypothesis of the authors.
It is relevant to reﬂect upon the methods used in this chapter. As Interaction Design Founda-
tion (2020b) and Curedale (2019) do not state how to specify the user requirements other than
including the users, the choices of methods, survey, and interviews are therefore made based on
what was found appropriate in this context of HRI at the Danish hospitals. The persona method,
used as the overall method, is also very case-dependent, as Nielsen (2020) states, and no clear
guidelines of contents therefore exist. This is an advantage in terms of being able to build the
analysis to ﬁt the purpose, but it can be a pitfall that limited indications of whether the analysis
is done right exist. Furthermore, it calls for a bigger need of making the ﬁndings intersubjective,
since they naturally will become more interpretative when a loose method is used.
This is also the case with the bricolage of the interviews. The full bricolage might not be neces-
sary for all User-Centered Design projects, but it is found necessary in this one because of the
complexity of the ﬁeld of HRI. Kvale & Brinkmann (2015) emphasize that the bricolage should
77
==PAGE:83==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
consist of the appropriate sub-analyses depending on the case. The analyses are therefore cho-
sen based on what ﬁt the type and number of interviews based on the aim of investigating both
explicit statements and implicit reasoning. It has been diﬃcult to know in advance whether the
analyses could support each other and at the same time produce new knowledge, or if they would
be redundant. In this regard, some ﬁndings might overlap between the diﬀerent analyses. How-
ever, this is not perceived as bad, since it is the purpose of a triangulated analysis that the same
ﬁndings can be found with diﬀerent methods.
It must also be considered whether both the survey and the interview guide have included the
right questions. The survey could have included more questions on the basics of the respon-
dents such as gender to make the user groups even more distinctive. Furthermore, when the
interviewees were asked to answer the scenarios from the survey again, it became clear that
some of the questions were diﬃcult for them to answer and that the respondents might have
just answered something without any speciﬁc argumentation in the survey. Even though the
survey was tested on three independent persons before distributing it, it could have been tested
more in order to make the questions more clear. However, the diﬃculty of answering some of the
scenarios is also evidence of the complexity of the HRI, thus it has contributed to the ﬁnal results.
The interviews might have been too focused on the scenarios from the survey, which can be
seen in the abstract user requirements. If the interviews had been longer and contained more
speciﬁc questions on the future robot, the requirements might have been less abstract. On the
other hand, abstractness can also be a sign of people ﬁnding it diﬃcult to imagine the possibilities
and challenges of the robot because it is still new. This issue might be diﬃcult to avoid no matter
the length of the interview guide. One possibility could have been to only conduct interviews
with people who actually know the robots today and ﬁnd it easier to imagine the interactions,
typically being employees at the hospitals. However, it was found in the preliminary work, that
people working at hospitals are very busy and are not always answering, which could have re-
sulted in too much wasted time. Other ways of avoiding the abstractness could have been to add
more expert interviews or to specify the interview guide to how well the interviewee knew the
robots beforehand. In this way, it could have been possible to gain diﬀerent types of insights
depending on the knowledge of the interviewees. A pitfall with this method would be that it is
diﬃcult to compare interviews conducted with diﬀerent interview guides.
Another issue about the interviews is the number of interviews. It was chosen to conduct nine
interviews - three interviews for each user group. This was chosen based on the limited time of
the project. It is worth considering whether this was correctly prioritized and if more interviews
would have provided more insight. However, the user groups seem to be suﬃciently distinctive,
thus tendencies have been found for each user group. Three interviews per user group have
therefore served the purpose, but more could have been added for stronger evidences.
78
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 7
Design Solutions
To accommodate the user requirements from the previous chapter, an Artiﬁcial Intelligent (AI)
robot is designed as an improvement of the existing mobile hospital robots. This chapter suggests
and investigates the development of a robot, that is from now on referred to as the AI robot.
Since it is beyond the scope of this project to implement the solution in a real-world setting, a
simulation environment is built as an alternative way of testing the implemented solution. This
chapter begins by describing how the environment is a simpliﬁed version of a hospital, where
human and robot interact while trying to fulﬁll their own goals. The implementation of the
AI robot is afterward described in detail along with a description of a simple Baseline robot.
The Baseline robot serves as a comparison to the AI robot. It is attempted to build it so that it
resembles the current MiR robots as much as possible and follows the observed behavior of the
existing robots (see Understand Context of Use, Section 5.4). The ideas, concepts and algorithms
of the simulation program and AI robot are described at a detailed level, but without going into
code-speciﬁc details. The focus is therefore on what the program does and why. How it does it
in the code is mostly left out of the report and instead, the reader is referred to the code found
at https://github.com/Charlottetheis/AI_mobile_robots.git (see description in Appendix C.1).
7.1
Concepts of the simulation program
The two previous chapters have looked at the context of use and the user requirements. This
work, along with the literature presented in the Background chapter, lays the foundation for
the implemented solution. The overall solution is inspired by the scientiﬁc literature while the
speciﬁc details on how the robot should act around humans are guided by the user groups, per-
sonas and particularly by the ﬁnal user requirements presented earlier. This section presents
the overall concept and ﬂow of the simulation program. The more technical aspects and formal
deﬁnitions are described in the subsequent sections.
The overall approach is model-based and thus falls within the ﬁeld of classic symbolic AI and
planning. This is as opposed to both a programming approach, where behavior is predeﬁned and
a learning approach, such as reinforcement learning, where the desired behavior is learned from
experience (Karpas & Magazzeni 2020). However, a learning approach is included when solving
the sub-problem of predicting the near-future behavior of humans.
The overall approach to the design of the robot is layered. This means that it has two major com-
ponents, a deliberate oﬄine/global planner and a reactive online/local planner as is seen often
in literature. The two planners have many similarities, but both are important in order to reach
79
==PAGE:85==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
the goal eﬀectively while avoiding all obstacles. Furthermore, the robot has a third, bottom layer,
that reacts to surrounding triggers momentarily without having to reﬂect on the reaction. This
layer is responsible for the safety, by triggering the emergency stop.
The ﬂow of the program is shown in Figure 7.1. The program is divided into four components;
a preprocessing component, a simulation environment, a visualization component and the AI
robot with the three-layer architecture. All the components have many elements that are brieﬂy
introduced below the ﬁgure. The simulation component and, in particular, the AI component are
further described in detail throughout the chapter.
Figure 7.1: Flow diagram showing the components of the implemented program. The program
is separated into four main components which contain several underlying functions that control
speciﬁc parts of the program. Square ﬁelds indicate a calculation while circles describe the result
of the action. The yellow square represents the simulation engine which is responsible for receiving
inputs, sending information back and keeping track of the state. The dashed arrows indicate a loop.
80
==PAGE:86==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
The purpose of the preprocessing component is to take a string-formatted map as input and trans-
form it into a graph object, that is used both in the simulation environment and by the robot. An
example of such a map is shown in Appendix C.2. In addition, the program takes information on
the number of human agents and the type of robot (e.g. AI or Baseline). The program can also
simulate the robot types in identical environments allowing for direct performance comparison.
Only the AI version is depicted in Figure 7.1.
The simulation environment is responsible for everything related to the human agents and for
the simulation engine (yellow square in Figure 7.1). The chosen action of each human and of
the robot is given to the engine, which checks for any inconsistencies in the state and updates it
according to the chosen actions. The engine also returns information to the robot that could be
obtained by sensing the surroundings. It is also here that the third reactive layer can invoke the
emergency stop of the robot.
The robot uses the A* search algorithm to ﬁnd the best path to its goals. Prior to simulation
start, the robot computes an oﬄine plan. Once the clock starts in the simulation environment,
the robot sends its actions one at a time to the environment and receives information on the
surroundings throughout the simulation. This information is used to predict the near-future be-
havior of the other agents. If a conﬂict is predicted it will again use the A* algorithm to come up
with a better, more considerate, plan. This is in the literature also referred to as the Sense-Think-
Act loop (Stern et al. 2019) where the ’think’ is the replanning of the robot (Movement prediction,
Predict conﬂict and A* planning-online in Figure 7.1).
Once all goals are reached, the ﬁnal paths taken by all agents serve as input to a visualization
module. This module creates a small animation/video of the already performed simulation. Ex-
amples of these visualizations can be found at: https://drive.google.com/drive/folders/1b5tdh2xN
Jn8G1byvWGM488GIwkcU7kWD?usp=sharing.
7.2
Simulation environment
As mentioned, the implemented solution is evaluated in a simulated environment rather than a
real-world setting. This requires the construction of such an environment. A visualization of
this is shown in Figure 7.2. The domain allows for an endless number of conﬁgurations, but this
particular layout is the one used for evaluating the performance of the AI robot throughout this
project.
81
==PAGE:87==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 7.2: Visual representation of simulation environment. Scratched yellow areas marks the
doors that human can walk in and out. Scratched green areas mark the targets of the robot in
enumerated order. Dark gray square is the robot, white faces are humans at their predeﬁned start
positions. Beds are placed semi-randomly at predeﬁned locations.
7.2.1
Simpliﬁcations
The simulation environment naturally includes many simpliﬁcations of the real world. One im-
portant simpliﬁcation is to have a higher abstraction level than what is demanded in a real-world
setting, where details such as the actuator control need to be addressed. The abstraction level
can be anything from very low-level motion planning, that controls the exact orientation and
velocity of the robot, to high-level task planning. In this project, it is deemed most relevant to
look at something in between; path planning. Path planning has an abstraction level between
task and motion planning and is well-suited to be implemented in a 2D-grid world, which is cho-
sen in this project. Here the world, or simulation environment, is represented as a graph where
only one agent can occupy a vertex (corresponding to a ﬁeld) and it is only possible for agents
to move along the edges of the graph. A 2k-neighbor grid is implemented, with k = 2, meaning
that each vertex is only connected to a maximum of four other vertices (Stern et al. 2019). Thus,
agents can only move in four diﬀerent directions; North, South, East and West. It is therefore a
simpliﬁcation that no agents are able to walk in a diagonal line. A small example of the graph
representation is shown in Figure 7.3.
82
==PAGE:88==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 7.3: Small example graph of how the world is represented. Yellow vertices indicate an en-
trance. Solid lines indicate the edges it is possible to move along. The dashed line indicates one of
the shortest path (having the Manhattan distance) from corner to corner
When crossing a large open space from the south-east corner to the north-west corner, it might
be natural for a human to walk along the shortest, diagonal route in the real world. In the imple-
mented grid world it is seen from Figure 7.3 that the agents cannot take this short-cut. Instead,
the shortest route will have the Manhattan distance (Craw 2010) from corner to corner. The
Manhattan distance is the diﬀerence between the two points in the x-direction added to the dif-
ference in the y-direction. One example of a shortest-path is indicated by a dashed green line. In
this example the diﬀerence is 3 in both directions, yielding a Manhattan distance of 6.
The design choice of implementing the simulation world as a grid also entails a discrete-time
model. This means that in one time step the robot will have to sense the surroundings, decide
upon the desired action and perform this action. Every agent will take exactly one action in one
time step. This simpliﬁcation results in every agent moving with the same velocity and thus
makes it impossible for the robot to slow down, which otherwise could have been a useful tool
to make humans more comfortable in crowded areas (Sgorbissa & Zaccaria 2015). However, if
this feature should be implemented, it could be done by only letting the robot move every other
time step.
The human agents are designed so that they take up one ﬁeld of the grid corresponding to one
vertex, which is the standard size of an agent in the grid world (Stern et al. 2019). However, it
is also possible to have larger agents which the robot is an example of since it takes up 2-by-2
ﬁelds in the grid. This makes it four times the size of a human, which is deemed realistic based
on the observations made at the hospitals both in the preliminary work and the current project.
However, it has also been observed that the robots can vary in size (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020).
In a real hospital setting, many diﬀerent objects and agents would be present in the same area
of the building. In the simulation, only one type of object is implemented; a simple hospital bed
with the rectangular shape of 2-by-3 ﬁelds. The beds are placed semi-randomly to ensure that
every simulation is diﬀerent. In the eye of the robot, it is not important exactly what object is
in the way and therefore only one type is enough to create the relevant situations, where the
83
==PAGE:89==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
robot needs to avoid a static obstacle. However, it might have been interesting to have remov-
able obstacles, that the humans could pull or push around during a simulation, as suggested in
the literature (Sgorbissa & Zaccaria 2015).
When it comes to the human agents there is also only one type implemented. Thus, they all
act according to the same principles and their goals, start positions and start times are assigned
randomly. A human agent only has a single goal, which always is to leave through a door. They
can either enter through a door or be present from the start at predeﬁned locations on the map.
When agents enter and leave in this way, it is in the literature called an intersection model in-
spired by self-driving cars entering and leaving a highway intersection (Stern et al. 2019). The
overall design of human behavior is layered and therefore, in some aspects, similar to the robot.
An oﬄine planning takes place prior to simulation start and online planning is used to resolve
conﬂicts. The technical aspects of the human behavior are described in Appendix C.3.
The simulation environment also contains walls, making it actually resemble a hospital ward.
This also creates realistic interaction due to the narrow hallways that many agents need to nav-
igate in order to reach their goals. Other important elements of a real-world hospital are left out
of the simulation environment even though they were observed to cause considerable problems
for the physical robots. This includes elevators and patient transportation (see cases in Section
5.4).
It is important to note that the simulation environment is built in order to facilitate the testing of
the robot. It is designed to be as simple as possible while maintaining the concepts most crucial
for testing the robot’s abilities in HRI. The human agents are exclusively a part of the simulation
environment and are therefore not designed to be individuals that interact with each other in
any interesting way. It is only the robot’s interaction with them that is of interest. Speciﬁcally,
this means that when solving a conﬂict including two human agents the problem is centralized
and viewed as a multi-body system, that plans for more than one agent simultaneously (Russell
& Norvig 2010). This is not realistic for humans, but it is deemed simpler in the speciﬁc case. In
general, the human agents are not close to exhibiting true human behavior, since that would be
a huge, perhaps impossible, challenge on its own. Instead, simple behavior is prioritized with a
few realistic design choices where relevant.
7.2.2
Domain deﬁnition
In order to solve the planning problems for both the robot and humans, it is necessary to have a
clear deﬁnition of the domain. The domain is deﬁned by the representation of the state and the
actions that can be applied. Moreover, the speciﬁc problems are speciﬁed by an initial state and
a goal state. In order to provide a clear formal deﬁnition, the domain deﬁnition is inspired by the
Planning Domain Deﬁnition Language (Ghallab et al. 1998).
A state is deﬁned by a set of predicates. To fully describe the implemented domain only three
diﬀerent lifted predicates are necessary along with some logical connectives:
• AgentAt(agent, (x, y))
84
==PAGE:90==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
• ObjectAt(object, (x, y))
• Free((x, y))
• Necessary connectives: ¬ (not) and ∧(and)
where (x, y) is a location, agent can be either the robot or a speciﬁc human, object can be a spe-
ciﬁc hospital bed. The AgentAt predicate would in a grounded form specify the presence of a
certain agent at a certain location, ObjectAt is similar and Free indicates that a certain location
is free and it is thus possible to move to that location. The Free predicate is needed since some
locations are unavailable because they contain a wall, object or agent. A state is deﬁned by a set
of these predicates in a grounded form, which means that the variables are replaced by constants.
To relate this to the aforementioned graph representation of the world, it can be said that there
is a predicate for each vertex of the graph. Thus, the set of predicates, that makes up a state, fully
describes the status of each vertex; whether it is free or occupied by an object or an agent.
The actions are very important in the domain deﬁnition. They are named after their direction
that is North, South, East and West. The standstill action is named NoOp (No Operation). Each
action is deﬁned by its preconditions and eﬀects. The preconditions determine in which states
the action is applicable and the eﬀects determine how the state is updated if the action is applied.
The move actions diﬀer depending on the type of agent since the size diﬀers. An example of a
human move action is shown below. A full list of all actions can be found in Appendix C.4.
Move South
Parameters: agent of type human with location (x, y)
Preconditions: Free((x, y + 1))
Eﬀects: ¬ AgentAt(agent, (x, y)) ∧AgentAt(agent, (x, y + 1))
7.2.3
Deﬁnition of conﬂict
Using the same concepts as Stern et al. (2019) the conﬂicts are deﬁned as follows. Here πi and πj
are the plans of agent i and j respectively. πi[x] denotes the location of agent i at time step x as
a set of occupied vertices.
1. Vertex conﬂict: When two agents are trying to perform an action that will take them
to the same ﬁeld. This includes the case where one of them performs the NoOp action.
Formally, this conﬂict occurs iﬀthere is a time step so that πi[x] ⊆πj[x].
2. Following conﬂict: When one agent is trying to move into a ﬁeld already occupied by
another agent that is moving out of the ﬁeld. Formally, this occurs iﬀthere exists a time
step so that πi[x + 1] ⊆πj[x] or πi[x] ⊆πj[x + 1].
Both of these are conﬂicts and the solution is not valid if any of them are present. Therefore, in
these two cases either one of the agents or both need to replan.
7.3
The A* algorithm
The A* algorithm (Russell & Norvig 2010) is a popular choice of state-space search. The algorithm
searches through the state space by exploring the most promising states ﬁrst and thus ﬁnds the
85
==PAGE:91==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
best solution faster than alternatives such as depth-ﬁrst search or a greedy best-ﬁrst search. The
search algorithm determines how promising a state is by calculating its f -cost, that is the sum
of cost and heuristic value.
f(n) = g(n) + h(n)
(7.1)
where n is a node/state in the search, g(n) is the cost of the node and h(n) is the heuristic value.
In this project, both an oﬄine and online version of the algorithm is implemented. The speciﬁcs
are described in the relevant sections. Common to them is that they are implemented as graph
searches, meaning that the algorithm keeps track of already visited states. The algorithm searches
until it ﬁnds a state that matches the desired goal state or alternatively until all states are explored
without ﬁnding a solution. In some cases, the algorithm is also limited by a maximum number
of steps, so that a solution is only found if it can be reached within a certain amount of actions.
The cost of the node referred to in Equation 7.1 is often related to the number of steps already
taken to reach a given state, but it can be determined by many other factors. As described later
it can also be a way to implement social norms and rules. The heuristic value is a guess about
what it will cost to get from the current state to the goal. There are many ways to design a
heuristic function, one way is to use a relaxed version of the original problem. A simple and
classic heuristic in a grid world is the Manhattan distance, as explained earlier. This heuristic is
a relaxation of the problem, since it computes the distance where all walls and obstacles are ne-
glected. This heuristic is used in diﬀerent forms in the A* algorithms implemented in this project.
In Figure 7.4 an example of one iteration of the A* algorithm in the current domain is shown.
The example illustrates a small room with a hospital bed and a human, whose goal is to walk
out of the door (speciﬁcally to enter the ﬁeld (3, 4)). A unit step cost and a Manhattan distance
heuristic are applied. For the initial state, the set of predicates is also shown in order to give the
reader an idea of how the state is transferred to a visual representation.
86
==PAGE:92==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 7.4: One iteration of the A* algorithm run on a small example in the hospital domain. The
initial state is also written formally as a set of predicates. The goal state would be a state containing
the predicate AgentAt(H, (3, 4)) shown by the yellow scratches. H is the human agent, B is a bed
object. Yellow encirclement indicates the frontier, green indicates the state chosen to be expanded
next.
In the initial state, S0, three actions are applicable, yielding three new states. The cost of all three
actions is 1, but their heuristic value diﬀers. The NoOp action yields a state, S3, that is identical
(same set of predicates) to the initial state and therefore this state is discarded. The two other
states, S1 and S2, are added to the frontier. The frontier is the set of states that the algorithm
can choose to expand in the next iteration. Moving south, S1, leads the agent closer to its goal
and yields the smallest heuristic value at 2 and thus also the smallest f -cost. Therefore, this state
is chosen for expansion. Expanding a state is simply to calculate all its possible children (states
that are reachable) just as done for the initial state in this example. These children are also added
to the frontier unless they are states that have already been visited. The algorithm continues in
this manner until it ﬁnds the goal state.
The choice of heuristic function is important in order to guide the search to the goal as fast as
possible. It is also important when it comes to guarantees of optimality of the algorithm. In a
graph search implementation, it is required that the heuristic is both admissible and consistent
in order to provide the optimal solution (Russell & Norvig 2010). Optimal being the solution that
yields the lowest cost. The Manhattan distance is an example of such a heuristic.
Another thing that is important when implementing a robot solution is to look at both the
time and space complexity. The complexity determines the need for computational power and
whether it is feasible in a real-time setting. State-space search has the risk of high complexi-
ties. In general, it depends on two things; the branching factor and the depth of the solution.
87
==PAGE:93==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Both time and space complexity are O(bd) where b is branching factor and d is depth (Russell &
Norvig 2010). Branching factor refers to how many new nodes that appear in the search when
expanding one node (i.e. how many actions are applicable). Depth refers to how many actions
it will take to reach the goal. In a grid world, the graph search complexity will, however, always
be bounded by the size of the grid. This is a much tighter bound, which can be seen from the
intuition that there are multiple paths to the same state. When planning with a single agent, the
state space is upper bounded by the number of vertices in the graph.
7.4
Design of the human-aware AI robot
As mentioned, the design of the robot is layered. It has an oﬄine layer, an online layer and
a bottom layer that ensures safety. All parts are described in detail in this section. Finally, the
learning approach to predicting human actions is presented. Prior to diving into technical details,
it is outlined how the user requirements are used as a basis for the design.
7.4.1
Human-awareness
As explained in Specify User Requirements, the purpose of designing a new robot is to make it
more human-aware and make it take more considerate decisions when planning to solve its own
goals. The design is based upon the user requirements primarily from the persona called Warm-
heart, as found in Section 6.7.1, who values the safety and well-being of humans very highly. It is
attempted to take as many of Warm-heart’s requirements as possible into consideration, as also
discussed in Section 6.7.1. Below is a description of how these requirements are interpreted in
order to be implemented. In the subsequent sections, the technical implementation details are
described. However, one can keep in mind that most of the requirements are met by applying
changes to cost functions or -maps.
To recall, the chosen user requirements are:
• Predictability
• Sociability
• Safety
• Comfort
• Not disturbing
• Ability of reading humans
• Adjusted to humans
• Reliability
The interpretation of each of these requirements in terms of the AI robot is described in the fol-
lowing.
Predictability
Looking at Figure 6.26 from Specify User Requirements, predictability is required by both per-
sonas, indicating that this is a highly important requirement for all users. Two measures are
taken to ensure a higher degree of predictability. One situation that would be very unpredictable
to humans is if the robot turns sharply around a corner and thus surprisingly appears in front
88
==PAGE:94==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
of the human without them having time to prepare. This situation is handled by making sure
the robot prefers moving around a corner with some distance to the wall so that the humans can
see it at a greater distance and predict its behavior. Another measure taken to ensure greater
predictability is to encourage the robot to stay still when waiting for a human to pass by.
Sociability
Sociability is also required by both personas. Thus, the robot is designed to be more sociable, in
terms of rules known from traﬃc, than its predecessor. Speciﬁcally, it tries to follow the social
rule of keeping to the right when navigating in a hallway and when passing other agents, which
throughout the analyses have been seen as important for all users.
Safety
Both personas have been found to require a robot that lives up to high safety standards, which
is also a universal requirement at the hospitals. Since the existing robots and thereby also the
implemented Baseline robot already has this feature, their safety procedure is simply transferred
to the AI robot. The principle is to make a full stop (emergency stop) if the sensors detect some-
thing so close that the robot is about to collide with it.
Comfort
Warm-heart also requires comfort when interacting with the robot as it is seen since comfort is
the greatest theme for Warm-heart in the thematic categorization of the interviews. A way to
elevate the comfort for the humans is to make sure the robot does not move too close to them.
This includes passing by with a distance, not moving right behind them and not moving right in
front of them. The robot plans its path while trying to respect the personal space of the humans
and thus making them more comfortable around the robot.
Not disturbing
The user requirement of not disturbing humans is related to aﬀordance which is a psycholog-
ical term from the 1960s. The term means; what the environment oﬀers the individual. High
aﬀordance is used in the artiﬁcial intelligence literature (Kruse et al. 2013) to denote the areas of
particular importance to human agents. These are areas that the robot has to be careful not to
occupy more than necessary. Thus, the robot is designed to keep away from the high aﬀordance
spaces so that it will disturb the humans less. Speciﬁcally, doorways are determined to be high
aﬀordance spaces, since it is important that humans can walk in and out of any room they want.
Ability of reading humans and adjusted to humans
The persona called Pioneer has a requirement of the robot being able to predict the behavior
of the humans. Warm-heart does not have this requirement but still requires that the robot is
adjusted to the humans. As discussed in Section 6.7.1, users represented by Warm-heart have to
compromise here, since predicting human behavior is necessary for fulﬁlling the requirement of
the robot being adjusted to humans. The predictions are therefore used in a manner so that the
robot tries to avoid the areas it believes the humans want to walk in. Thus, it adjusts its own
path to the desired paths of the humans.
89
==PAGE:95==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Reliability
One of the observations made at the hospitals is that employees sometimes manually stop the
robot to get it out of their way (see Case 6 in Section 5.4). They primarily do this when the robot
is annoyingly blocking their way. As described in the Case 6 this leaves the robot out of operation
for some extended time period. Therefore, it can also be said to aﬀect the operational reliability
of the robot. Taking all the above requirements into consideration has also signiﬁcantly reduced
the number of times the robot blocks a hallway and thus, indirectly improved the reliability.
7.4.2
Oﬀline planning
The oﬄine layer is simple and consists only of planning problems solved by using the A* search
algorithm. The solutions are found prior to the beginning of the simulation. This section is a
description of the planning problems and all relevant parameters used in the search. Since the
domain of the planning problem is already deﬁned, it is primarily the initial state and the goal
state, that needs to be deﬁned here. The only interesting parameters in the search are the cost
and heuristic function. These are described in separate sub-sections.
When the robot is initialized in the hospital simulation environment it is given a start position
and one or more targets to reach on its route. The word target is chosen because it makes it possi-
ble to reserve the word goal to be used in relation to A*. The targets are locations somewhere on
the map. The start position is the docking station of the robot and the robot is designed to always
return to this position. The robot uses the A* algorithm to calculate the shortest path from start to
the ﬁrst target, from the ﬁrst target to the second and so on. Finally, it calculates the shortest path
from the ﬁnal target back to the docking station. Thus, the A* algorithm is used the same amount
of times as the number of targets plus one. If there are three targets, four searches are completed.
The search is always single-agent. Thus, it is only the four move actions related to the robot,
that can be applied. The NoOp action is left out since it is irrelevant in the oﬄine search.
The initial state of the oﬄine search is similar to the set of predicates that describes the initial state
of the simulation environment. However, in the initial state of the search, the robot knows only
the layout of the hospital ward but does not know how obstacles are placed or where any humans
are. Therefore, all AgentAt predicates related to other agents are replaced by Free predicates and
the same goes for all ObjectAt predicates. The goal state is simply the state where there are four
AgentAt predicates mapping the robot to the entire target, called the goal area. Since the NoOp
action is left out of the search, the branching factor is four. The goal might be quite far from
the initial position of the robot, but since the search is bounded by the number of vertices in the
graph, the complexity is not an issue. Furthermore, the oﬄine search is only completed one time
for each target, so the complexity will never be a crucial element.
Oﬀline cost
The cost used in the oﬄine A* search is a combination of a cost function and a cost map. The
cost function is a simple step cost that is 1 for a NoOp action and 1.5 for all Move actions. This is
to make sure that the robot prefers standing still, which is particularly beneﬁcial if it is waiting
90
==PAGE:96==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
for a human to pass. The cost map consists of both a static one and a context-dependent one.
The two cost maps are stacked. The costs of all four ﬁelds that the robot occupies are summed
and added to the result of the cost function to get the total step cost.
The static cost map is responsible for adding costs to ﬁelds that are undesired no matter the
context. This includes standing in doorways or moving sharply around a corner. The context-
dependent cost map applies a cost to move in the left side of the hallway. This needs to be
context-dependent since it depends on the position of the robot. Figure 7.5 shows an example of
the two cost maps stacked. The numbers indicate the additional cost of stepping into the ﬁelds.
The red numbers originate from the static cost map and the blue from the context-dependent
cost map. In this example, it is expensive to move south, because this would imply that the robot
is moving in the left side of the hallway. The cost map and cost function both help to ensure the
user requirements of Not disturbing humans, Predictability and Sociability.
Figure 7.5: Example of a cost map used in oﬄine planning. Numbers indicate additional cost,
where red is a static cost and blue is context dependent. Scratched areas in red are Type 1 corners
and doorways. Scratched areas in green are Type 2 corners and doorways. Scratched areas in blue
are Eastern walls.
The cost map needs to detect corners, doorways and the hallway and does so from the aforemen-
tioned graph representation of the world. Their deﬁnitions are presented below.
• Corners and doorways
– Type 1: Vertex has 4 neighbors and 1 of its diagonal vertices is missing
– Type 2: Vertex has 3 neighbors and all 4 diagonal vertices are present
• Hallway walls. Robot coordinates are {(x, y), (x+1, y), (x, y +1), (x+1, y +1)} in the
graph.
– Northern walls: Vertices (x, y −1) and (x + 1, y −1) are not in graph or they both
have 3 neighbors.
91
==PAGE:97==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
– Southern walls: Vertices (x, y + 2) and (x + 1, y + 2) are not in graph or they both
have 3 neighbors.
– Eastern walls: Vertices (x + 2, y) and (x + 2, y + 1) are not in graph or they both
have 3 neighbors.
– Western walls: Vertices (x −1, y) and (x −1, y + 1) are not in graph or they both
have 3 neighbors.
Note that hallway walls are detected in both the case where the robot is right next to the wall
and when it is close to the wall, but there is one ﬁeld in-between the two.
Oﬀline heuristic
To complete the oﬄine search the robot uses a simple Manhattan distance (Craw 2010). Since the
robot and its goal has the size of 2-by-2 ﬁelds, the deﬁnition is slightly less trivial and therefore
mathematically deﬁned below:
hrobot_off((x1,r, y1,r), (x1,g, y1,g)) = |x1,g −x1,r| + |y1,g −y1,r|
(7.2)
where (x1,r, y1,r) is the coordinates of the top-left ﬁeld the robot is at. (x1,g, y1,g) is the top-left
corner of the goal area, that is the same size as the robot, 2-by-2 ﬁelds.
7.4.3
Online replanning
The robot’s second layer is an online system. This layer has a task-switching design, where the
robot continuously asserts whether it should switch to perform another task than it is currently
performing (Mohseni-Kabir & Veloso 2018). The robot only has two diﬀerent tasks. The ﬁrst
is to simply follow the oﬄine plan. The second is to avoid an obstacle before returning to the
oﬄine path, which is more complicated and requires the need for an A* replanning search. The
task-switching design also entails the need to deﬁne task-switching stimuli (Mohseni-Kabir &
Veloso 2018). In this case, it is chosen to be the predicted nearby presence of a human agent.
Before diving into the details on the A* replanning, it is important to understand how the pre-
dictions of human behavior occur and how they are used. Therefore, the following will describe
these predictions. Afterward, details on the A* search, including initial state, goal state, cost
and heuristic, are described. Finally, two sub-sections present more details on how sensing is
interpreted in the simulation environment and how the emergency stop is handled.
Predicting the future
The robot is designed to take the preferences of the humans into consideration when trying to
obtain its own goals. It is almost impossible to do so if the robot has no notion of the goals or
behavior of the humans. The best way to avoid a human depends greatly on where the human
is heading. Therefore, information on the current location of other agents is not enough. The
robot also needs to predict its desired future behavior.
92
==PAGE:98==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
In every time step, the robot tries to predict the future path that the humans are going to take. It
predicts 15 time steps ahead. The robot uses a neural network for the prediction and the output
is a probability distribution over the possible actions. The neural network is described in Section
7.4.4. If an action is illegal (e.g walking into a wall), this action is always assigned zero proba-
bility. The robot will store the action of the human with the highest probability as the predicted
action and use it to predict the next action and so on. Thus the robot will for every agent know
their most likely future path but does not keep information on other paths, even though their
probability is not necessarily much lower than the one with the highest probability. Whenever a
human agent acts diﬀerently than predicted, the robot notes this and re-predict the agent’s most
probable path.
Since the probabilities are available, the robot does keep track of the distribution a single time
step ahead. This does not require any further computations and is therefore very easy to imple-
ment. However, it could have been interesting to include the probabilities in a more oﬀensive
way, so that a map of probabilities of future locations was calculated instead of simply the most
probable path. This issue is further discussed at the very end of this chapter.
The robot uses the predictions to both detect the task-switching stimuli and afterward in its A*
replanning. During online A* replanning the predictions are not used to alter the set of predicates
deﬁning the state but is only used to alter the cost map. The cost map is altered as described in
a following section and exempliﬁed in Figure 7.10.
The task-switching stimuli can be divided into three types.
1. Predicted nearby presence of human agents within the next four time steps (based solely
on the most probable path).
2. Probability over 10% of a nearby human agent in the next time step.
3. Wrong prediction of any human agent while the robot is following a set of actions that
were the result of a previous replanning.
For both type 1 and 2, nearby is deﬁned as a predicted position of a human agent either in one of
the ﬁelds the robot has planned to be in, a neighboring ﬁeld or a diagonal ﬁeld. Thus, a 3-by-3 area
around the position in which the robot expects to ﬁnd itself. The presence of a task-switching
stimulus indicates that there is a high risk of a conﬂict (see earlier deﬁnition of conﬂicts) and a
very high risk of the robot driving too close to the human and thus making the human uncom-
fortable by violating the personal space.
An example where the second type of stimuli makes a diﬀerence is shown in Figure 7.6.
93
==PAGE:99==
Technical University of Denmark
