Figure 7.6: Example where robot switches task to replanning because of the probability that the
human might get too close even when the most likely action would not yield a need for replanning.
Yellow scratches is the human path. The predicted future path is the opaque yellow and the past
path is more transparent. Gray scratches is the robot path. Black outline is the position of the robot
in the next time step. Blue outline is the area in which we look for another agent when detecting if
there is a task-switching stimuli in the next time step.
In this example, the robot predicts the probability of all ﬁve possible actions for the human. Since
moving north has the highest probability the robot will assume the human takes this action and
the robot predicts 14 additional time steps based on this. However, there is also a high risk that
the human continues west and therefore, the robot also detects a type 2 task-switching stimuli
which initiates a replanning.
The third type of task-switching stimuli is necessary because a wrong prediction can indicate
that the resulting path of a previous replanning is no longer the desired path. An example is
shown in Figure 7.7.
(a) Robot detects a type 1 task-switching stimuli.
(b) Robot detects a type 3 task switching stimuli
Figure 7.7: Yellow circle indicates a human, yellow scratches is its predicted path. Dark gray square
is the robot and gray scratches is the robots intended path. The outlined black squares indicate the
expected position in three time steps.
In this example, the robot ﬁrst detects a type 1 task-switching stimuli, since it predicts that
the human is near-by in three time steps. This would probably result in a new plan, where the
robot stands still six time steps before continuing. However, after only three time steps, the robot
realizes that it made a wrong prediction; the human agent chose to go south instead of continuing
94
==PAGE:100==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
east through the crossroads. At the time of this realization, the robot was in the middle of carrying
out the previous replan. Therefore, the robot will detect a type 3 task-switching stimuli and
initiate a new replanning. This replanning will probably lead to the robot continuing north and,
thus, dropping some of the standstill actions, since they are no longer beneﬁcial for anyone.
A* search for online replanning
With a better understanding of when a replanning is initiated, it is now possible to look into
the A* search for online replanning. The initial state, goal state and the complexity are ﬁrst de-
scribed. Afterward, the cost and heuristic function are described in their own sub-sections.
The search is single-agent and, thus, only includes the actions of the robot. These are the four
robot move actions and the NoOp action.
The initial state of the search is based on the state of the simulation. Only, the predicates related
to other agents than the robot are excluded and replaced by the Free predicate. If they were kept
in the initial state they would end up acting as a static obstacle, since no human actions are ap-
plied throughout the search. Instead, the robot uses its predictions for each human throughout
the search to alter the cost map. Furthermore, the time is added to the representation of the state
in order to allow states in the search where the robot has the same position in diﬀerent time
steps, which for example is the case if the robot stays still instead of moving. This is required
since the A* is implemented as a graph search. Alternatively, it could have been implemented as
a tree search, which would yield a similar end result, that also allows the robot to stand still. A
tree search diﬀers from a graph search by not keeping track of already visited states. Thus, the
time complexity easily explodes in a tree search.
The goal state is a state in which the robot is positioned in an area that is part of the path it is
following. Only areas that are in the robot’s path six or more time steps ahead in the future are
considered goal areas. This is to make sure that the robot has passed by the detected obstacle
before returning to the original path. The entire robot needs to be inside the goal area. Further-
more, the new path must still ensure that the robot fulﬁlls all its original targets, that the oﬄine
search already targeted. One exception to looking six time steps ahead exists. This exception
occurs if the robot is close to reaching one of the original targets. In this case, the number of
time steps ahead is reduced so that the target is included in the goal areas of the search. To have
a general reference, the number of time steps ahead is referred to as the look-ahead parameter.
The online A* search is limited in depth to 25 actions. This is to make sure the time and space
consumption is not too large and because planning very far ahead is not relevant in online re-
planning since the surroundings are increasingly diﬃcult to predict. No obstacles are so large
that it is not possible to move around them and back to the original path in 25 time steps.
Below is a visualization of a replanning example. In Figure 7.8a the robot predicts that the human
will be nearby in four time steps. This is a type 1 task-switching stimuli and thus the robot
changes from just following its plan to deciding on a new plan. The new plan is shown in Figure
7.8b.
95
==PAGE:101==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
(a) Robot detects a task-switching stimuli.
(b) New improved robot path after replanning.
Figure 7.8: Yellow circle indicates a human, yellow scratches is its predicted path. Dark grey square
is the robot and light grey and green scratches is the robots intended path. Green also indicates a
goal area in the replanning search. The outlined black squares indicated the expected position in
four time steps.
The online search is applied on-the-go many times, which makes the complexity of the algorithm
important. The complexity is no longer directly bounded by the number of vertices in the graph.
This is due to the fact that the time is added to the representation of the state. Therefore, it is
important to limit the search complexity in some way. Both the branching factor and the depth
of the solution are important factors. Since the branching factor is ﬁxed at ﬁve, it is natural to
limit the depth. This is the idea behind the robot searching to get back to the path only six time
steps ahead. If this was not an issue, the A* search could just continue all the way to the actual
target.
Limiting the search to 25 time steps also greatly reduces the worst-case scenario. The worst case
is now a situation in which the solution is actually 25 time steps ahead and the environment
is open without any walls to reduce the possible actions. The complexity can be illustrated as
a pyramid, where the x- and y-axis are the coordinates in the graph and the z-axis is a time
dimension. This idea is shown in Figure 7.9.
Figure 7.9: Illustration of how complexity grows with time.
The top square is the initial position of the agent and the squares below are the states found after
expansion. In this way, the pyramid shown in the ﬁgure contains all reachable states within three
time steps. The number of squares is, thus, the upper bound on how many states are expanded
during the search. The number of squares within a pyramid can generally be computed as:
96
==PAGE:102==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Number of squares =
N
X
i=0
(4 ·
i
X
j=1
(j) + 1)
(7.3)
where N is the number of time steps (layers in the pyramid). Even with a depth of 25 time steps,
the upper bound is no more than 11,726. However, the most interesting is to compare this with
the complexity of the tree search. Here, the time complexity is simply the well-known O(bd).
With 25 time steps, this becomes 525, a huge number, and shows how using the tree search can
quickly grow infeasible.
Online cost
The cost function is deﬁned as in the oﬄine search and the same principle of adding all costs is
applied. The cost map used in the online search is a combination of a new cost map and the cost
map presented earlier used in the oﬄine search. The new addition is a cost map that relates to
making the human agents more comfortable. The cost map makes it more expensive for the robot
to plan moving close to the human. Moreover, the cost map is designed so that it is extremely
expensive (an addition of 100) to move into ﬁelds that would cause a conﬂict (see earlier deﬁnition
of conﬂicts). It is also more expensive to walk in front of the human since this is likely to disturb
the human. The cost of passing the human on the human’s left side is also more expensive than
passing on the right side. This is implemented to make the robot more sociable and the concepts
are exempliﬁed in Figure 7.10.
Figure 7.10: Human-dependent cost map. Yellow circle represents a human agent, red numbers
indicate the additional cost for the robot of stepping into a given ﬁeld.
During the online search, this cost map is used around every human agent. Since the true future
locations are naturally not known, the predictions are, as mentioned, used to specify the loca-
tions. Whenever a new state is expanded, a new cost map is calculated. If the search extends
itself above 15 time steps, no predictions are available and it is, thus, impossible to calculate this
online cost map, but the oﬄine cost maps still apply. Because there is no longer an available
online cost map, the search will continue as if the human agents are not present in the time steps
above 15.
In the online search, the ﬁrst time step includes the aforementioned probabilities. The cost map is
97
==PAGE:103==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
used in conjunction with these by multiplying the costs by the ﬁve probabilities and then stack-
ing these ﬁve partial cost maps together. Five partial cost maps are stacked since there are up
to ﬁve possible actions for each agent. If an action has zero probability this action will naturally
not contribute to the ﬁnal cost map.
In Figure 7.11 the situation from Figure 7.6 is reused to illustrate the cost map that the robot
would encounter in the ﬁrst time step of the replanning search. The same probabilities are used
in order to alter the costs. These are the probabilities; 50% (North), 2% (South), 3% (East), 44%
(West), 1% (NoOp).
Figure 7.11: Example of agent-dependent cost map at the ﬁrst time step in an online search, where
probabilities are available. The cost map is visualized in a heatmap style, where darker red cost
indicates higher cost. Rounded costs are also written in numbers on the ﬁeld the given cost belongs
to. The location of the human agent is outlined in yellow, the location of the robot is shown as a gray
square.

cation of the human is (x, y) and the deﬁnition depends on the predicted direction of the human.

Online heuristic
The heuristic of the online search is again inspired by the Manhattan distance. However, in the
online search, there is not just one goal area that the robot needs to enter. As mentioned, it now
just needs to return to the path six or more time steps ahead and on rare occasions less than six.
This number is, as mentioned, called the look-ahead parameter. Therefore, the heuristic takes
the path from the look-ahead time step to the 25th time step ahead and calculates the distance
to all diﬀerent ﬁelds of the path. It returns the minimum of all the Manhattan distances as the
heuristic value.
hrobot_on((x1,r, y1,r), path) =
min
∀(x1,p,y1,p)∈path |x1,p −x1,r| + |y1,p −y1,r|
(7.4)
where (x1,r, y1,r) is the coordinates of the top-left ﬁeld of the robot. (x1,p, y1,p) is the top-left
corner of an area in the path. The path is the set of all future positions of the robot from time
step x+look-ahead to x+25, where x is the current time step.
Sensing the surroundings
An important aspect of a human-aware robot is the ability to understand its surroundings. This is
established by sensing the surroundings. Sensing can be done using diﬀerent sensors or cameras.
The current lidar sensors on the robots are only able to detect an obstacle but not to classify it
(see the interview with MiR product manager, Section 3.3 and Appendix A.1). For human-aware
navigation, it is necessary to also classify the detected obstacles. For the implemented solution
it is only necessary to distinguish between objects and human agents. It is assumed that this is
possible. However, it could improve the solution if the robot was able to distinguish between
diﬀerent types of humans, for example between patients and employees.
Since the implemented solution is tested in a simulation environment and not the real world, the
robot has direct access to all the information it is allowed to use. In the implementation, the robot
receives at every time step information from the simulation environment about the position of
every human agent and every obstacle. It only reacts to the information if it is nearby, but during
the replanning, it will use all the information. This means that the robot has full observability.
Whether the assumption of full observability is realistic depends on the number of cameras and
sensors that is assumed to be mounted at the hospital. If only cameras and sensors are mounted
on the robot itself, this assumption is not realistic. Instead, there should be some calculations
99
==PAGE:105==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
involved to determine the visibility of obstacles, for example, to make sure the robot does not
know that an obstacle is present if it is hiding behind a corner. However, as the product man-
ager at MiR mentions in Section 3.3, they are already developing AI cameras that are mounted
around the hospital, in particular in crowded areas. These cameras are able to classify obstacles.
This would help the robot get full observability of its surroundings and with this in mind, the
assumption is quite realistic.
Emergency stop
The real-world mobile hospital robots always stop if something suddenly appears right in front
of them (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020). This emergency stop makes sure they never collide with
a human and causes an accident. The emergency stop is an instant reaction and thus there is
almost no time from the sensing input is processed to the robot stops. This functionality ensures
safety in the interaction between human and robot.
In order to imitate this behavior in the simulation environment, a third bottom layer is added
to the robot. The third layer is a system that momentarily reacts to sensing input by invoking a
full stop. It only happens when the robot is just about to move into the same ﬁeld as a human.
This system does not follow the pattern of discrete-time that is otherwise a cornerstone in the
design of both the oﬄine and online systems. The third bottom layer violates the discrete-time
model since the reaction happens after the robot has sent its desired action to the simulation
environment. The reaction thus cancels the planned action without the need to reﬂect upon a
new set of actions. Instead, the robot stays still for four time steps before it begins to plan its
way out of the situation, which corresponds to observations of the existing robot (Friis Theisen
& Marott 2020). The third layer works outside the Sense-Think-Act loop that characterizes the
rest of the online planning.
7.4.4
Neural network for prediction
A deep neural network is implemented in order to predict the behavior of humans. Using neural
networks to predict or classify something has grown in popularity over the past years as the
computational power has increased. Training a network usually requires large amounts of data
and thereby also computational power (Goodfellow et al. 2016). The networks are great to ex-
ploit complicated patterns in diﬀerent types of data, for example, images. A deep neural network
consists of an input layer (the data), several hidden layers and an output layer (the result). The
network will transform the data through the diﬀerent layers by applying appropriate weights and
using activation functions to invoke non-linearity. If the task is a classiﬁcation task the output
layer will have as many nodes as there are classes. The result can be converted to a probability
distribution over the classes for the given input if a softmax function is applied to the output.
The most simple type of hidden layer is a fully-connected layer, where a connection between
all units in the previous layer to all units in the current layer exists, as well as connections to
all units in the following layer. Another type of layer is the convolutional layer that is used for
images. A third type is a recurrent layer, which is used for time series data. All three types of
hidden layers are used in the implemented network. The weights are the ones that are learned by
running large amounts of already classiﬁed data through the network. The detailed architecture
100
==PAGE:106==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
and training process are described with the assumption that the reader has a prior understanding
of neural networks in the following sections. Unless otherwise stated the concepts and notions
mentioned arise from Bishop (2009).
The architecture of the implemented network is inspired by the one presented by Rabinowitz
et al. (2018) that they call ToMnet (Theory of Mind network). Their simulation environment is
very similar to the one implemented in this project since their world is also grid-based with ﬁve
possible actions and human agents that have the size of one ﬁeld. The input data to the network
is therefore also treated in the same way as Rabinowitz et al. (2018) treat the input to the ToMnet.
The input data is important since the network will never become any better than the quality of
the input. Therefore, the input must contain all the information that can have importance when
predicting human behavior. This includes information on the context and past actions since the
network exploits spatial-temporal patterns to predict the next action. Below is an illustration of
part of the input data.
Figure 7.12: Illustration of input data to the neural network. Black squares indicate a one in the
data, white indicate a zero. In this example the robot has moved to West, since the fourth action
layer is set to one.
The input to the network is similar to standard image data. Colored images have diﬀerent layers
in order to specify the pixel intensity of each RGB-color. In this case, there are also several lay-
ers, one for depicting walls, one for beds and one for other agents, since these are the only three
things that can appear in the simulation. Instead of pixel intensities, there are ones (the number
1) in the positions where something is present and zeros (the number 0) otherwise. The input
extends itself ﬁve ﬁelds in each direction from the human and is, thus, 11-by-11 ﬁelds.
Since the actions taken in each past time step are important to include in the input, the action
vector is spatialized and afterward concatenated to the rest of the input data. The action vector is
in the format [North, South, East, West, NoOp], where the direction taken, is one, all others are
zero. This means that ﬁve layers are added, each representing one possible action. All elements
in the action layer that represent the true action taken are changed to one. In the four other
101
==PAGE:107==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
action layers, everything is zeros.
The past ﬁve time steps are transformed to this format and serve as the input. Furthermore,
the network also needs information on the current state and therefore the current state is trans-
formed in the same way. Only the wall, bed, and agent layers are present since the action in the
current state is unknown and is the thing the network attempts to predict.
Architecture
As mentioned the network architecture is heavily inspired by Rabinowitz et al. (2018). The net-
work is in fact a combination of two diﬀerent networks, however, they are trained together. The
ﬁrst network is called the Character Net, that takes the past context and actions as input and
outputs a 2D-embedding. The second network, called the Prediction Net, takes the embedding
together with the current state as input and outputs a probability distribution over the possible
predictions (North, South, East, West, NoOp).
Figure 7.13: Architecture of deep neural network for prediction.
The Character Net has a convolutional layer, a convolutional Long Short Term Memory (Con-
vLSTM) layer and a fully connected (FC) layer. The Prediction Net has two convolutional layers
and a fully connected layer.
The convolutional layer is, as mentioned, designed for images or data similar to images. It detects
spatial patterns, which in this case are the patterns in the hospital environment surrounding the
agent. This layer could for example learn to detect doors or hallways. The key element is the
102
==PAGE:108==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
kernel (or ﬁlter) that is applied to the entire image through a convolution in order to obtain a
feature plane (or feature map). It is the weights of the kernel that is learned in the training pro-
cess. The number of kernels is equal to the number of feature planes produced in that layer. A
kernel always has the same depth as the input data. After ended training the diﬀerent kernels
will be able to extract diﬀerent features of the input data that are important for the classiﬁcation
output.
A convolutional Long Short Term Memory layer (Shi et al. 2015) also uses the notion of kernels
but does so in conjunction with a recurrent structure. Any recurrent layer has the purpose of
detecting temporal patterns. In this case, it could be the notion that an agent is more likely to
continue in the same direction. Since it is combined with a convolutional structure, it is even
possible to detect more complicated patterns that involve both time and space. This layer uses
the information from the previous time steps when predicting in the current time step. The
ConvLSTM layer has the recurrent LSTM structure (Hochreiter & Schmidhuber 1997), which is a
special type of recurrent layer designed to detect patterns over both short and long time intervals.
Instead of having simple hidden units it has the more advanced LSTM cells, that have diﬀerent
gates. The gates are trained to adjust which information is allowed in and out of the cell and
which information is passed on to the next time step.
Training
The training of the network is carried out with data from 100 simulations and during training
validated on another data set with 50 simulations. All simulations are completed using the layout
shown in Figure 7.2 with 25 human agents and a number of semi-random beds in each simulation.
The training data is used in batches of 32 data points and it goes through the network twice, hence
two epochs. Cross-entropy is used as the loss function that the network is trained to minimize
and it is done using the Adam optimizer (Kingma & Ba 2015) with a learning rate set to 0.001 and
no regularization since overﬁtting is not detected. The speciﬁcations are summarized in Table
7.1.
Training speciﬁcations
Size of training data set
69,423 (100 simulatons)

Table 7.1: Speciﬁcations of the neural network training.
Figure 7.14 shows how the cross-entropy loss and prediction accuracy evolve over the course of
training. The ﬁgure shows the evolution of both training data and validation data. The training
data is plotted per batch and therefore has a much larger variation. The pattern is the same for
both types of data, the network quickly learns to predict the next action with an accuracy of
103
==PAGE:109==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
around 80%. The accuracy slowly increases all the way to a validation accuracy of 93.6%. The
loss function shows the same behavior but is of course decreasing instead of increasing.
Figure 7.14: Evolution of cross entropy loss and accuracy during training. Shown for both training
data set and validation data set. On the x-axis is the batch number multiplied by the epoch number
to get the correct order.
Performance
The network is trained to predict the next-step action of a human in the given simulation envi-
ronment (shown in Figure 7.2). However, in the simulation, the robot needs to predict more than
just one step ahead. The network can be used for this purpose by using the ﬁrst prediction to
create data that serves as a new input to the network. The network will then make a prediction
of the second action, which is afterward used to create new input data for the network and so on.
This way of using the network to predict further into the future is evaluated to get a more realistic
view of the performance. The performance of the network is also compared to the performance
of a very simple prediction model; linear prediction. Linear prediction simply predicts the next
action to be the same as the previous action. If the human just moved south, the linear prediction
will continue to predict it is moving south in all future time steps.
The linear prediction here serves as a comparison to the neural network, but it is also used a few
times in the implementation. Whenever the robot does not yet have the previous ﬁve actions of
a human agent, it uses linear predictions. When the human has been present in the simulation
for more than ﬁve time steps, the neural network replaces the linear prediction and continues
till the agent leaves the environment.
In Figure 7.15 the performance of the two methods of prediction is shown. Both the accuracy of
exact predictions and an accuracy allowing for some deviation are depicted.
104
==PAGE:110==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Figure 7.15: Accuracy of Neural Network and linear predictions as a function of the future time
steps. The accuracy is calculated both as the exact location and in a more liberal way, where the
position is allowed to vary in a 5-by-5 area around the correct location.
As seen in Figure 7.15 the accuracy decreases as a function of the future time step. Here, the
data comes from a single simulation. The results vary slightly from simulation to simulation but
always follow the same pattern as in Figure 7.15. As expected the accuracy of the ﬁrst time step
is 94% and, thus, similar to the validation accuracy from Figure 7.14. At the fourth time step, it is
decreased to 78.8%. The neural network continues to perform better than the linear prediction,
which has an accuracy of predicting the ﬁrst action of 74.4% and at the fourth time step that is
reduced to 44.4%. At the 15th time step, the linear prediction has 0% accuracy, where the neural
network still has almost 40% accuracy.
The light blue and light yellow lines in the ﬁgure indicate the accuracy, where the predicted lo-
cation of the agent is allowed to deviate from the true location in a 5-by-5 area around the true
location. The neural network still outperforms the linear predictions, but the linear prediction is
in this case actually not too bad. It is probably due to the fact that the map has four entrances,
where the northern and southern are placed almost directly across from each other and the same
is the case for the western and eastern entrances. When allowing for some deviation, it is obvious
that in about one-third of the cases the agent will have a goal that it can reach by continuing in
its initial direction. The neural network is probably not sensitive to this speciﬁc layout and could
be trained to work well on other layouts.
The reason why it is interesting to not only look at the exact accuracy is that the cost map covers
an area around the predicted location and thus the robot will beneﬁt from staying away from the
whole area.
7.5
Baseline robot
A Baseline robot is built based on the observations made in the preliminary work (Friis Theisen
& Marott 2020), cases of challenging interactions (Section 5.4) and the interview with MiR (Ap-
105
==PAGE:111==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
pendix A.1). It is built in order to test if the implementation of AI provides any improvement
to the existing robot. It should be noted, that the robots at the Danish hospitals are of diﬀerent
conﬁgurations based on the buildings and the purpose of the speciﬁc robot. The Baseline robot
is therefore an average robot built on what is learned from the diﬀerent hospitals. Due to lim-
ited time, the Baseline robot is primarily based on Case 2: Humans on the route, Case 5: The
robot has ﬁrst priority in the hallways, and Case 6: Emergency stop when robot is annoying.
With more time, the simulation environment could have had the opportunity of elevators and
forbidden zones and thereby also be covering Case 3: Empty elevator required, Case 4: Elevator
control take-over and Case 1: Forbidden zones on the route.
Overall, the existing robot does not have any social abilities (see Section 3.3 for a summary
of the interview with MiR product manager), which means that it handles humans the same as
objects. The Baseline robot built for the simulation environment has the same lack of social skills.
The simulated Baseline robot has the same layered design as the AI robot. In fact, the third bot-
tom layer is identical. The ﬁrst oﬄine layer is very similar, with the only diﬀerence being that the
baseline uses a simple unit step cost and no cost map. The second online layer is quite diﬀerent.
First of all, there is again no advanced cost function or -map. Secondly, it has no notion of hu-
man behavior and does not attempt to predict anything. However, it does have a task-switching
design. The task-switching stimuli are simpler; it just looks at its own path four time steps ahead
and a stimulus is detected if anything is currently in that area of the path.
In the next chapter, the performance of the AI robot is compared to the performance of the
Baseline robot as part of the Evaluate Against Requirements from User-Centered Design.
7.6
Reﬂections on Design Solutions
In this section, a few reﬂections are made on some of the implemented design choices. These
are reﬂections regarding the probabilities of human behavior, the sometimes arbitrary choices of
costs and the neural network.
As earlier discussed, the prediction of human behavior results in a distribution of probabilities.
The probabilities are only used in the ﬁrst time step of the prediction, but they could be used in
all of the future time steps. Instead of predicting the most probable path, a whole map of prob-
abilities could be computed. A map would be computed for each of the 15 future time steps and
contain a probability in all reachable ﬁelds. The probabilities could then be used in conjunction
with the cost map in a similar way as to how it is currently used in the ﬁrst time step. This would
be a more comprehensive way to view the predictions, and it is possible, but unknown, if it would
yield a better result in the end. However, it requires many computations, since the paths, the ﬁve
previous time steps, are essential when predicting the behavior. The required number of passes
through the neural network depends on the desired number of predicted future time steps and
on the number of past actions used in each prediction. This is in the implementation set to 15
and 5, respectively.
In the ﬁrst ﬁve time steps, the growth of complexity is straightforward exponential; in a worst-
106
==PAGE:112==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
case scenario the ﬁrst prediction takes one pass, the second takes ﬁve and the third takes 25, and
so on. For the time steps above ﬁve, the calculation is based on the number of possible locations
ﬁve-time steps earlier. The pyramid introduced in Figure 7.9 shows how the number of possible
locations grows with time. The number of paths with length ﬁve, that ends at time step 15 is,
therefore, the number of possible positions in time step 10 multiplied by 55. This gives 565,625
passes through the network. Initially, all paths need to be passed through the network, this num-
ber is just above 2 million and in each new time step during simulation 565,625 new paths need
to be passed through the network. The result of the rest of the paths are already computed and
can be reused. It should be noted, that all these, more than half a million paths, need to be passed
through the network for each human agent in each time step. Furthermore, many calculations of
multiplying probabilities are also required to get the accumulated probability maps all the way
from time step 1 to 15. Thus, this is an interesting perspective, but in order for it to be feasible
it should probably be implemented in a more clever way. This could for example be by always
considering the two most probable action instead of all ﬁve.
In this chapter, diﬀerent methods for implementing human-awareness are proposed and exem-
pliﬁed, but it should be noted that many parameters have a more or less arbitrary value. This
is for instance the case for the chosen cost in the cost maps. They have been assessed through
subjective evaluations during the design process and changed accordingly. During this process,
it, for example, became evident that the cost map needed to cover a larger area around the hu-
man agent, than just the neighboring ﬁelds. It was also noticed that the costs needed to be quite
large in order to have the desired eﬀect. However, the values in the cost maps have never been
assessed in a more systematic way, by implementing diﬀerent versions and evaluating the result
through statistics. If the solution were to be used in the real world much of this tuning on diﬀer-
ent parameters should be conducted.
The idea of predicting human behavior is important, also in a real-world setting. The way of
using the predictions is perhaps not far away from something that could be implemented in a
physical robot. However, the neural network used to predict future behavior is speciﬁcally de-
signed to work in the simulated environment. Therefore, it is unrealistic that this neural network
would perform well if used on real-world humans. The good performance is probably due to the
quite simple behavior of the simulated human agents, where true humans would have much
more variation in their behavior. Furthermore, when predicting the behavior of true humans it
would likely be beneﬁcial to include more information, for instance, the gaze and orientation
of the human. However, the idea of using a neural network to detect both spatial and temporal
patterns is still relevant in the real world. It would be possible to transfer real data to a 2D-grid
world layout and train the network on this. The result would deﬁnitely be interesting.
107
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 8
Evaluate Against Requirements
In this chapter, the suggested AI robot presented in the previous chapter is evaluated. The aim is
to assess if it is a better solution than the existing robots at the Danish hospitals (represented by
the Baseline robot) and if the user requirements are actually met. The evaluation is carried out
through simulations (Ross 2013) in the presented simulation environment and is based on bench-
marks covering the user requirements. The chapter begins by presenting the evaluation strategy
along with which benchmarks the evaluation is judged on. Afterward, the actual simulation
results are presented and evaluated through the benchmarks and relevant examples.
8.1
Evaluation strategy and benchmarks
The evaluation of the robot is inspired by what has been learned through literature. According
to Kruse et al. (2013), there are two main evaluation strategies: empirically placing the robot in a
human environment to create situations of interaction, or running automated experiments in a
simulator. The ﬁrst strategy is beyond the scope of this project, and therefore the latter strategy
is used. It requires tangible benchmarks that can be computed without the assessment of real
humans. When simulating the performance, both Stern et al. (2019) and Frank & Riek (2020)
suggest that the solution should be tested against other solutions to evaluate if it is better than
the alternatives, which is also the procedure in this project.
8.1.1
Evaluation strategy
The overall purpose of the evaluation is to assess if the development of the AI robot creates
more value than the current Baseline robot. As discussed in Chapter 5 about Understand Con-
text of Use, the value creation depends on a well-working Human-Robot Interaction. Taking the
user requirements into account when designing the robot is assumed to improve the interaction,
thus it is relevant to assess if the solution does in fact live up to these requirements. Further-
more, Understand Context of Use elaborates on the concept of how creating value at a low layer
will propagate upwards and eventually create value at the top layer that concerns both innova-
tion and eﬃciency. The AI robot is obviously an innovation project, but it is not obvious that it
elevates eﬃciency. Therefore, it is interesting to evaluate if it does improve the overall eﬃciency.
The evaluation of the AI robot takes place in the simulated world presented in Figure 7.2. The
evaluation is based on running the program 250 times and measuring on diﬀerent benchmarks;
these are deﬁned in the next section. All 250 simulations include 25 simulated humans with ran-
dom start positions and goals. Furthermore, a varying number of beds are placed semi-randomly
108
==PAGE:114==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
in the environment. One robot is present that needs to reach three diﬀerent targets before re-
turning to the docking station.
As mentioned, the AI robot is evaluated against other solutions. These are the Baseline robot, as
presented in Section 7.5, and a robot with perfect knowledge on how the human agents are go-
ing to act. The latter is referred to as the Informed robot. Instead of using the neural network for
predicting the future behavior of the human agents, the Informed robot simply has access to the
true plans of all human agents. This is of course impossible and not realistic, but it allows for an
idea of how important accuracy is when taking human behavior into account in the replanning.
In all other aspects, the Informed robot and the AI robot are identical.
The three types of robots are tested in simulations that are initiated in the exact same way. This
means that in one simulation run, the goals and paths of all human agents are identical for all
three robot types. This allows for a direct comparison. It is expected that the AI robot will per-
form better than the Baseline robot but not on the level of the Informed robot.
It should be noted that even though the assessment of the robot by real humans is beyond the
scope of this project, some situations have required that the authors have evaluated the per-
formance of the robot. These situations are too complex to evaluate through simple tangible
measurements. Therefore, a subjective evaluation is required, as well.
8.1.2
Benchmarks
The benchmarks, designed for this project, aim to investigate if value is created. Two benchmarks
are designed to encapsulate most of the user requirements and a third benchmark is designed
to measure the eﬃciency. The benchmarks are listed below and afterward described in detail. It
is both described how they relate to the real world and how they are tangibly measured in the
simulations.
• Social burden
• Comfort
• Eﬃciency
Social burden is inspired by Frank & Riek (2020) and concerns how much the humans are dis-
turbed in their every day by the robot. It measures how often the humans need to adjust their
behavior because the robot acts in a disturbing way, by for instance blocking the hallway. Social
burden is also related to the term sociability (Kruse et al. 2013), because a disturbing behavior is
connected to not following the rules and norms found in the hospital domain. The Social burden
benchmark is related to the user requirements of Sociability, Not disturbing, Ability of reading
humans and Adjusted to humans.
Speciﬁcally, Social burden in the simulations is measured on three parameters: How many times
the human agents have to replan because of the robot, how many times they are forced to use a
rescue plan because of the robot and the human behavior prediction accuracy. The rescue plan
is triggered when the human agent has tried to replan 10 times without success. The rescue
plan, therefore, indicates that the robot has blocked the hallway and made it impossible for the
humans to pass. In this case, the robot applies an enormous social burden to the human agents
at the hospital.
109
==PAGE:115==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Comfort is directly related to the user requirement of the same name. As Kruse et al. (2013)
suggest, it can be tangibly measured by the distance between human and robot. This is in re-
lation to the ﬁndings from the survey (Section 6.2), where the majority of the respondents did
not want the robot to be closer than 0.5 meters, what is called "Personal distance" by Kruse et al.
(2013). Some of the respondents wanted the robot to be at a "Social distance" at 1.2 to 3.6 meters
from them (Kruse et al. 2013).
In this benchmark, it is therefore measured how often the robot is within the personal and so-
cial distance of a human agent The 2D grid world interpretation of this is shown in Figure 8.1.
It is not taken into consideration from which direction the robot approaches the human agent.
Furthermore, the number of times the robot is forced to do an emergency stop is also included in
this benchmark since this happens when the robot is just about to collide with a human agent,
which is assumed to be very uncomfortable for any human.
Figure 8.1: Interpretation of personal distance (P in the ﬁgure) and social distance (S in the ﬁgure)
in relation to the human agent (Yellow circle in the ﬁgure) in the grid world.
Eﬃciency is, as mentioned, related to creating value at a high layer. The eﬃciency is measured
by the human and robot makespan and how many times the robot has to replan. The makespan
is the number of time steps it takes an agent to reach its goal. It might be necessary to discuss
the trade-oﬀbetween human eﬃciency and robot eﬃciency since these are not necessarily cor-
related.
To sum up, the three benchmarks are measured on nine tangible measurements, which in the
following will be compared between the diﬀerent robot types to evaluate the AI robot:
• Social burden:
1. Number of times human agents replan because of the robot
2. Number of times human agents use a rescue plan because of the robot
3. Human behavior prediction accuracy of the robot
• Comfort:
1. Number of times the robot is within personal distance to a human agent
2. Number of times the robot is within social distance to a human agent
3. Number of emergency stops
• Eﬃciency:
1. Robot makespan
2. Average human agent makespan
110
==PAGE:116==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
3. Number of times the robot has to replan
Note, that the user requirements of Reliability and Predictability are more subjective to the indi-
vidual human, which cannot be captured in tangible measurements, thus they are not mentioned
in any of the above benchmarks. Instead, they are included through the authors’ subjective eval-
uations at the end of the next section. Furthermore, as it is explained in Design Solutions it is
assumed that the requirement of Safety is already fulﬁlled by the Baseline robot and the same
concept is directly transferred to the AI robot, thus, this is not included in the evaluation, either.
8.2
Simulation results
This section presents the results of the nine measurements divided into the three benchmarks as
a comparison between the Baseline robot, the Informed robot and the AI robot. A table overview
showing a summary of the results can be found in Appendix D.1. Here, the results are presented
and brieﬂy assessed and, afterward, coherently discussed in the next section. It should be noted
that the primary interest is the comparison between the Baseline and the AI robot. The Informed
robot is an impossible ideal and is only included to illustrate the impact of prediction accuracy.
8.2.1
Social burden
The ﬁrst measurement of Social burden is the human replanning. When introducing the AI robot,
the number of times human agents replan is, on average, reduced by 95.0% compared to the
Baseline robot. When using the Informed robot, the human replanning is reduced by 98.9%
compared to the Baseline robot. These reductions are very high, but it should be noted that it
is partly due to a skewed distribution, where the Baseline robot has some extreme outliers. The
distributions are shown in Figure 8.2. Note that the distribution of the Informed robot is left out
since it is rare that human agents have to replan with this robot. The human agents replan in
16.4% of the simulations with the Informed robot, but in these cases it is almost always only a
single human agent that replans.
Figure 8.2: Boxplot of the distribution for the number of times humans replan for the Baseline robot
and the AI robot. 18 observations above 50 was observed for the Baseline robot, but is left out in the
plot. Outliers (marked as small circles) are deﬁned as more than 1.5 times the inter-quartile-range.
111
==PAGE:117==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
It is seen in Figure 8.2 that the human agents replan less when interacting with the AI robot than
with the Baseline robot. In fact, in 50.8% of all simulations, no human agent needs to replan be-
cause of the AI robot. Compared to the Baseline robot there is always at least one human agent
that needs to replan and usually more. The median for the Baseline robot is 11 and it is observed
to have some extreme cases where the robot causes most human agents to replan several times.
The rescue planning is a more rare incident and it only happens in 0.4% of the simulations for the
AI robot (one observation). The Baseline robot triggers this situation more frequently, in 9.6% of
the simulations. The Informed robot has not been causing any rescue planning.
Looking at the accuracy of the prediction, speciﬁcally related to the user requirement of Ability
of reading humans, the AI robot has an accuracy at 90.5%, whereas the Informed robot has an
accuracy at 99.0%. The reason for it not being 100% is that the human agents also do online re-
planning, resulting in them not always acting as ﬁrst assumed. Accuracy can obviously not be
measured for the Baseline robot since it does not attempt to predict the behavior of the human
agents.
8.2.2
Comfort
As explained, Comfort is measured by how close the robot comes to the human agents. This is
shown in Figure 8.3:
Figure 8.3: Bar chart showing how often the diﬀerent robots are in the personal distance and the
social distance to the humans.
The overall pattern is that the AI robot is much better than the Baseline robot, but not quite as
good as the Informed robot. The Baseline robot does not use the distance during its replanning,
thus it will always choose the shortest path even if it is uncomfortably close to a human agent.
The AI robot and Informed robot use the same cost map and will, thus, be equally willing to
avoid humans. The diﬀerence in performance is probably due to the prediction accuracy.
When summarizing the two types of closeness, the AI robot reduces the number of times it is
close to a human agent by 83.1% compared to the Baseline robot. For the Informed robot, this
reduction is at 89.5%.

22.02.2021
Table 8.1 shows an overview of the use of emergency stops in the simulations. For the Baseline
robot, an emergency stop is invoked in almost every simulation and when it is invoked it happens
more than three times on average in each simulation. The two other robots are more rarely in
a situation that requires an emergency stop. For the AI robot, an emergency stop is invoked in
7.2% of the simulations, for the Informed robot it happens in only 1.6% of the simulations.

Eﬃciency is measured by the diﬀerence between the Baseline robot and the two other robots in

The eﬃciency of the human agents is improved when simulations are run with the AI robot
compared to the Baseline robot. Here the length of the human paths is on average 15.5% shorter.
However, this is partly due to some simulations in which the Baseline robot blocks the hallway
and the humans are forced to take much longer paths, resulting in some outliers. This is evident
when looking at the median, where the improvement is 3.44%. It is remarkable that the improve-
ment in human eﬃciency is almost identical for the Informed robot.
The robot eﬃciency is worsened for both the AI robot and the Informed robot compared to the
Baseline robot. This is probably because the Baseline robot just pushes forward without consid-
eration for other agents. The Informed robot is performing better than the AI robot. This might
be because the AI robot sometimes makes wrong predictions that result in it waiting for a human
agent to pass, which turns out not to be necessary and, instead, a waste of time.
In Figure 8.4 the distribution of the robot replanning is shown as another measurement for Eﬃ-
ciency.

==PAGE:119==

Figure 8.4: Boxplot showing the distribution for robot replanning. 4 observations above 100 was
observed for the Baseline robot, but is left out in the plot. Outliers (marked as small circles) are
deﬁned as more than 1.5 times the inter-quartile-range.
From Figure 8.4 it can be seen that the Baseline robot replans less than the two other robots.
However, it has a few outliers, where it replans many times during a simulation. The other two
robots replan more frequently since they are designed to be more considerate whenever a human
agent is nearby. The AI robot replans more often than the Informed robot. This diﬀerence can
perhaps be explained by the fact that they are both designed to replan every time they make a
wrong prediction, but this happens rarely for the Informed robot. The number of times the robot
replans should be seen in relation to how much time it takes to replan. Therefore, the run time of
the replanning is also measured in the 250 simulations. The average run time is 1.57 seconds for
the AI robot. This is the time it takes to complete the entire online system when a task-switching
stimuli is detected.
8.2.4
Subjective evaluations
In order to understand the behavior of the diﬀerent robot types better, subjective evaluations are
also carried out. The evaluations are based on 10 simulations for each robot type. The simulations
are again initialized identically to allow for direct comparisons. The simulations are recorded as
videos and afterward analyzed. They are referred to as runs and are enumerated from 0 to 9
in both the videos and in the following, such that the reader can ﬁnd the exact situations in the
videos. The videos can all be found at: https://drive.google.com/drive/folders/1b5tdh2xNJn8G1by
vWGM488GIwkcU7kWD?usp=sharing. Below, diﬀerent situation are evaluated.
Scenario (a) and (b) shown in Figure 8.5 are both scenarios involving the Baseline robot. They are
similar to situations observed at the hospitals with the existing robots (Friis Theisen & Marott
2020).
114
==PAGE:120==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
(a) Baseline robot in run 8 time step 30-40.
(b) Baseline robot in run 0 time step 103-145.
Figure 8.5: Situations with the Baseline robot. The solid arrows indicate the directions of the agents.
The dashed line is the past path. Red indicates that the human agent has to replan.
Scenario (a) illustrates a situation where the robot is driving in the left side of the hallway. This
causes troubles since two human agents are approaching the robot in the same side of the hall-
way, thus both of them end up replanning to get around the robot. This situation could easily
have been avoided by making sure the robot drives in the right side of the hallway, which is
included in the AI robot. Scenario (b) is a situation where the robot blocks the hallway. This is
not a severe blockage case and no human agents need to use the rescue planning, but the robot
still ends up not moving for more than 20 time steps. Furthermore, six human agents end up
being delayed because of the blockage. This is deﬁnitely a case in which real humans could have
lost their patience and maybe hit the emergency button to pacify the robot and get it out of the
way. The AI robot is never seen causing any similar situations. The avoidance of such situations
is important since it elevates the reliability, which is a requirement from the users.
In Figure 8.6 the AI robot is compared to the Baseline robot. This is a situation that occurs at the
beginning of the simulation, where the robot enters a room.
(a) AI robot in run 1 time step 0-20.
(b) Baseline robot in run 1 time step 0-12.

directions or future plans of the agents. The dashed line is the past path. Red arrow indicates that
the human agent has to replan. The red outline of the robot indicates that it stays still.
Figure 8.6a shows how the AI robot takes advantage of the space between the wall and the bed.
The robot comes from the hallway and when entering the room the human agent shows up forc-
ing the robot to ﬁnd a way that avoids the conﬂict. Using this space is the easiest way. The
Baseline robot in Figure 8.6b does not realize the risk of a conﬂict. The robot ends up continuing

on its own intended path, but it ends up forcing the human agent to replan. The Baseline robot
also drives very close to the human and it is easy to imagine this situation as much more unpre-
dictable and uncomfortable to the human agent than the situation depicted in Figure 8.6a.
Figure 8.7 shows two scenarios in which the AI robot acts in a considerate way towards human
agents.
(a) AI robot in run 2 time step 100-108.
(b) AI robot in run run 0 time step 115-130.
Figure 8.7: Situations with the AI robot. The solid arrows indicate the directions or future plans of
the agents. The dashed line is the past path. Red arrow indicates that the human agent has to replan.
The red outline of the robot indicates that it stays still.
Figure 8.7a shows a scenario where the robot tries to enter the hallway, but when a human agent
is detected it drives back into the room it came from and stays still waiting for the human to
pass. This behavior is likely to feel quite predictable and comfortable to the human. The second
scenario in Figure 8.7b shows a scenario where the robot wants to head from the north to the
south through a narrow passage. However, human agents are approaching from both directions
and the passage is not free. In order to get out of the way, the robot drives into a room and stays
there until the passage is free. In both cases, the human agents are able to follow their own plans
without being disturbed by the robot. However, the robot spends time waiting which delays it.
8.3
Conclusive discussion of the results
The performance of the AI robot is now evaluated through both tangible measurements and
subjective evaluations. The general conclusion is quite clear; the AI robot is acting in a more
considerate way towards the human agents compared to the Baseline robot.
On the benchmark of Social burden, the AI robot is outperforming the Baseline robot. The AI
robot reduces the number of times the human agents replan. The median is reduced from 11 to
0, which is a big diﬀerence that real humans will appreciate. Thus, the AI robot to some extent
lives up to the user requirements of Not disturbing in combination with Sociability, Ability to
read humans and Adjusted to humans. As illustrated in the subjective evaluations, following the
social norms and rules is one of the reasons why the robot ends up being less disturbing. Another
reason is that the robot reads the human behavior and afterward adjusts to it.
On the benchmark of Comfort, the AI robot is also more impressive than the Baseline robot. It is
clear that the AI robot is aﬀected by the additional costs of moving close to the human agents.
116
==PAGE:122==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
It results in a robot that is much more comfortable for the humans to share environment with,
thus the robot lives up to the user requirement of Comfort.
The two user requirements, Predictability and Reliability, are not captured by the benchmarks,
but from the subjective evaluation there is evidence that these are also improved upon.
The conclusions on the benchmark of Eﬃciency are not as clear. In the benchmark, it is mea-
sured if a direct impact of less replanning can be found on the eﬃciency. The human agents’
eﬃciency is slightly increased, but the robot eﬃciency is decreased. From a human perspective,
this is probably a good trade-oﬀ. However, from the decision-makers’ point of view, it depends
on the cost of having a robot in operation. If the robots are, or perhaps will be in the future,
less expensive resources than human personnel, it is not a bad trade-oﬀfor the decision-makers
either. If the robots are very expensive, it might be too high a cost to pay. However, exploiting
the potential of letting the robot operate during the evening and night, could potentially over-
come some of the challenges of the reduced eﬃciency, which is already in focus at some of the
hospitals (Friis Theisen & Marott 2020).
Furthermore, another important factor is found in how the value, created through a good inter-
action, will propagate upwards to improve the eﬃciency. As stated in Specify User Requirements,
if the employees are positive towards the robot it can improve the overall success of the team.
If they are very negative it can have substantial consequences, such as sabotage. This eﬀect is
impossible to measure through simulations but is important to keep in mind. This aspect is par-
ticularly important since the robot is designed to satisfy the Warm-heart persona. Warm-heart
strongly believes that the comfort and eﬃciency of humans are more important than the eﬃ-
ciency of the robot. Thus, all users, that can relate to the Warm-heart persona, are likely much
happier with the AI robot than the existing robots. The decision-makers should be seeing this as
a huge beneﬁt of implementing the AI version.
Comparing the AI robot to the Informed robot it is surprising that the eﬃciency of the human
agents is almost identical. The Informed robot still outperforms on both social burden and com-
fort. Perhaps this would lead to real humans perceiving the Informed robot even more positively
than the AI robot. This indicates that extremely accurate predictions can potentially indirectly
improve the human eﬃciency related to the robot. However, it seems to be the case that having
extremely accurate predictions of human behavior is not crucial for a good performance.
8.4
Reﬂections on Evaluate Against Requirements
The ﬁrst aspect to reﬂect upon concerns the chosen evaluation strategy being the automated
experiments in a simulator. Normally, the method of User-Centered Design suggests to test the
product with real users. The evaluation strategy is, however, naturally chosen based on Design
Solutions. As stated, an alternative way of evaluating the performance of the robot is to imple-
ment the code in a physical robot and place it in a real human environment. Compared to the
simulated world, experiments in a real human environment could have provided more insights
on how the real humans would act when meeting the AI robot compared to the Baseline robot.
117
==PAGE:123==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
Additionally, it would be possible to understand real humans’ judgment of the improvements
by talking to them. The simulated evaluation does not take the real feelings into consideration,
because the human agents are simple simulated humans that all act the same.
However, the automated experiments in the simulator allow for a larger amount of simulations
since these do not have to be in real-time. In a real-world experiment, it is diﬃcult to decide
if a given event is an isolated case or a common case because it will require many conﬁrming
experiments to decide this. Thus, the advantage of the simulator is that many simulations can
be used to explore general patterns.
Another important aspect to reﬂect upon is the fact that four out of ﬁve considered user require-
ments are captured in the benchmark of Social burden. One could argue that a further division of
Social burden would provide each user requirement a more appropriate amount of importance.
An argument against this is that the user requirements of both Sociability and Adjusted to hu-
mans are quite diﬃcult to directly measure. Thus, it is only feasible to measure the eﬀect of them
based on whether the social burden is reduced. The user requirement Ability of reading humans
is easy to measure through the prediction accuracy, but again, it is the eﬀect of it, measured
through the social burden, that is of primary interest. Thus, it makes sense to merge them into
one benchmark.
Only the user requirement of Comfort has its own benchmark making this user requirement ap-
pear more important than the others. However, comfort has been found to be important for both
the persona Warm-heart and the red users which Warm-heart is inspired from. Comfort was the
most prominent theme for the red users in the thematic categorization and it has been evident
in the discourse and in the preferred one-way relationship, as well, and hence in the creation of
Warm-heart. Thus, it can be justiﬁed that comfort has its own benchmark.
118
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 9
Discussion
Each of the previous four chapters, representing the four phases from User-Centered Design,
have contained reﬂections upon the work in the speciﬁc phases. This chapter contains an overall
discussion of further work and the use of the interdisciplinary approach. The chapter begins
with a discussion on and suggestions to further work based on the iterative feedback loops in
User-Centered Design. Afterward, an assessment of the interdisciplinary approach takes place
to discuss the project seen from a meta-perspective.
9.1
Further work based on the iterative process of User-Centered
Design
As stated in Chapter 4 about the research design, the aim of this project is to complete one itera-
tion of the User-Centered Design including all four phases. As seen in the previous chapter, the
ﬁrst iteration turns out to be successful, since the AI robot is an improvement compared to the
Baseline robot. It is therefore now possible to discuss what future iterations can include in order
to improve the already gained results.
To recap the User-Centered Design phases, Figure 9.1 shows these with the potential feedback
loops marked with the dashed arrows. Below, the content of each of these three loops is discussed
for further improvement of the mobile robots at the Danish hospitals.
Figure 9.1: User-Centered Design phases with possible feedback loops.
The loops can be seen as three alternative ways to gain further improvement, depending on how
far back in the process of User-Centered Design one wants to go. Thus, it is not necessary to
119
==PAGE:125==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
follow all three loops.
Loop 1: Understand Context of Use
Currently, Understand Context of Use is used to investigate why the robots are implemented and
how they constitute a change. This also includes how the robots are meant to create value. How-
ever, this all concerns the Danish hospitals seen as one unit. The cases of challenging interactions
today, presented as a part of the context, are also not very speciﬁc to each of the hospitals, be-
cause they serve as more conceptual challenges.
If going back to Understand Context of Use, it will be relevant to dive into each hospital to specify
the context even more. Two ideas arise in this regard:
1. One idea is to investigate the middle layer value further to understand what organizational
requirements each of the Danish hospitals have. This might be done by interviewing the
administration at some of the hospitals to gain insights in their speciﬁc requirements. By
knowing more about the organizations of the individual Danish hospitals, it will be possible
to provide more organizational suggestions to the requirements for users in the end of
Specify User Requirements. Furthermore, the knowledge can then be taken into account as
requirements to the robot in Design Solutions. Maybe it will be possible to develop one robot
to ﬁt all these requirements, or maybe it will turn out that the hospitals are so distinctive
that one robot cannot ﬁt all hospitals.
2. Another idea is to understand the exact situations where the interactions are diﬃcult to-
day more in depth. This can be done by more speciﬁc observations. Based on these, exact
illustrations of the situations of challenging interactions can be developed. The illustra-
tions can then be used in the subsequent phases. In Specify User Requirements they can
be included directly in the survey and the interviews to get peoples’ opinion on situations
seen directly at the diﬀerent hospitals. In Design Solutions, the illustrations can serve as the
layout used in the simulation environment to capture a more realistic test setting reﬂecting
real-life situations.
Loop 2: Specify User Requirements
After knowing that improvements are obtainable with the AI robot, it is relevant to further un-
derstand if the requirements, deﬁned on knowledge of the users and interpreted by the authors
of this project, really match the real users. Therefore, if going back to Specify User Requirements,
it will be interesting to establish an advisory group of users that will continuously guide to obtain
even more learnings about the users. This group has to be diverse and contain representatives
from each of the three detected user groups. The advisory group can be established by contacting
some of the respondents of the survey. Alternatively, it can be established by letting new people
answer the survey and then use the knowledge from the clustering to place them in one of the
three user groups. The advisory group has to be of suﬃcient size to be representative of the real
users.
The aim of the advisory group of users is to conﬁrm both the personas, the user requirements
and the interpretation of the user requirements. This will require new interviews. The conﬁr-
mation of the personas can be based on how easy it is for the advisory users to identify with the
personas or parts of the personas, or alternatively place themselves in-between the personas. It
120
==PAGE:126==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
will be a question of how realistic the personas are. The user requirements and the interpretation
of them can be conﬁrmed by the advisory users based on how much the users, identifying the
most with one of the two personas, agree on the respective requirements. It also concerns how
much they actually understand the requirements and ﬁnd them relevant. All of this can provide
better knowledge of how the robot has to be developed in Design Solutions because it will be
more speciﬁc and leave less to be interpreted by the designers of the robot.
Loop 3: Design Solutions
If going back to Design Solutions three further developments of the AI robot can be done depend-
ing on what one ﬁnds the most appropriate:
1. As User-Centered Design suggests, the best way to evaluate the product is to involve the
real users. Thus, the code of the robot can be modiﬁed to be implemented in a physical
robot that is placed in a real-life environment where the robot’s performance in the inter-
actions can be tested. This is a diﬃcult and time-consuming task, however, it is essential
to do.
2. Another way to improve the AI robot is to implement diﬀerent types of human agents in
the simulation world. This is in order to get a more realistic picture of how considerate the
robot is towards diﬀerent types of human agents. Since this is a requirement, it is relevant
to test if the robot is an improvement for people acting diﬀerently than the single type
already implemented. Other simpliﬁcations of the simulation environment could also be
made more realistic.
3. It is also possible to focus on other technical aspects such as the communication skills of
the robot. This might lead to the robot being able to fulﬁll some of the user requirements
that are currently left out. Communication can be many things, for instance, imitation
of human language, or human body signs that the robot has to interpret. Alternatively,
communication can be captured by installing lights or sounds in the robot indicating its
intentions. This is already seen to a certain extent at the hospitals today.
These diﬀerent ways of working with the iterative process show that there are interesting possi-
bilities for further work in each of the three loops. The suggestions above might not be exhaustive
but highlight some of the immediate ideas based on what has occurred throughout the phases of
the ﬁrst iteration and the ﬁnal results. It is now up to the designers of the further improvement
to decide where to start and if several iterations are relevant.
9.2
Assessment of the interdisciplinary approach
This section discusses the advantages and pitfalls of both the interdisciplinary research as the
concept of the project and User-Centered Design as the overall framework.
As argued at the beginning of the project, interdisciplinary research is essential for HRI, since
both human and robot must be taken into consideration. By improving the HRI, the ﬁt between
human and robot is aimed strengthened, with references to Value Proposition as presented at the
beginning of the project. This is done by including diﬀerent disciplines. Additionally, the project
121
==PAGE:127==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
is centered around the distinction of the authors coming from two diﬀerent areas of engineer-
ing, namely Business Analytics focused on management and Artiﬁcial Intelligence. It is, in that
regard, natural that the project embraces an interdisciplinary approach in order to combine the
knowledge from both engineering areas. Thus, overall the idea of the interdisciplinary approach
and the use of User-Centered Design ﬁts the project well.
The pitfall of the project being interdisciplinary is that all parts of the project might end up
being more superﬁcial than if the project only included the area of either management or AI.
Since all parts must be coherent in an interdisciplinary project, the option of further deep-diving
into one of the areas is not possible, which might otherwise be obvious for each of the engi-
neering disciplines. However, as argued above, the interdisciplinary approach ﬁts HRI, thus a
further deep-dive might not serve the project well because it would be too focused on either the
human-centered HRI or the robot-centered HRI. The interdisciplinary research causes the project
to always be realistic and case-centered to ﬁt the Danish public hospitals. This is due to the fact
that both the human perspective and the robot perspective are kept in mind in all analyses; the
user requirements aim at being realistic to implement in the robot to a great extent, and the de-
sign of the robot aims at implementing the user requirements. This is ensured through the use
of User-Centered Design and the four phases.
One of the pitfalls of User-Centered Design as a framework is that the four phases are sequential,
which has been challenging because this project naturally is more parallel because of the time
constraint. Another pitfall is that the process is very case-dependent. Every case is unique with
diﬀerent kinds of users and a diﬀerent product in focus. Thus, it is diﬃcult to search for guide-
lines in the literature on which direction to take the project. Each of the four phases is therefore
based on what the authors ﬁnd appropriate in the case of HRI at the Danish hospitals. However,
this liberal approach is also an advantage. It provides room for creativity in order to explore the
best ﬁt for this exact project. Furthermore, it is interesting for authors to explore new areas.
In conclusion, the advantages of both interdisciplinarity and User-Centered Design are stronger
than the pitfalls for this exact project. Other approaches to the project might also have led to suc-
cess. However, it can be concluded, that both the interdisciplinarity and User-Centered Design
serve as intended and ensures a coherent work including both human and robot in the improve-
ment of the mobile robots at the Danish hospitals. The interdisciplinarity serves as a fascinating
challenge on a methodological level.
122
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
This chapter concludes on the overall research question presented at the beginning of this project
(see Section 2.3). To answer the research question, the six associated sub-questions are answered
below. The conclusion is based on a single iteration of the four phases of User-Centered Design.
The original research question is:
How can the challenges, that arise today in the interaction between humans and mobile robots at
Danish hospitals, be interpreted and improved from both a human perspective and by using Artiﬁ-
cial Intelligence to create value?
Implementing mobile robots at the Danish hospitals can be seen as a change that has a great im-
pact on all humans at the hospitals. The change is determined to create value at multiple layers.
At the bottom layer, the value is created through a good Human-Robot Interaction. This value
is expected to propagate upwards to the top layer, where the focus is on eﬃciency and innovation.
Through observations at the hospitals, it is found that there are many cases where interac-
tions between humans and robots are challenging today. Speciﬁcally, the existing robots are
not human-aware and socially naive. The existing robot, for instance, tends to block the hallway
and is not aware of traﬃc rules, such as driving in the right side. These challenges are important
in order to understand and model the existing robots so that they can be improved upon.
The ﬁrst step of improving the interaction is to gain an understanding of the humans involved.
Through quantitative survey data, it has been possible to identify three diﬀerent user groups.
Based on qualitative interview data with users from each of these groups, in-depth knowledge
of the user groups is gained resulting in an understanding of the diﬀerent user patterns. The de-
tected user patterns allow for the creation of two personas with opposite tendencies towards the
robots; Warm-heart and Pioneer. Warm-heart is skeptical and values the well-being and safety
of humans very highly. According to Warm-heart, robots can pose a danger at the hospitals. Pio-
neer is positive towards robots and ﬁnds that robots can remedy the personnel and create a more
eﬃcient and innovative workday at the hospitals. The improvement of the robot in this project is
built upon Warm-heart since it is found important that all skeptical users are considered in order
to positively aﬀect their opinion to create as much value as possible at the hospitals. Thus, the
user requirements that are taken into consideration are Predictability, Sociability, Safety, Comfort,
Not disturbing, Ability of reading humans, Adjusted to humans and Reliability. These are inter-
preted and implemented in the improved robot.
123
==PAGE:129==
Technical University of Denmark
22.02.2021
The in-depth understanding of the users has also resulted in insight into how the users can
change their behavior to ﬁt the improved robot. It is obvious that the improved robot cannot
satisfy all users, since the two personas provide evidence on opposite patterns. Furthermore,
since the requirements are based on the personas, representing user patterns but not being real
users, the real users must learn how to behave around the robot for smooth interactions. The
requirements for users are, however, quite simple because of the considerate improved robot.
It is required that the users are purposeful when moving at the hospitals, that they keep to the
right, and that they see the robot as a team member helping to solve the interaction. The latter is
the most diﬃcult to implement since it might require a new perception of the robot as an active
agent and not just a tool for some users.
The next step of the improvement is to use the user requirements as a basis for the design of an
AI robot as an improvement of the existing robots. The AI robot has a layered design with both
an oﬄine system and an online system. The online system detects obstacles, including human
agents, and replans a way around them. In both systems, the A* algorithm is used to ﬁnd the
best paths. Cost maps are developed to make the robot more human-aware and they are used
in the A* search. An important element in the AI robot is its ability to predict the behavior of
human agents. This is implemented through a deep neural network. The network has both con-
volutional layers and a ConvLSTM layer, that in combination detects spatio-temporal patterns.
The validation accuracy of the next step prediction is 93.6%.
The improvements implemented in the AI robot are demonstrated and evaluated through simula-
tions. The evaluation is made on benchmarks encapsulating both the user requirements and the
top layer value of eﬃciency through tangible measurements. Furthermore, subjective evaluations
are made by the authors in cases that are diﬃcult to measure. The AI robot is evaluated against
the Baseline robot and a robot with perfect knowledge of the human agents, the Informed robot.
The evaluation clearly shows that the AI robot outperforms the Baseline robot on both Social
burden and Comfort. However, there is a trade-oﬀon the Eﬃciency benchmark. The eﬃciency
of the human agents is elevated when interacting with the AI robot compared to the Baseline
robot but the eﬃciency of the robot itself is decreased. However, overall the improvements are
evaluated to satisfy the users represented by Warm-heart more than the existing robots do today.
Thus, the AI robot can be seen as a success.
To conclude, some of the challenges, that arise today in the interaction between humans and
mobile hospital robots at the Danish hospitals, can be overcome through new innovations. It
is found possible to include the user knowledge and requirements through AI in an improved
robot, that meets some of the challenges of today. The suggested AI robot is shown to create
value at both a bottom layer and a top layer at the hospitals. Thus, both the human perspective
and the robot perspective, including Artiﬁcial Intelligence, are concluded to be very important
for the improvement of the existing mobile robots at the Danish hospitals.
